The Red Page
We Also Serve...
It cannot be denied that the poets and
writers of thd Commonwealth have, in the
present world catastrophe, a necessary and
highly important function to serve. While
the patriotic manhood of the continent is
steadily enlisting and going to the Front in
thousands, upon us, who stay at home, there
has devolved the duty of stimulating that
patriotism by our little patriotic verses and
our exhortations in as patriotic prose. A
few of us have, doubtless, given way to an
inexplicable conviction that their presence
is necessary at the Front if the Empire is
to be saved from extinction; but happily,
the great majority of us who write have
remembered in time that the pen is mightier
than the sword —even against the Kaiser’s
millions.
While we agree, in general terms, that
Australia must send every possible soldier
to reinforce the armies of the Allies, there
are cogent reasons why the bards, the
critics, the freelancers and the writers of
topical verse and patriotic songs should
sturdily stick to their job at home. In this
terrible crisis it is encouraging to find that
the majority of our poets have kept their
heads. Obviously it is better to keep our
heads than to run the risk of having them
blown off by shrapnel; but it would be a
grave reflection upon the patriotism of you
and me to infer that that considera-
tion had the least weight with us. There are
many reasons why the other bloke should
enlist. He is only a clerk, whose place can
be taken by any other clerk; only the owner
of an out-back station, that any other person
could manage as well; only a laborer, whose
pay-sheet can be as easily filled in to another
name; only a doctor or a nurse, whose work
can be just as efficiently carried on by some
other doctor or nurse; only a politician, or
navvy, or bank-manager, or farm-hand, or
lawyer, whose death would make little dif-
ference to this favored and peaceful com-
munity.
With us, who write verses, and stay at
home, there are, of course, a thousand valid
reasons for our apparent lack of patriotism.
It is not cowardice nor disloyalty. No body
of citizens has shown themselves more
patriotic or brave—in their verse's. And
many of them have recognised their duty to
their country in this awful hour of their
country’s need by deliberately, and at much
personal sacrifice of their valuable time,
joining a rifle club. In this loyal act they
have shown heroism equal to that of the
business men comprising the Millions Club.
No business appointment can keep this brave
body of Sydney citizens from giving patri-
otic luncheons to themselves, at which
speeches of the most fervent loyalty are
almost a commonplace. Nay, the sound of
the Millions Club cheering themselves can
be heard by all the other diners at their
palatial eating-house. And they have been
foremost, too, in collecting subscriptions
from their fellow-citizens, even though those
fellow-citizens cannot afford to lunch in the
same building.
So the poets and writers of this continent,
including, let us hope, you and me, have
joined their rifle club, and devote one even-
ing a week 'to defending Australia from
assault. So strong is their patriotism that
there is even talk of one rifle club putting
themselves, at their own expense, into uni-
form. This ip not only a sacrifice in the
cause of the conquered and starving Bel-
gians, but a sacrifice of their own respect.
For in these days a uniform —any uniform
—is honored ; and the member of a rifle club
must have a considerable sense of patriotism
before he goes forth in his rifle-club uniform.
He might be actually mistaken for a non-
literary private who is eagerly waiting for
his transport to sail. Prosperous, but patri-
otic citizens, passing him in the street on
his way to his parade, may say unctuously to
themselves: “Ha! Another of our brave,
devoted fellows, food for powder, bless him!”
Yet this contingency the rifle-clubman is
ready, nay, eager, to meet.
&
No; you and I cannot be accounted want-
ing in a proper sense of our responsibility
in this grave crisis. “They also serve who
only stand and wait.” We cannot be
spared; our duties are too responsible.
Doubtless our wives and children could
spare us—at least, other wives and children
are doing it every day; but it is inconceiv-
able that our country could spare us. In-
conceivable —to us. We serve in the noble
army of literature, and our place is with
the staff, not at the Front. Being poets and
writers, no doubt our actual participation
in the war of the trenches would vivify and
stimulate bur art, as no peaceful staying at
home could do it. No doubt we should
return from the war finer men and there-
fore finer poets. And what subjects for
poetry we might find on the battlefield!
We should, haply, lose some of our literary
affectations by our presence in the firing
lines of death; and our life and death part-
nership with mere unpoetic blokes might
rub some of the pardonable conceit out of
us; but these are considerations to which
we wisely pay no heed. Our sacred task is
here —to spur the other fellow to enlist.
We do not write long letters to the press
exhorting the other, or mere, bloke to fight
for his country and us ; for editors do not
pay space rates for correspondence. Our
function is more artistic. We write the
poems of the war, the exhortations, in neat
verse, to the young manhood of this con-
tinent to wake and rise up and smite the
Kaiser; we write the satirical verses for the
benefit of the other bloke who won’t enlist;
we call the Kaiser and the German names
in scathing metre, and we celebrate the vic-
tories of the other bloke in noble measures.
And if we were not here —all of us —to do
it, who would? And if we were all killed,
what would become of Australian Literature,
what, indeed, would become of this priceless
page? No, hard though it be to refrain, it
is to the everlasting honor of our noble
craft that we refuse to take our places in
the ranks. And if we are paid for our
patriotic verse, let us console ourselves with
the reflection that there can never be ad-
equate remuneration for any piece of real
literature.
&
When the war is over further sacrifices
will be demanded of us. And we shall, one
does not hesitate to say, face them bravely.
For in those days the young man who has
to explain why he didn’t go to the war will
have a rather unhappy existence, and not
to wear a scar will almost be a disgrace.
The women of our race are quick to stigma-
tise as cowardice what may be, after all,
only a devotion to literature and it may be
the fate of you and me to be despised by
the whole womanhood of Australia. But
considerations like these will, one feels sure,
carry no weight with us true poets.
Remember the saying which we may par-
donably adapt: “I care not who fights the
battles of my country, so long as I can
make its songs.” That is the proper atti-
tude, even if we poets may have to learn
German in order to write our country’s
songs.
Business as usual.
The Red Laugh
“ ‘Are you afraid?’ I repeated kindly. His
lips twitched, trying to frame a word, and
the same instant there happened something
incomprehensible, monstrous and super-
natural. I felt a draught of warm air upon
my right cheek that made me sway—that
is all —while before my eyes,, in place of the
white face, there was something short, blunt
and red, and out of it the blood was gushing
as out of an uncorked bottle. And that
short red and flowing ‘something’ still
seemed to be smiling a sort of smile, a tooth-
less laugh—a red laugh.”
This incident of a shell decapitating an
officer on the battlefield, so brutally
described, gives the title and the realistic
method of Leonidas Andreief’s The Red
Laugh (Unwin,' Is.). In Russia Andreief’s
popularity almost transcends that of Gorki;
he, too, has “invented a new thrill.” And
though in The Red Laugh the background is
the Manchurian war, the work is horribly
applicable to the present trouble. Its first
words, “Horror and madness” form the key
to these “fragments of a discovered manu-
The book is a ghastly record of
mutilations, but its treatment is profoundly
psychological, and the mutilations of the
bodies of the soldiers are overshadowed by
the mutilations of their minds. Modern
war is not meant for modern nerves, and
this Russian author stresses one side of
modern war to which there has been already
a number of guarded references from the
front—the mention of combatants whose
brains have been unable to stand the strain
of the modern battle-front. There is one
terrible pictures of the mad soldiers who
wander about the battlefields. “Sometimes
they are fired at by mistake, sometimes on
purpose, for they make you lose all patience
with their unintelligible, terrifying cries.”
Andreief sees war as the act of madmen.
This is the realisation that this war will
bring to the world —but first it is necessary
to exterminate the insane brood of Huns
that thought to smash civilisation with
shrapnel.

est the ‘Dawn-morn’ rime controversy, the
writer, after some years of careful study
of Austral-English, ventures to rush in with
an opinion. As English is spoken by nine
out of ten Australians dawn and morn rime
perfectly. The Australian shortens the r
sound in the latter word, so that a listener
might easily conclude that is was spelled
mawn. Ido not criticise this pronunciation,
only cite it as a tendency on the part of
Australians towards elision of vowel sounds
and shortening consonants. There should
be, as a matter of fact, a great distinction
between morn and mourn; but listen care-
fully to very well educated Australians and
you scarcely find one in twelve who con-
siders the value of vowel sounds, or who
gives the slightest roll on the r, a letter
■which loses its value phonetically unless it
be slightly rolled or prolonged. Having
taught English for several years on both
lent, the writer has noticed
les mentioned are wide-

Austral-English. The ear
ied to this pronunciation,

Fires of Spring and How to
Light Them
This is Lesson II of the Kindergarten
Class for Lady Novelists. The first was
“Purple Mists and How to Write Them”
(R.P. 23/4/T4).
Ladies, now we come to the Seductive
Novel, the Near-the-Knuckle Novel, the
Society Novel of Sex, the Novel of the Fox-
Hunting Rich. It is guaranteed highly re-
munerative, and it is dead easy. That is
why we are discussing it to-day. The text
for to-day’s lesson is The Fire of Spring,
by Ethel Boileau (Nash). The ingredients
of this highly seasoned dish are easily pro-
cured.
(1) An alluring title, suggesting passion-
ate possibilities.
(2) A moated grange in a fox-hunting
county.
(3) A heroine unhappily married.
(4) A perfect rotter of a husband for No.
3.
(5) A society adventuress, necessary for
the disposal of No. 4.
(6) A big man, extremely male.
(7) A locked bedroom door.
(8) The adultery of the ill-used wife and
the big male. (Be careful, however, not to
call it adultery. The adultery is what the
rotter of a husband commits with the ad-
venturess. It’s just the sort of rotten thing
you’d expect him to do. But No. 8 is quite
a different sort of thing. It’s a grande
passion or something equally French, and
you’re merely dreadfully sorry for them
both.)
(9) The enchanted garden—usually situ-
ated in Africa, with the usual furnishings—
the sensuous perfumes of the East, the
weird melancholy of its music, the hot hush
of the desert, etc. (Here you go one better
than Robert Hichens.)
(10) More adultery in the garden of
dreams.
(11) The world calls the man. All Eng-
land is waiting to be saved, 'or regenerated,
or something, and the big male is the only
person for the job. So he go'es.
(12) They say farewell. (Linger over
this, and see that the background is sen-
suously romantic.)
(13) Kill him.
(14) Better stop. Leave it at that. Any-
how, you’ve filled 350 pages.
d*
The title need not trouble you much. This
novel was originally titled The Garden of
Dreams —sufficiently suggestive of Robert
Hichens, but the author found that some-
body else had already usurped that title. What
more natural, then, than to label her product
The Fire of Spring? It suggests the
awakening of love, the surge of passion.
Ethel makes her meaning clearer by putting
opposite the title-page: “In the life of
every man and woman there lies hidden an
enchanted garden.” Doesn’t that touch a
chord in your own heart? Haven’t you felt
just that, yourself? Read on, and you come
to the dedication: “To le pays du tendre.”
How romantic that sounds! Now you are in
the right mood to condone adultery.
Of course Angela only thought herself in
love with Tony, the rotter she married. She
was obviously attracted: she thought him
“a splendid devil.” For Angela—you must
always be careful about this—is no bread-
and-butter miss. Her mouth was “ador-
able; provoking, mocking, with the true
curve of passion.” She was a “ grande
amoureuse with a soul —there are a few
such, and even in the abandonment of pas-
sion she was elusive, unconquerable”....
“Angela had always been one of those in-
describably sensuous-looking women who
are born to set men’s blood on fire.” But,
mind you, she wasn’t common or obvious.
That must be left for her rival, the notorious
Mrs. Carton,. with whom her husband Tony
comes to grief. And then when Angela
knows that her love for her husband is dead
she meets the other bloke. .
Here, ladies, is where you have your
chance. Don’t worry about drawing any
real character; just project, from the
chaste recesses of your feminine brains,
the sort of male that you would like to love
you. You can let yourselves go—it is
quite safe in print. When you meet him,
you will become “suddenly conscious of a
peculiar strength and vitality in his gaze,”
and when he speaks to you you will notice
that his voice has “an intensely male ele-
ment.” His eyes, too, are “the eyes of mys-
tery and longing, of an impenetrable sadness
and reserve”; but his mouth and chin are
“firm, determined, vigorous.” Stress the
maleness of the man—and, after all, why
shouldn’t a man be male? Of course, his
clothes are “perfect, everything exactly as it
ought to be, and not the least suspicion of
exaggeration anywhere.” He is always “per-
fectly turned out” and he always “dominates
his clothes.” And when he hunts —of course,
he hunts —he is “splendidly mounted, on a
fine blood bay, standing 16 hands, with
powerful quarters and a tremendous fore-
hand —but a rolling, vicious eye.” And what
more natural for him to remark that he likes
something to manage, and that he wouldn’t
give anything for a horse that hadn’t a touch
of the devil—or a woman either? And when
he comes close to Angela it is inevitable that
“a curious electric current seems to pass
from him to her.”
J*
Of course, they fall in love with each
other —that is what they are there for. He
doesn’t beat about the bush: one of his
first remarks to Angela is, “What others have,
I want,” and “his voice became dangerously
attractive... .and the devil in his eyes
caught and chained the one in hers.” And
ultimately, inevitably, “she saw his nostrils
quiver.” So what more natural than that
Angela should stretch her arm out to him
impulsively, while she told him how fine it all
was, and how she believed in him, or that he
should take her hand, and another wave of
electricity should go up it right to Angela’s
heart? It is true that he knew she was
married, and that he had been engaged to
defend her husband in a divorce case. How-
ever, when men are male they don’t allow
a trifling fact like that to stand in their way.
And this is how he talks to her:—
I want to stand and watch you sleeping
with your, hair about you, and your Kk
buried in the pillow like a tired child. I
want to watch the shade of your long black
lashes upon your cheek-until in your sleep
you feei my eyes upon you—and you move
a little and sigh and half-turn towards me
and stretch your arms, throwing tne clothes
from you and then I shall see the line of
your throat and the whiteness of your body*
beneath a cloud of lace.” Beautiful and
tender and poetic, isn’t it? You can go on
like that tor pages—until he does.
But the divorce case falls through, and
your heros public career would be blighted
by any scandal. He is an immensely im-
portant man, of course. True, he might go
to Canada with her ; but she reminds him of
his dreams a united Empire, a regener-
ated England. You must finish your work
here; they are counting on you.” So you
see, an open scandal would have fatal conse-
quences for the Empire. But there remains
Tangier for a month of happiness in a hidden
garden. Here a careful study of Robert
Hichens becomes necessary. The male rents
a villa ; and you can splurge to your innocent
heart’s content over the furnishings. Angela’s
room had a floor of pink marble, covered
with deep-colored Persian rugs, the bed a
low, broad divan. And leading out of the
bedroom was a bathroom, also of pink marble,
the bath sunk in the floor and full of clear’
green water. And the background is the sen-
suous east. What more can any maiden’s
heart desire?
But remember always, ladies, your charac-
ters must be aristocratic. Scatter lords and
ladies with a lavish hand ; and you can easily
fill a chapter with a description of a hunt.
On the tables “everything was of cut glass—
except the knives and forks—rare Georgian
glass such as one does not often see.” Almost
Oorellian is that touch about the knives and
forks; it saves your reader from imagining
that they too might be of cut glass, which
would obviously show that you were not
familiar with the furnishings of the best
houses. That would be fatal to your circula-
tion.
&
Well, ladies, you may perhaps ask what
good such a book is ? What relation, if any,
has it to real life, and why should it be
necessary to write it at all? My answer is:
This is a commodity that is in great demand
by the readers of your sex who frequent sub-
scription libraries, and there are good busi-
ness men—not excessively male, perhaps—
who are always willing to supply a public
demand by putting on the market as many
Fires of Spring as you like to supply them
with. My advice to you is to delve into the
maidenly recesses of your innocent hearts,
and get busy.
e
“How ‘Bellerive’ would have gloried in
those crabs!” this page remarked after
quoting a peculiarly “Bellerivean” poem by
Thomas Hardy. And promptly “Bellerive”
glories, “with all do respect and honor to
Mr. Hardy.”
A male body was dragged
From a river deep,
That swollen and blue—
My blood did creep,
Borne to the morgue—
Without pomp nor grace,
With cruel crabs clinging
To the dead man’s face,
Hardy and I are the
Two boys for drabs,
But tis a joke to me
To rhyme on crabs.
With the sudden enthusiasm for the foun-
dation of rifle-clubs throughout Australia
there should be a big demand for Ernest H.
Robinson’s admirable and non-technical Rifle
Training for War (Cassell; Is.). It gives
invaluable hints, not to be found in the ser-
vice manuals.
George Hooligan sorrows with “Bill Bow-
yang” (“Red Page,” 11/3/’ls), for he, too,
has been stung:—-
Some months ago I was fool enough to send a good
meaty article to a London agency. There were no
reading fees, they simply charged 6s. for submitting
to five papers, and were to deduct 20 per cent, of the
payments. In due time the article came back with-
out the five bob, and with the names of five papers,
which it had been alleged it was submitted to. There
were three of the five which in my densest ignorance
I would not have dreamt of submitting the article to,
for it was totally unsuited to them. Just the same
way that an “Abo.” par. would be rejected by S. M.
Herald. I did not despair, but seat another article
with another ss. A stereotyped reply was duly
received, saying that the article would require to be
recast, which would entail an expenditure of 10s.
I reasoned out that if the article were accepted it
would be worth about £1 Is. After I paid ss. for
submission, 10s. for recasting, and then 20 per cent,
of the balance, there would not be much left for
stamps. Hence they have still got my article and
65., and I am not likely to see either.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Karl, the German.
Who will tell me, who determine
Why his heart should still be alien,
As he smokes there, Karl, the German,
Thirty years and more Australian?
Round about him, rich as marrow, _
Lie his acres, spread and splendid ;
Thirty years, with plough and harrow,
Has he tilled their breadth, and tended.
Yon and yonder dwell his neighbors.
Are his thoughts of them, I wonder —
Or of bloody sounds and sabres,
Screaming shells and guns that thunder?
Were the Prussian ten times over
Blasted with his country’s blindness,
In her madness —still her lover—
Could he all forget their kindness?
Yet his pipe-bowl fades and whitens,
And, caught up to heights elysian,
Lo! his country flames and brightens,
Like a large orb on his vision !
Thus he hears her broadsides thunder,
Thus he sees her mighty, regal—
Sees the round world cowering under
Prussian heel and Prussian eagle;
Sees —though spacious plains environ
(Following where his wishes lead him)
Prussia’s crushing grip of iron
Tense upon the throat of Freedom.
So she stands with flowers to wreath her,
Laurels thick and brave upon her;
Prostrate in the mire beneath her
Liberty and Truth and Honor!
Karl the German ! Time shall wither
Ere thy dream her scroll disgraces !
Thank the Fates that sent thee hither,
Set thy feet in pleasant places;
Gave thee wheat-land, gave thee wine-land,
Gave thee sunshine such as never,
Warming all thy storied Rhineland,
Ripened grapes by stream and river.
Fangs of wolf and claw of vulture,
Tooth and talon —who would cherish?
German faith and German culture
Likewise, scorned of man, shall perish.
Karl the German ! old and hoary,
Smoke thy pipe and love thy neighbor;
Quit thy dreams of power and glory;
Plough and harrow wait thy labor.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Strangle Tree.
Though you make a detour wide
An you would not wish to see,
Steal but one brief glance aside
And you’ll find the strangle-tree!
Stark as Death and bare of leaf,
Many a mile it may be seen,
Grey as is the hair of Grief,
Like a spectre ’mid the green!
Bleached by rain and scarred by storm,
Taller than the forest things;
Lifting up a draggled form.
Turning, turning as he swings.
He that played a caitiff part,
So we left him to the gales,
With a red sin in his heart
And the life-blood on his nails.
What though priest with power to shrive,
Placed no blessing on his head,
We who hated him alive
Should we fear him now he’s dead?
Yet to pass the strangle-tree,
Squire and man and maid would blench!
All save one of low degree—-
She, the toll-gate keeper’s wench.
For each morning, all alone,
Fares she forth, the gossips say;
With her basket full of stone
For to drive the birds away!
Simple folk as she goes by
Nudge each other; lass and lad
Each avoid her roving eye.
All the village call her mad.
And they say that, rain or fair,
Hies she to that silent place,
There to pray a mumbled prayer
Gazing at his swollen face.
For though Evil must atone,
And the tainted hand must pay,
E'en the hangman's heart of stone
Cannot drive her love away!
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
Ballade of a Dark Lady.
At many shrines I knelt of old,
But in the mists of yesterday
Felice the fair and Babs the bold,
Whose tresses were as new-mown hay,
Now dwell; while I may homage pay
To one with charms more rich and rare
Than theirs, or those of Madge and May—
I love a Maid with Midnight Hair!
The trivial tales are quickly told
Of loves that languish and decay;
No fond illusions do I fold
Concerning Madeline or Fay.
Dark Madeline a wisp of gray
Disdained to hide, and Fay was fair,
But now, in quite another way,
I love a Maid with Midnight Hair!
A cataract of living gold
About Tot’s shoulders used to play;
Jean’s nut-brown strands, but loosely rolled,
Each vagrant zephyr led astray;
Blue-eyed Phrynet'te, insanely gay,
Red tresses flaunted free from care,
But, though these still their charms array,
I love a Maid with Midnight Hair!
L’Envoi.
Eros, beneath your kindly sway,
Of blisses I have known my share;
But for no further boon I pray-
I love a Maid with Midnight Hair!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON OWEN.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will he paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, APRIL 1, 1915.
WILHELM VON POLYPHEMUS.
In estimating the possible duration of the
war it is necessary always to remember that
Germany is the modern Polyphemus, with
only one eye. An army bereft now of the
services of aeroplanes is almost as sightless
as a blind giant in a dark cave,
and almost as impotent. Though the
enemy’s guns may be decimating it from
behind an adjacent hill, the position of the
batteries can only be guessed at, and the
chances against dealing with them are im
measurable. Vast reinforcements may be
approaching with hostile intent. The army
without aeroplanes can only make its dis-
positions when the foe heaves in sight. What
gives vision to the aeroplanes—the article
without which it is a mere tangle of wood
and metal —is petrol. In the matter of
petrol Germany, by comparison with the
Allies, is one-eyed. It has what remains of
the stock which was accumulated for a war
that was not expected by its military chiefs
to last longer than six months; and it has
what it has been able to import in the last
six months from neutral countries. It is
already impossible, in Germany, according to
returned travellers, to buy motor-spirit of
any sort for private use. The unhappy
Deutscher’s car is mouldering in a garage
when it hasn’t been commandeered for mili-
tary purposes; and there is no benzine to
exorcise the stains of the beer and sauer-
kraut from its owner’s waistcoat. When the
end of the State’s supply comes, the uncouth
bully of Europe may be in as agonising and
helpless a position as his inebriated Sicilian
prototype was when the fire-stick entered
his brain.
* * * *
There is also the matter of legs, and the
problem of arms. A modern nation, obsessed
with the “will to conquer”—Teuton for
the “resolve to rob” —denends as much on
strong arms and swift legs as any raiding
nuisance in all mythology. Germany, so
long as it hopes to sustain its role of Poly-
phemus, must hit hard and move briskly.
For that it requires plenty of rubber and
copper. An army without well-tyred motor
vehicles is in as invidious a position as a
burglar with locomotor ataxia. It is at all
times liable to fall down and be stumbled
over, and beaten flat, and hurried off to
gaol. And an army without ample copper is
like a pugilist with a broken hand. It can
neither defend nor attack effectively. Unless
the reports from the world’s markets mis-
represent the position, the Hun has em-
barked on the most gigantic steal in history
handicapped at the very least by (1) failing
sight, (2) corns on both feet, and (3)
rheumatism of the arms —all of which com-
plaints are liable to develop into cases of
complete disablement. #
The enemy’s petrol-shortage is stressed in
nearly all his own papers. As regards
rubber, however, it is claimed that, "with
good foresight, Germany got in a great sup-
ply before the war.” But rubber does not
keep. By no means can it be prevented from
quickly perishing. Nor does the yarn as- to
large supplies carry conviction when viewed
from another aspect. In the year before
the war Germany and Austria exported be-
tween them £7,523,000 worth of rubber
goods. This represented a fair average year’s
output. As they have never taken more
than about 15 per cent, of the world’s sup-
ply, it is hard to see the margin for a store
of State-owned rubber. But whatever the
stock in hand may be there is certainly no
more arriving. Britain has complete control
of the world’s rubber. It began by prevent-
ing the exportation of the raw substance to
any foreign port whatever. The U.S.A.,
which had been absorbing 45 per cent, of the
world’s production for years, were badly
scared. They were willing to do or promise
anything for the humble article which is
milked from trees, by naked savages. When
they were told that they could buy it
once more provided they undertook not to re-
export it in any form except to Britain, their
gratitude was boundless. Germany also
cheered Up considerably. But in the past
few days a blockade has been instituted
against Germany. Unlike the blockades of
the past it is apparently going to be almost
wholly effective. Things are thus made worse
than ever for the Hun. It looks as though
his legs will not be the same again till the
war is over.
* * * *
So far as copper is concerned, two things
are known for certain: That Germany had
not an abnormal supply—at any rate, in the
crude state —when the war began, and that
it is rapidly consuming what it has day and
night. The authorities have recently started
buying used copper cartridge cases and any-
thing else containing copper that can be
raked up on the battlefield. All the tele
phone and electric traction wires that can
be spared are being taken down and melted.
Civilians have been required to give up all
domestic utensils of copper throughout the
Empire. The Cologne Gazette mentioned
lately some of the articles which the nation
had other uses for. It implored the people
to see whether there was copper in their
curtain-rings, toys, door-knobs, gas and
water connections, clothes-pegs, lanterns,
lamps, band instruments, and so on. All
this may be further evidence of the Teutonic
passion for thoroughness. But on the princi-
ple that a man with a few sovereigns in the
bank does not commonly dry-blow the gut-
ters for the treasures that may lie there, it
looks suspicious. If copper really is short
in Germany, the time may come when the
latter-day Polyphemus will have nothing
left to fight with but his mouth.
* * * *
All these possibilities having been .can-
vassed, it remains to be said that they are
only possibilities. Their interest arises from
the fact that they may conceivably end the
war with dramatic suddenness. Meantime,
it is going on as if it meant never to stop.
The ravager of Europe may have only one
eye, but the organ is in good working order
at present. He is holding the approaches
to his cave with one strong hand, and dealing
out death and destruction with the other.
He is as quick a mover as any of his foes,
and as hard a hitter. If the Allied blockade
can blind him, or lop off a leg or an arm, it
will be a happy solution of a painful pro-
blem. It may be, though, that civilisation is
going to be less fortunate than Odysseus
was. If so, the world has an awful ordeal
before it.
THE ANTI-SOCIALISM OF LABOR.
The most striking feature of recent politics
has been Labor’s dislike of its own pro-
gramme. State Labor administrations do
nothing, by abolishing the imported-
Governor system, or wiping out the super-
fluous “Upper House” arrangement, or re-
ducing the membership of the Assemblies,
to cheapen government. Borrowing rages
more furiously than ever. About the only
distinction between the new condition of
things and the old is that different men are
handling the screws and perquisites of office.
Private Enterprise, which once quaked
with dread when it heard a Labor candi-
date talk, has become easy in its mind again. It
realises that Labor’s threats were only elec-
tioneering guff. In the biggest of the States
a British company has just secured a con-
tract to perform for the Government activi-
ties which, in the Toriest of countries, are
the business of Socialism. In the Federal
sphere the tariff is being attended to by a
body whose recommendations come in so
gradually that the present generation’s grand-
children seem unlikely to enjoy effective
Protection. Life under Labor in Australia
is a great time for foreign-traders, anti-
Socialists and opponents of democracy in
general.
* * * *
For many years this paper has been call-
ing for the establishment of stud-farms for
the breeding of army remounts. Labor Gov-
ernments came and went, and did nothing.
The idea, sometimes expressed, more often
implied, was that war was a long way off,
and, meanwhile, there were better things
to “blew” the nation’s money on than back-
blocks horse-farms. What the politicians
who used this argument meant was that
there were more vote-producing things. Now
war, on a gigantic scale, has arrived. Im-
perial horse-buyers are, in consequence, at
work in every State. The Government is
snapping up all the horses it can get for
its own use. It is no good repining at this
stage, but it may usefully be noted that had
stud farms been instituted, as they logically
should have been when the universal train-
ing system was started, they would have
paid the Empire in this crisis nearly- as well
as the local navy.
Stud farms being non-existent, the Gov-
ernment has had to do the next best thing
and buy its horses from dealers and breed-
ers. There are two ways of doing that.
Under the Socialistic system, which is fol-
lowed by Britain and other theoretically
anti-Socialistic countries, expert buyers of
recognised probity are employed at fixed
salaries, plus travelling expenses. It is
their business to drive hard bargains, and, in
general, do their best, for the State. Under
the Private Enterprise system, which is fol-
lowed by the nominally Socialistic adminis-
tration of the Commonwealth, an unsalaried
buyer is employed on a commission basis —
his chief aim being, in the nature of things,
to do the best possible business for himself,
so far as he honestly can. In one horse-
breeding State one dealer has, practically, a
monopoly of the business. It is true that a
beginning was made there with a Socialistic
buyer. A well-known man was engaged at
a fairly good salary. But his travelling ex-
penses were not found; he had to pay for
his purchases by means of vouchers (a hope-
less system in a trade which is, traditionally,
on a cash basis) ; and there was always
liable to be trouble about the certifying of
his horses. The Government quickly changed
that arrangement. A Private Enterprise
man was put on who gets no salary.
He makes a profit of so much on
each horse. Putting it as low as
£2, his monthly earnings must equal a
trust magnate’s. He is in a position to pay
cash in connection with every deal. His
own vet. sees and passes the horses. lie
probably buys the best available, since his
honesty is unquestioned, and he knows all
there is to be learned about the friend of
man. But there is no other check on his
movements, which is not exactly business
as the cynical world of commerce under-
stands it. And, finally, unless the Govern-
ment agent is less shrewd than he is reputed
to be, he is improving the shining hour in
another way. His quest of sound, young
four-year-olds and over naturally brings
him in touch with the State’s sound young
three-ycar-olds and under, which in conse-
quence of the drought are a drug on the
market. A good-quality animal of this divi-
sion is to be got for a ten-pound note. A
man who was buying horses on a vast scale
would be foolish if he did not sink his spare
cash in animals which, this time next year,
are almost certain to be worth double, or
treble, their present price, no matter how
the war fares.
* * * *
If Russia, which has been importing horses
on a tremendous scale of recent years, had.
done business on the same lines, the dealers
of Petrograd and Moscow would long since
have been millionaires. The allegedly effete
Muscovite autocracy was not, however, quite
so foolish. It employed soldier-buyers at a
fixed salary, and saved millions. There seems
no reason why Australia should act differ-
ently—except that its Government is sup-
posed to be run on Socialistic lines.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More Scrap Heap.
Having spent many days hearing the elabor-
ated arguments of expensive counsel, and
more days considering what it was going to
do about the matter, the Inter-State Com-
mission ordered an injunction to issue
against the N.S.W. Government restraining
that second-hand outfit from further pro-
ceedings under the Wheat Acquisition Act.
The High Court has now intimated that the
Inter-State Commission’s injunction wasn’t
worth tuppence, because the Commission had
no authority to issue such a document or
order —in other words, that all the original
proceedings before Messrs. Piddtngton,
Swinburne and Lockyer were a waste of
money, a farce and a mockery of law. All of
this is calculated to give the community
cause for great admiration of the men who
fashioned the Inter-State Commission,
dressed it in the uniform of bogus authority,
and sent it forth to war armed with soft
nosed bullets and a bayonet rigid as a piece
of string. The intention behind the clauses
of the Constitution giving the Common-
wealth authority to appoint an Inter-State
Commission to handle the provisions relat-
ing to Trade and Commerce was undoubt-
edly that this tribunal should have powers
of adjudication and enforcement. This in-
tention. entrusted for verbal expression to
the eminent lawyers who drafted the Con-
stitution, has now been found by other
equally eminent lawyers (including one or
two of those who did the drafting, or, at any
rate, had their fingers in the job) not to
have been carried into effect by the words
used, with the result that the Federal Par-
liament, instead of, as it imagined, bringing
into existence a Court, created merely a
thing of shreds and patches—an instrumen-
tality of government with certain limited
functions ancillary to mere administration.
The essential part of the Act constituting
the Commission having been thus tossed out
of the High Court window as invalid, and
the Commission itself given an uppercut 'of
great violence, the question of what shall be
done with the corpse or remnant calls for
attention. Apparently without an amend-
ment of the Constitution the Commission
now has no other function than to wander
aimlessly round the continent hearing evi-
dence about the tariff. Its usefulness in this
direction long since became more than doubt-
ful. Taking the most charitable view of the
disembowelled remains now on hand, the
only conclusion possible seems to be that the
sooner they are buried the better. For the
rest, the High Court’s judgment contains one
hint to the Federal Parliament. When any
supposedly constitutional step is taken, a
friend should be put un to immediately chal-
lenge it in the High Court for the purpose
of finding out whether a second step can be
risked. Until we are prepared to scrap the
Constitution and bring in Unification, that
would be the ouickest way and the cheapest.
The cost of finding out that tliis Inter-State
Commission was a sham has been enormous.
The Cracked Federal Instrument.
Tiie High Court’s judgment on the con-
stitutional issue raised by the Common-
wealth Government’s challenge of the
N.S.W. Wheat Acquisition Act has been
received by several Bumbles who draw Min-
isterial salaries from the Ma State with
squeaks of much delight. People with a
bioader outlook, however, will regret that
the law is such as the corpipunity now finds
it to be. The judgment handed down ,by
the High Court adds another to the proofs
that the Constitution is so gravely defective
as to bear very little re'sgmblanoe.'tp.. tlje
instrument the people thought'
bargaining for. By the of. appro-
priation each State can now. w'ifho\ity‘ques-
tion, hold up trade at its borders
And the reason js not that any such t ravesty
on national government 'was < contemplated
in pre-Foderafion days, but 'that' the
Constitution, through defect in draftsman-
ship, allows it. So far as wheat is
concerned, the community could understand
the Commonwealth handling the situation
created by drought, not in the special inter-
ests of a particular section of the continent,
but according to the requirements of all
the people of Australia. What they were
unable to understand prior to this revela-
tion of Constitutional impotency on the
part of the national Government was the
reason for each State fiddling about with
ridiculous Boards and Commissions, regard-
ing the citizens of neighboring States as
aliens, and making a general mess of what
should really have been a national under-
taking. The case for amendment of the
Constitution is overwhelming, for the people
cannot be expected to sit quietly by and
accept without action so complete a leversnl
to what Federation was expected to yield
The Menace.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Germany will use every opportunity to starve
England in revenge for England’s starving Germany.
—Official statement.
Wilhelm the Hogman,
Slaughterer-general,
Tearer of treaties,
Heavyweight partner
Of death and destruction,
Spoke in the hearing
Of ships and of Zeppelins,
Cannon and howitzers,
Mine-fields and submarines,
Saying tremendously
“England shall starve!”
And the ships and Zeppelins and cannon
and howitzers and mine-fields and sub-
marines answered as one goat: “Eng-
land shall starve.”
Wilhelm the Hogman
Said it in August last,
Loudly and angrily.
August passed warmly,
September went gustily,
' Fretful October
Slid on to November.
London and Birmingham
Glasgow and Liverpool
Still had their bread and
Potatoes and beef.
And when Wilhelm asked what was doing ,
the ships and Zeppelins and cannon and
howitzers and mine-fields and sub-
marines gloomily murmured: “England
shall starve.”
Breakfast grew scarcer in
Munich and Wurtemberg;
Deadlier sausages
Raged in Berlin.
Dresden and Hanover
Used up in sauerkraut
Husks that the swine had
Declined to consume.
Wilhelm the Hogman
Observed it regretfully,
Marked how the old year
Went out with a frostbite,
And England had still a
Firm fork in the beef.
And when the slaughterer-general asked,
again what wds doing, the ships and
Zeppelins and cannon and howitzers
and mine-fields and submarines
answered thickly, with ’fog in their
throats: “England shall starve.”
Wilhelm the Hogman,
Scanning the oceans for
„ Murder and joy,
Saw where a merchant ship
Bumped by a submarine
Hurled to the planets wool,
Tallow and hides.
“There goes the dinner of
England,” he chortled loud ;
“Famished Westphalia
Shall yet be avenged.
AVrite it in jaewspapers,
Blow it on war-bugles,
Shout it in Mecklenburg:
. .... England shall starve!”
And the ships with barnacles on them and
the Zeppelins that had dropped bombs
on Norfolk turnip-fields and the cannon
that were getting worn out with noise
and the howitzers that were mostly
sitting in the mud and the mine fields
that hadn't yet been dragged away and
put to sleep and the active but in-
effective submarines murmured doubt-
fully but dutifully: “England shall
starve.”
England, despite the noise,
Still with a fork in beef,
Still with a crust of bread,
Even some artichokes,
Mustard and cress,
Heard with astonishment
Wilhelm remarking it:
“England shall starve.”
They hadn't noticed the remark before, for
they thought the ships and Zeppelins
and cannon and howitzers and mine-
fields and submarines were merely say-
ing: “England be blowed!”
Now in their wrath they rose.
This was the fatal word.
Hogmany’s arrogance
Aimed at their waistcoat girth,
Threatened the dining-room.
Large was their wrath.
Fiercely they spoke the word,
War-bugles echoed it,
Newspapers quoted it,
All men applauded it:
“Wilhelm shall hang!”
And the great ships without barnacles, and
the new armies without scars or fatigue
and the big guns that were already
overpowering those of the Hogman took
it up cheerfully and with enthusiasm —
“Wilhelm shall hang!”
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.


Kitchener, Socialist.
The clash between military preparation and
Trade Unionism which has led Kitchener
to establish something like State Socialism in
England was not unlooked for. It is all very
well to denounce the workman as unpatri-
otic when he kicks at a time of national
crisis; but the man who turns the wheels
and grinds out the things Kitchener’s
armies require has the perfect right to retort
that you must give him something to be
patriotic about before you can expect a
white heat of patriotism. There are a good
many people in England who toil on from
year to year in a pretty hopeless fashion,
getting barely enough to eat, wearing the
coarsest clothing, with little in the way of
recreation, denied even the means of cleanli-
ness and health, to whom it can matter little
enough whether England comes out on top
in the present struggle or . Germany battles
her way through. Whoever is master the
European workman gets just as little as his
masters can afford to give him, and, like
Thomas Hardy’s Dorset peasant who could
see nothing beyond the eight shillings a
week on which he had to keep a family, he
is apt to be blind to those higher interests
on which the daily newspapers lay so much
stress. What are his higher interests? His
employers, representing to his mind the
nation, have narrowed those higher interests
down to the far-off hope of winning an extra
sixpence a day. It is not much on which
to found an ardent patriotism. Certainly,
if he knew, he has a better chance of getting
that sixpence under English masters than
under German : but he is to be pardoned if
he puts the Trade Union that fights for
him above the interests of the employer who
starves him. However galling it may be to
find the British workman hang back in the
traces when the nation wants all the pull
it can get, there can be very little doubt
that John Smith, workman, has won a
very big thing for himself by hanging back.
He has secured at one word of the military
authorities an advantage that he could not
have gained by a thousand strikes. He has
got his private employer wiped out and has
become the direct servant of the State, with
the hope of a share in profits and even a
chance of medals and decorations if he
puts his muscle into his work. That an
organiser like Kitchener sees the advan-
tage of this goes far to put matters in the
right light and show where the industrial
failure lay. It is not John Smith, work-
man, who has proved a failure: all that is
proved is that J. Smith has failed under
Private Enterprise. The organisation of him
and his fellow-workmen under a new sys-
men for war foreshadows their organisation
also for . peace. If England cannot afford
to have its gun-making and bullet-moulding
muddled by P.E. in time of conflict, it is
pretty clear that it cannot in time of peace
afford to let its manufacture of calico or
soap or iron bedsteads be muddled either.

The Soldier in the Workshop.
The big war now cleaning up Europe is
being fought in the workshop as much as in
the trenches. The man who goes to the
Front and takes the risks and the credit is
no more important than the man who stays
at home and constructs battle ironmongery.
This is a fact that is only beginning to force
its way into the intelligence of a lot of
people to whom it should have been a
commonplace 20 years ago. The Germans
were aware of it, and because they had long
ago put their knowledge into practice they
were able to score at the outset of the dis-
turbance, and have been able to hang on
against three great nations and a small one
through a long, bitter winter. In old days
when a nation went to war any of its citi-
zens who felt inclined or who happened to
be sober enough at the time went into their
own backyards and looked for an axe or
any other implement that was hard and
heavy. Once the axe was picked up the
soldier was armed and ready, and the battle
wrnnt to the side that could hit quickest and
hardest. All this is changed. A big gun
takes months to make ; a warship years. To
increase suddenly the manufacture of the
implements of battle is almost impossible.
It is first of all necessary to increase the
machinery for making them and to train the
men to use that machinery. England,
according to Kitchener, having manufac-
turing appliances at hand, has trebled the
output of war material since the Germans
rushed on Liege. It is a notable perform-
ance—perhaps the most notable performance
of the war—and one that must count for
more than all the gallant deeds done in the
fighting line; and because of it none can
doubt that the grim game is going
well. The lesson for Australia is
plain. The defence workshop must be put
in order quickly. The trained war mechanic
is as important as the other soldier, and the
work done 20 years before the day of test-
ing is the thing that will settle the argu-
ment when it arrives. The idea that Aus-
tralia has lots of time is the most pernicious
one conceivable. Time is the very thing
Australia cannot afford to squander. This
continent has some wealth—a good deal
of it potential rather than actual —a cer-
tain amount of pluck and resource, a mod-
erate supply of energy and enthusiasm, and
a vast quantity of the raw material of which
effective defence weapons can be built; but
time is the unknown quantity in the
national, problem. The man who knows the
burglar is coming but does not know whether
he will arrive at midnight or at five o’clock
in the morning will be well advised to get
bis revolver ready and huDt up the cart-
ridges the evening before. If he lets time
slip and goes out to try to buy a six-shooter
after the pubs are closed, the burglar may
have his plans ruined by falling over the
family cat, but the odds are much in favor
of his taking'away the pick of the wife’s
jewellery.

The Money Bug.
In the middle of December last the Censor
brought off a masterstroke by prosecuting
the: Perth Daily News for publishing a
description by a passenger l of the defences
of the Suez Canal. The charge said that it
amounted to giving “information which
mig|ht be of use to the .enemy.” Taking the
view that all a passenger could have seen
was as near nothing as possible, and that
anyhow the information was weeks old,
Davies, P.M., dismissed the case with scorn
and promptitude, and turned to the daily
array of drunks and disorderlies. Now that
the information is four months old and the
original rigmarole has been republished all
over the Commonwealth, the High Court
takes a hand. In the interval the enemy
has found out ten times more about the
defences of the Suez Canal —if only through
the medium of the survivors of the force
which left 200 dead Germans, not to men-
tion Turks, floating about in it—than any
tourist is likely to learn from the deck of
a liner. The proceeding illustrates the High
Court’s conception of its duties. In
Eather’s notorious case nothing more im-
portant than the liberty of an Australian
citizen was in issue. The High Court
couldn’t be bothered. This Perth matter
concerns money. Therefore no pains must be
spared. It can’t even be hung up till the High
Court gets in due course to the *Western
State. The case is hauled east to be argued
afresh, ,so that our highest tribunal may
decide whether the publication of the casual
impressions of a Canal passenger is likely
to shatter the foundations of the British
Empire. It is quite on the cards that, since
that tribunal has decided that it ought not
to interfere with the judgment of the lower
Court on a matter of fact, the costly absurd-
ity will be passing through the Canal
in question well after the war is over—on
its way to the Privy Council and further
costs. The paper has money, the Censor has
money, and the issue is money. This
Money bug is almost our worst pest.

One Way Australia Can Help.
“W.8.D.” proposes to make four loaves out
of three: —
The intention of the N. S. Wales Government to
make a State monopoly of the bakery trade is not
the novel thing it looks. In Germany and France
the production of “war bread” synchronised with
mobilisation. Morally the proceeding is sound so
far as N. S. Wales is concerned. When the Common-
wealth Government announced at the beginning of
the war that it was “with Britain to the last ear
of wheat” it said a big thing, for it is partly on
Australia that Britain and the Allies depend for
bread. It is hard for the well-fed citizen of the
British Empire to realise how important wheat is.
They can best do so by looking through the eyes of
people such as the captain of the Prinz Eitel Fried-
rich, who was prepared to risk war with the United
States for the sake of sinking a cargo of it; 01 of
Admiral von Tirpitz, who has willingly lost 12
submarines on the off-chance of destroying a ship-
load here or there. Tirpitz and his friends under-
stand its value well. They are living on potato-
bread. And even of that debased food a recent
Berliner Tageblatt said: “Take care of it as if
it were holy. Do not squander even a crumb.”
The measure of Hans’s incapacity to supply himself
with wheaten flour may be taken by Britain, where
the cost of living has risen 25 per cent, in spite of
a command of the seas more absolute than that of
Nelson’s day. Thanks to Australia’s Foreign Trade
party, which consistently set its face against the
establishment of factories for the creation of muni-
tions of war, Australia has been able to do little
enough so far in the matter of supplying armies to
the Empire. But if N. S. Wales and every other
State took a firm grip of the bread question, and
produced the right sort of loaf, they could effect in
combination a sufficiently substantial saving on
wheat at present wasted to bring about an appreci-
able reduction in the cost of living in the United
Kingdom as well as here. This can be done by
establishing what is called the Standard loaf. The
establishment of this would set free for Britain
that 25 per cent, of our own wheat consumption
which is now wasted. In 1911 the Harmsworth
Press started a campaign in the. United Kingdom
in favor of this loaf. It was taken into use at
Windsor Castle, and thereupon everybody commenced
to use it. The point was that in order to secure
white flour the more nutritive parts of the wheat
berry were thrown away. The Daily Mail, “follow-
ing the advice of eight of the most eminent physi-
cians and surgeons of the United Kingdom, urged
the millers and bakers of the country to produce
and sell flour and bread containing 80 per cent, of
the wheat grain.” Experiments made by “the lead-
ing physiologists of England, France and Russia”
showed that bread made from “standard” flours was
5 per cent, more “nourishing, life-sustaining, and
made stronger men and women than the anaemic
sterilised white flour of the modern roller mill.” The
same paper said that 25,000,000 sacks of a white
flour absorbed by Britain last year were produced
by discarding 40 per cent, of the wheat grain—-a
waste of about 10,000,000 sacks. In “household
flour” 30 per cent, of the grain is discarded. “Should
the Government order the bakers to produce at once
a loaf containing the whole of the grain, less per-
haps 5 per cent, of hard exterior bran, the bakers
would probably be able at once to add' 25 per cent,
to the loaf production of the country.” There has
been any amount of talk about the sacrifices Aus-
tralia is prepared to make. Here is a great service
which isn’t even a sacrifice. Up to date we have
sunk the Emden, cleared the Pacific of Germans,
helped to hold Egypt, and with any luck are likely
to play a substantial part in taking Constantinople.
If in addition this country could lower the cost of
living in the United Kingdom by 5 per cent, it
would be doing almost as much as if it sent another
army corps.
Melbourne’s Civic Birds Foul Their Own
Nest.
Melbourne City Council has frankly con-
fessed itsejf a friend of Tammanyism and a
patron of log-rolling. Councillor Atkins,
who is a comparatively young municipal
business man, moved
That the tender be declared invalid of any ten-
derer for works or supplies to the corporation who
approached a councillor between the time of lodging
a tender and the date of acceptance thereof.
Presumably a glaring instance of personal
appeal to the city fathers, and of jobbery
resulting from it had recently occurred,
though Atkins, after the manner of one who
throws out a hint instead of casting an
aspersion, went on to say he “did not imply
that there had been improper practices.”
The motion merely asserted the good princi-
ple that all tenders should be considered on
their merits, and all councillors should be
protected from the importunities,of \yhisper-
ers who want the Council to have no good
principles at all. This aroused the siporn of
Henry Weedon, ex-Mayor. He depounced
the motion as “somewhat of a reflection on
the Council,” and evidently thought that any-i
thing in the way of a reflection on his little
self was a disgrace to the country. Ten-
derers, according to Weedon, shouljfl
full liberty to approach councillors impro-
perly, seeing that he and his brethren “know
how-to treat these people in a proper man-
ner. The Atkins motion had, of course,
proposed to treat them properly by cancel-
ling their tenders, whereas Weedon’s con-
ception of proper treatment might mean a
drink for aught he explained to the contrary,
lhis, indeed, was so obvious that brother
Labena advised his fat colleagues not to
turn the down lest they would seem
to offer “an invitation to tenderers to come
along.” Nevertheless the Council did turn
it down as “impracticable.” Members had
been warned by Cabena not to leave them-
selves under suspicion; yet they deliberately
declined to put theipselves above it. They
preferred to admit that, as long as tenderers
think it worth while to interview them, they
will submit to be interviewed—and endure
the insult.

It was refreshing to get straight from
Paris the confession that French military
strategy was a series of costly mistakes at
the outset. The truth had no chance of
getting to the public until it was officially
statednow. it comes as one of the most
reassuring bits of war news we have re-
ceived. In plain English the Germans
carried everything before them in Northern
I ranee merely because the defence was not
properly planned. When the German fight-
ing machine was at its very best, the
French machine was at its very worst. The
invaders were working according to time-
table ; the French armies had no time-table.
They missed chances to stop the invasion
and lost ground they never should have lost.
The fact is candidly owned up to, and the
candor inspires confidence. It is reassur-
ing, too, in that it discounts the credit given
to German prowess in August. As soon as
the resistance became cohesive the German
masses ceased to be “irresistible”—which
was so horribly surprising to the Kaiser
that he sacked Yon Moltke, his boss
strategist, and has made no advance to
speak of since.
The Peace cranks appear to be equally
Teutonic in their humorlessness a'nd in
their ideas of what ought to happen to
belligerents after the war. One Dillon,
hon. sec. of the Melbourne society, told his
brethren the other night that “the society
must insistently demand peace in the
future.” Its business is to see that there
be “no more wars, whether just or unjust.”
Wherefore Dillon, taking his pen in his
hand, will be prepared to touch any right-
eously indignant, or unrighteously comba-
tive, nation on the shoulder and say: “Now,
stop it! No fighting!” As regards the pre-
sent war, which has got beyond even
Dillon’s fountain-pen, he proposes an all-
round return to “as you were” in territorial
government; but when Belgium and France
are cleared of the invader Dillon will insist
on “a disbanding of the German and Aus-
trian armies. and navies.” Also he will
demand an indemnity from Germany to-
wards rebuilding in Belgium and Poland,
and an indemnity from France for a like
purpose in Alsace-Lorraine. But no pay-
ments of indemnity towards the cost of the
war! Finally Dillon, on behalf of the
Peace Society, will insist on “free trade
between belligerent countries,” so that Aus-
tralia may be rewarded for its belligerency
by a flood of goods made in Germany and
elsewhere. But Dillon missed one point:
Turkey, of course, must also be compen-
sated for any damage done to its civilisation,
and its rulers must be permitted to go on
ruling as before. The first qualification for
a Peace party is surely a constitutional in-
ability to see a joke.

“Full Astern” : Last Anniversary Regatta
day the skipper of a Sydney ferry steamer
was threading his way through the mos-
quito fleet and at the same time trying to
keep to the holiday time-table. During the
process he collided with a sailing-boat, whose
movements had been hidden. For this he
was towed before a magistrate a few days
ago, and, despite the obvious extenuating
circumstances, was fined £lO. The Court
said it had no option, since, according to
the Act, a steamship shall keep out of the
way of a sailing vessel. The trouble is that
the Act was passed for vessels on the high
seas, where there is unlimited room; but, as
there is only one Act, it applies also to
traffic on crowded, narrow harbors. I sub-
mit that it is up to the Harbor Trust to
make some sort of a local rule imposing a
duty on boats and the like craft to keep a
clear track. After all, for the tens who use
boats thousands use the ferries, and as the
law stands a crowded ferry can be delayed
unreasonably by any fool in a dinghy who
likes to claim the rule of the road. It is a
common thing for a boat-load of “sports”
to make a feint of crossing a steamer’s bows.
The skipper rings full speed astern, yells
“Hard a port” and “Stand by the lifeboat.”
Then, when the steamer pulls up all stand-
ing, the pleasure boat’s helm goes down and
she steers off, the crew politely putting their
fingers to their noses. The wife-beater,
milk-adulterator and backyard-thief must be
a shocking offender to earn a £lO fine, but
a worried, trying-to-do-his-best ferry-master
can expect it at his first anpearance, and
may thank his stars it isn’t £2O.

“E.L.C.W.” invites attention to a way they
have of handling the beer question in South
Africa: —
The men who ran over to draw a bead on the
Boer will remember the bright splash of color in
Natal towns made by the native ladies. On market
day in Durban they were to be seen walking in
single file down the street, each balancing a big gourd
of native beer on her head. The making of the
native concoction of mealies and kafir corn—
utswala —was for a long time quite unrestricted.
Every house brewed all it wanted and a generous
supply for the neighbors. But with civilisation
came the industrialisation of the stuff; it was brewed
for sale, and with not much regard for purity. The
towns filled up with kafirs working in shops and
houses, and as their families were in distant kraals,
the “boys” had nothing to put in their off-time with
but beer. And they put it in well. The Monday lists at
court grew weekly longer, for the trade got mixed up
with surreptitious fire-water sales and consequent
murderous outbreaks. Something had to be done.
So the Natal Leg. Ass. passed an Act enabling muni-
cipalities to brew and sell kafir beer and to sool
-off* the native-woman manufacturer. Durban adopted
the Act (1908), and with such success that other
towns fell in line, and no«r Pietermaritzburg, New-
castle, Ladysmith, Vryheidand Grey town all run
municipal beer halls. Durban makes a nice little
profit of £25,000 per annum on this side-line alone.
The Act requires that the profits made by the trade
in kafir beer shall be earmarked for the service of
the natives, and it goes for hospitals, schools, etc.,
for their behoof. In the Transvaal the municipalisa-
tion of the liquor trade is also permissible. But
the Transvaal never got any forrarder, although the
Rand, with its quarter of a million native workers
—anything from 50 to 75 per cent, are drunk on
Monday—wants a municipalisation scheme badly, one
of the best features of such control being that it
does ensure the supply of decent stuff, and prevents
the maddening of men by the serving of mixtures of
Dop Doctor, with a little methylated spirit and vitriol
to make them bite.

A further instance of the untidiness of
Victoria’s. Price of Foods Board is afforded
by its “injurious differentiation” between
butter and wheat. At present, when the
farmer hasn’t any wheat to sell, having long
since sold it to millers or large holders, he
is allowed by the Board to charge any old
price he can get—that is, he could charge
it if he had any. On the other hand, the
butter grower has butter to sell, though
it is costing him much more than it usually
does ; so the Board fixes the price for him
at Is. 2d. —which is rather less than usual
at this time of year. Furthermore, when
the precious Board formerly “regulated”
wheat, it also fixed the retail price of bread ;
but though it has settled the wholesale price
of butter at the aforementioned Is. 2d., it
has said nothing to the retailer, who is
charging Is. 6d. and Is. Bd. for the same
article. The unlovely point about this dif-
ferentiation is that nine-tenths of the wheat
in the State, other than seed, is in the hands
of large holders ; whereas nine-tenths of the
butter which is being produced and sold
to-day belongs to small farmers and their
co-operative societies. In studying the in-
terests of consumers and producers and
stamping on those of middlemen. Peacock’s
Foods Board is simply a marvel.
While the N. S. Wales Government has
been fluting about its devotion to the “cheap
loaf,” and sacrificing the cocky as a burnt-
offering on the shrine of economy, and pro-
claiming its readiness to crucify the “big
buyers” (who were at once surreptitiously
given nice, easy jobs on a fat commission),
great silence broods over everything con-
nected with the price of beef. Acting on the
representations of the British Government, a
measure was passed by the N.S.W. Assem-
bly a few weeks ago giving the Ministry
authority to acquire meat required for the
War Office. This provides that for any meat
so commandeered a fair market price should
be paid. The immediate result was the
bringing of many heads very close together,
and the consummation of “an arrangement”;
and, finally, instead of paying the indivi-
duals who hold beef in store the current
market rate, the Government has rushed in
and handed out l|d. per lb. more for meat
than it was being sold for to local butchers.
The deal covers 30,000 quarters; and no
valid explanation of the liberality with an
extra £30.000 of another Government’s
money has been made. Obviously if there is
now a huge buyer in the market ready to pay
more for meat than it has been locally sold
for, the consequence will be to push up the
already high price the Sydney citizen has
to pay for the steak and sirloin which are
wearing down his teeth to the gums.. The
anxiety of Holman and his henchman,
Hall, to save the aforesaid citizen sixpence
a week on his bread bill, and their apparent
disregard of the cost of meat, would in the
case of any other set of men call for ex-
planation. But with Holman away on the
usual holiday and Hall orating in S. Aus.
about the cost of living, the whole weary
business is just passed with a shrug of the
shoulders in the old way.

Earle Hermann, who was sent up the
other day by a Sydney Judge for three years,
was mainly one of the by-products of the
boom in P.N.’s and lOU’s. An adven-
turer, backed by 'great audacity, he
arrived in Sydney from South Africa
and felt his feet just when the reck-
less public borrowing was getting into
full blast. Dropping into the swim, he set
up in business as a financier, agent, promoter
of companies, broker, and all that sort of
thing, in offices of great pomp and impres-
siveness. For a time the man’s success was
remarkable and his profits (on paper) dazz-
ling. All manner of people were his clients;
he became known to everyone as a kind of
financial wizard. But as had been the case
with many such a one before, and will hap-
pen often enough again, Hermann was
cooked as soon as the boom began to totter.
Cash became short and loans difficult to
negotiate; the fraudulent p.n. and the meet-
ing of creditors followed ; finally the dock and
charges of forgery and uttering and the
desperate defence of insanity.. Using the
word in the loose way, it is perhaps true
that Hermann was mad ; but such madness
comes to every reckless gambler who fails to
back a winner. If such evidence as was
tendered had been successfully advanced, no
offender charged with embezzlement or for-
gery, or indeed any other crime, need have
been again convicted in N. S. Wales. The
way out would have been made easy. Still
it had its uses, this yarn about cerebral
irresponsibility ;■ for the jury acquitted on
the charge of forgery, convicting only of
uttering. All that can be said is that
Hermann was fortunate in his counsel, and
in his judge and jury; and viewed from the
standpoint of moral guiltiness, the repara-
tion asked of him by the Law makes the
majority of sentences passed in the same
Court look brutal.

service of the country, members on both sides, taking
a practical, business-like view of the subject, want

With Italy getting ready for mobilisation,
Itoumania edging closer to her guns each
day, and the Bulgars swung intj line along
their southern frontier, the position in
Southern Europe is for the moment one of
tense uncertainty. Should all these people
come into the fray, the Turk will be pricked
by a hostile bayonet on every side; and
unless Allah comes along at the last
minute and spirits him aloft to sit on
Mahomet’s coffin, it looks a!s if the aggre-
gate Unspeakable is going to get seriously hurt.
On the northern battle lines the position
remains practically the same, although it
seems as if the Russians were at last break-
ing through into Hungary and establishing a
permanent ascendancy in that field of opera-
tions. In Flanders all parties are pulling
themselves together for another effort. The
losses at Neuve Chapelle indicate how gigan-
tic the struggle for mastery ' among the
trenches and barbed-wire entanglements is
going to be. The hope that. the war might
be quickly terminated —that is, within a few
months—finds little justification anywhere.
Its duration will depend entirely upon the
number of men the Allies can bring to bear
against the resisting Hun; and as this objec-
tionable person knows a lot about the art
and practice of scrap, the turning of men
into soldiers with the greatest possible
degree of speed must now and until the con-
flict is over be the main objective of every
belligerent nation. The time for singing “Te
Deum” is as far away as Tipperary; but the
time for every available man to prepare to
take his place in the ranks is with us now.
To break the German attack is alone a
mighty task. To overcome a Germany, re-
sisting invasion will require the serious
attention of all hands and the cook.
The defeat of the Peake Government in S.
Aus. on Saturday means less than usual.
No big question of policy was at stake.
Uncommonly sensible concerning most things.
S. Aus. has just about persuaded itself that
there isn’t much to choose between parties,
however much there may be between men.
Hence, since a large proportion of the
people are not tied tight to either crowd, and
since neither crowd performs up to its pro-
mise, the State has more or less got into the
habit of kicking out its Government every
time it goes to the poll. Had Labor been in
office it probably would now. have felt the.
boot; as it happens, the accidental Peake
was there, so he suffers.. Crawford
Vaughan, who will succeed him, is untried
as a Premier. But he has large capacity :
and he has a solid following. In both of
these respects he has an advantage over his
predecessor, who was merely a time-server,
leading a thing of shreds and patches.
According to the present look of the plebis-
cite in S. Aus., the number o$ people who
want to see Bung put up the shutters at o
o’clock is considerably greater than the tally
of citizens desirous of keeping the pump at
work until 11; and soon the convivial souls
of Adelaide will have to either do without
wassail o’ nights or rush round during busi-
ness hours and capture the flowing bowl in
a bottle. Whether they will by this method
continue to capture as much as before re-
mains to be seen ; but if they try hard enough
the probabilities are that they may succeed
and keg-parties will become fashionable.
“Dn": I see that Julius Ashton has
written Rodin, the great French sculptor, to
the effect that, if he have any more precious
stones like those he lately bestowed upon
John Bull, Sydney will be glad to house
one of them. It may be interesting
to artistic Australians to know that Aus-
tralia a very few years ago had an oppor-
tunity of securing several magnificent
Rodins, or its pick of the bunch, which in-
cluded replicas of some of the sculptor’s
finest groups, at ridiculously low rates.
Ambrose Patterson, the Vic. artist, coming
from Europe, was commissioned to find
buyers for these replicas; and although he
knew he had a good thing and hawked it
assiduously, not a single buyer here, public
or private, would give the goods as much
attention as would have been bestowed upon
the samples had Fatterson been trading in
soap.

There isn’t much excuse for the manager
who finds Australia a rocky road. One of
this indebted country’s great natural
resources is a supply of feminine youth and
beauty; and, so long as a manager remem-
bers to tap it occasionally and make up a
panto, company, he can revive his fortunes
and pick up the money he didn’t make with
Bernard Jaw or “Beyond the Beyond,” the
intense society problem play that crawled for
1200 nights' in London and New York.
These theatrical by-products are really only
meant to keep playgoers alive and managers
amused between pantos. “Sinbad the Sailor,”
COMEDIAN RICKABY AT THE
BRISBANE EMPIRE.
Rickaby (1) curses Carrots (2), sings “PC
49” (3) a nd cracks a joke: “I went in to a
swagger hotel this morning to get a cigar—
when some damned fool trod on my fingers."
the Bilanderson effort now running at Syd-
ney Adelphi, makes its progress mostly on
the shapely limbs of girls who are too
numerous to mention on the programme.
They sing any old thing with vim, and dance
joyously till the lights go out and it is time
for the two false-alarm sailor humorists to
totter on and howl unconvincingly in each
other’s faces. Or if the girls are not chased
off by them, it is the female old man of the
sea who obtrudes. These three hard-work-
ing characters should not be administered to
any audience without chloroform. The
amazing Maggie Moore wears so youthful a
face as Mrs. Sinbad that the usual insulting
panto, gags rebound from the greasepaint-
armor and fall flat. She has more real
vivacity, not to mention humor, than many
a modern ragtime lady, and her voice is still
sweet and true. Broadly speaking, she really
justifies the enthusiasm of the older genera-
tion of playgoers. Olive Marston, who wears
the principal tights, curves generously, and
obliges with a nigger serenade and shuffle
in the forgotten pre-ragtime manner. Only
an old practitioner could do it. Harry
Burgess, late of “The Grafters” co., is gen-
uinely amusing with legs and face, and the
stage manners and singing voice of Rita
Renas are very soothing. The humorous
acrobatic interludes of Morris and Momo
earn honorable mention; likewise the sing-
ing of John Ralston. But the main prop of
the show is Youth —which, in addition to
the usual modest wage, asks only a pretty
dress and music to set its eager feet going.

Julius Knight will cease to apply for his
Royal Divorce after to-night (Thursday). On
Saturday evening “The Lifeguardsman” is
due at Sydney Royal; and when his spurs
and military haberdashery vanish _ from the
scene, there will be no more Knight for a
long while. The production, in fact, is
billed as a farewell; but the chances are
that it is just the usual so-long-for-the-
present.
** * *
The glittering Cinderella panto, is settling
down nicely at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, and
sorting out its elegant legs. Barry Lupino,
who has sworn off red nose-paint for the
production, emerges successfully from the
terrific voluntary test, and the only fault of
the show seems to be that there is too much
of it. But, then, isn’t every good panto,
expected to suffer from obesity ?
Hs * * *
Despite the absence of the official Starr,
“Bought and P_aid For,” with Sylvia Bremer
in the big part, continues at Sydney Cri-
terion to provide good United States melo-
drama at the usual prices. A second con-
sideration of William Harrigan and Dorothy
Davies leads this paper to credit them with
making the fourth act the most interesting
of the play. Harrington’s part, that of
James Gilley, fits admirably ; he manages to
make this blatapt specimen of New York
clurkdom human as well as humorous. Dor-
othy Davies who, on the first night, was
held to be neither good nor bad, is now given
the benefit of the doubt, and is informed that
she is more good than bad in a part that
requires some acting. If this critic went a
third time, it is quite possible that he would
arrive in such a mellow mood that he would
regard Dorothy Davies as a delightful artist.
* * # *
The first annual meeting of subscribers to
the Sydney Repertory Dramatic Society,
held in the Repertory Theatre last Thurs-
day evening, was not as dreary as most
annual meetings. For two one-act plays
were performed, “The Human Document,”
an Australian play by Montague Grover, and
“Little Sloe Eyes,” a Cockney play by
Frederick Ward. Grover’s play dealt
dramatically with “crook” life, and, despite
its amateur cast, stood out as an interesting
and cleverly-constructed bit of stage realism,
worthy of resurrection for the Australian
public. Gilburt Howell, the producer, and
Dr. Foster were consistent in their respect-
ive parts of a dealer in stolen goods and a
detective; but Miss Dowland failed to grasp
in its entirety the character of the dealer’s
“ruined” daughter. “Little Sloe Eyes”
had the advantage of production by profes-
sional actors, and Frederick Ward and Eily
Maly on brought out fully the theatric value
of a rather slight playlet, though in stressing
the pathos Miss Malyon became sometimes
inaudible.
** * *
The Irish Pipers, under the Tait manage-
ment, opened their two nights’ season in the
Sydney Town Hall on Saturday. Murphy
claims that his wind-bags are the daddies of
their species, and that the first Mackenzie
who piped on his cold Caledonian, hilltops is a
mushroom compared with the minstrels who
skirled as Brian Boru stuck a shamrock in
his belt and marched into battle at Clontarf.
The Irish-Australian Pipers are led by pipe-
major Yeale, a broth of a boy supported by
a fine pair of calves. At the first
concert, the pipes, the best part of
the company’s outfit, gave forth such
stirring shrieks, notably in the War Pipe
Duet, that an Emerald Isle audience simply
broke out into a volcano of applause. Of
the vocalists, a young soprano. Vera Carew,
may be dismissed with a caution against gasp-
ing for breath in unexpected places; and
the other soloists and the dancers were
better in intention than performance.
