Glory be to St. Pathrick, Allen Doone will
be playing at the Palace from Saturday
night onward. “Barry of Barrymore” will
open the ball.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli is manufacturing amuse-
ment on much the same lines as last week.
The fresh interest of the present bill is sup-
plied by Thorne and Leopold, a mixed double
whose burlesque spar is good.. Capable
strangers are being earnestly invited to fall
into Odiva’s tank, and the rest of the show
is supplied by Geo. Mozart (mimic), Chester
Kingston (contortionist) and other workers
in the good cause of cheerfulness.
* *
Wirths’ tented field at Prince Alfred Park
(Sydney) is drawing its tramloads. The
Religious Editor took his infant last week,
and the four-year-old stopped dead on the
grassy threshold of the menagerie. and
howled bitterly to be taken back again—a
splendid tribute to the expensive realism of
the show, which looks like the contents of
several picture-books. There had previously
been a horrible misunderstanding before the
bath-cage of the blood-sweating hippo, when
that remarkable animal gladly opened its
hinged head to the full extent to receive
one wretched peanut from a generous
patron. The most frenzied explanations and
guarantees were required before peace was
restored and it was possible to report busi-
ness as usual.

Kellerman, will disport on the Palace (Syd-
ney) film-screen for the last time to-night
(Thursday). On Good Friday there will be
two displays of '‘From Manger to Cross.”
If Archgaiters Wright had been desirous of
adding some interest to his Easter pastoral,
he would have recommended his flock to get
in a supply of peanuts and go along to, see
this canned miracle.

** * *
“High Jinks,” having served its Sydney
turn, came to Her Majesty’s (Melb.) last
Saturday, and got all the welcome it de-
served. The crude title of this broad cari-
cature of French farcical comedy fully
explains the case, so the statement that it
is a mere musical jollity seems unnecessary.
As regards the names of author and com-
poser, the programme preserves a dead
silence, but their joint address can be sur-
mised —they probably have their workshop
in the caverns of memory. The plot is a
farrago of familiar social indiscretions, re-
lieved by the incredible simplicity of a
matron and a maid, the maid being an art-
less serio-chronic who has strained her
throat whilst warbling at the Folies Ber-
geres. The various indiscreet persons are
married or otherwise when in Paris, but
when they go to a seaside pub they mostly
get registered as the wrong husband or
wife, and their only means of escape from
the Divorce Court is by sniffing a perfume
which makes everybody joyful for five
minutes. But the perfume is inconsistent in
its influence ; hence, whereas Dorothy Brun-
ton bursts into romantic song after sniffing
it, others begin to dance, whilst Florence
Vie, the naughty stout lady of the piece,
wants to moisten her happiness with more
champagne. It is all very frivolous and
very remindful of French farces in general.
Also at its best the music is rather a noisy
jumble of echoes of bygone strains, the air
which falls to festive Dorothy being sugges-
tive of “The Chocolate Soldier.” At its
worst it is tattered ragtime.
* * * *
None of the principals, bar Florence Vie
and Paul Plunket, are naturally suited to
their characters; but Fisher Rawlins, Ger-
trude Glyn and Marie Eaton fill their places
fairly. Dorothy Brunton is an engaging
charmer as usual, and Workman is a droll
comedian disguised as a devoted lover. The
orchestra performs its monotonous duties
with undue vigor in some cases, and the
chorus lives a strenuous life on account of
frequent changes of costume. All the remain-
ing possibilities of concerted gesture and
movement are exhausted by the hard-worked
choristers. No matter how much a week
they get for their long series of drill forma-
tions and Sandow exercises, these automatic
auxiliaries are not overpaid. Finally, the
painters’ work is picturesque, and little
Novotna and Victor Lauschman contribute
a charming novelty dance to the third act.

“ Camille” is having a six-nights’ run at
Melbourne Princess’s, with Vera Remee as
the consumptive Flossie and George Cross as
her doting boy. Miss Remee is not parti-
cularly comfortable. Some of the moods of
the character are beyond the lady’s little
gallery of mechanical emotions, while her
voice is always rather metallic even in its
most wooing moments. As Armand, George
Cross is as tender and melting as may be,
and is about the only person in the piece who
employs a good clothes-builder to point a
fashion, and adorn a tale. Ronald Riley’s
Comte de Varville belongs to the languid
school of Doncherknow, and Ward Lyons, as
Duval, senior, is gruffly emphatic in present-
ing the old gent.’s point of view. Frank
Neil (Gaston) and Maurice Nodin (Gustav)
are more convincing in a small way than
Miss Robertson (Nannie) and Miss Bow-
man (Madame Prudence), whereof the last-

At Melb. Royal there will be no Easter
change of bill. “Seven Keys to Baldpate”
is only in its third week, so Niblo will go
on with his story of the amazing spoof that
spoofs himself and the audience.
* * * *
Melb. King’s is to be reopened next Satur-
day by a syndicate described as Bill Adams,
Ltd., and the enterprising Alf Lumsden will
duly present his “Bill” in farcical-comedy
form. A new Australian comedy from a
comparative novice in stagecraft, suggested
by the late Snazelle’s somewhat-forgotten
yarn of Bill Adams, the Hero of Waterloo,
is, anyhow, a plucky experiment. But the
King’s is the home of plucky experiments,
successful and otherwise, and public curi-
osity will give “Bill Adams” a fair chance
to make good. Lumsden has secured a strong
eo.—Herbert Ford, Charles Brown (in the
name part), Harry Paulton, jun., Maggie
Knight, Ethel Buckley and three others.
* * * *
The second edition of The Follies, which
consists partly of the first edition over again,
and includes a fairly bright travesty of
“Bought and Paid For,” continues to be the
backbone of Melb. Tivoli programme. James
J. Corbett now tells his gentle anecdotes of
his stoushing days in the first part, and
Smith and Nielson, with a vaudeville turn,
are the latest arrivals. Also, Miss Clerc, the
toe-dancer, who arrived a week earlier, is
still there.
** * *
At the Auditorium the Royal Strollers
have caught on, as was expected. On Saturday
they changed their songs and dances, but the
first part concluded as before with Sydney
James’s ventriloquial manipulation—if the
term is permissible—of Little Billy, the
bloke who appears almost human in all
respects save his revolving neck and trucu-
lent smile. The new burlesque, in succession
to “Faust,” is “Our Picture Palace,” which
terminates to the strains of Gor’save.
& % *
Advts.. of . “The Naval War” which Arthur
Russell is introducing to Melbourne notice
at the Auditorium next Saturday lay stress
upon the fact that it is not a cinematograph
show or a panorama or any old thing of a
kindred character. A scenic background,
painted by Grist, is the only fixed portion
of the outfit; for the rest, the “millions of
gallons” of water are subject to least move-
ment. “The Naval War” is a matter of model
battleships, mimic forts and spectacular dis-
turbances that will soon make a loud and
prosperous noise outside, as well as inside,
the building when the novelty comes to light.

The Robert Parker concerts at Melb. Town
Hall, beginning on Easter Saturday, are to
be supported by Mrs. Carmen Harris, whose
mezzo-soprano has been uplifted in opera
abroad since Melbourne last heard it, and
Miss Scapine, a young violinist. Parker
himself is well remembered as the tall and
tuneful basso of the visiting Quinlan opera
companies, and the soul of the musical world
awaits his coming.

At Melbourne Bijou the Stanley McKay
Panto. Co. is now leading a moderately fes-
tive existence in the home-made extrava-
gance, “Old Mother Hubbard.” Miss Essie
Jennings endows the part of principal boy
with considerable shapeliness and a small,
sweet voicewhile the only other notable
entertainer is the drily humorous James
Gerald in the role of Pansy Hubbard. In
the first part of the bill, Tom Lesso is re-
appearing in a fresh juggling act, and Arm-
strong and Howarth, sketch artists, have
broken out in another rash experiment
entitled “A Foolish Fool.”
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Masetto”: Otto Sobell, the vocal expert
who, according to Marshall Hall, is going
to do big things at Melb. Conservatorium,
is ostensibly an importation from the Cold
Country. He certainly lived there for some
years, but Adelaide held his people 30 years
back, his father being its best-known ama-
teur, Otto Fischer. A sister—Minna—-mar-
ried actor Herbert Flemming, and on
his death went before the registrar with
G. H. Clutsam, the song-writer, who now
lives in London.
“THE STROLLERS.”
A musical director in Stroller Land.
“Pinafore” is funnier than it ever was.
The satirical song of the patriot sailor, who
might have been a Rooshun, etc.-, but re-
mained an Englishman, is taken quite
seriously by its Melbourne audience, which
overlooks the joke and gives the song a
treble encore. Probably the audience would
give 10 encores to the Rule Britannia tab-
leau which used to cap the absurdity of
the pirates of Penzance laying down
their cutlasses and pistols when the ser-
geant of police bids them surrender in the
name of their Gracious Sovereign; but the
tableau is omitted in the performance of the
latest Gil.-Sul. Co. Quite a lot of people
took it seriously even in the old days. The
question is: How many of Gilbert’s satires
and ironies do they not take seriously?
“Hamer”: There are rumblings of a com-
ing earthquake in the theatres. The big
city managements, with one or two excep-
tions, have always been hostile to the
unions. In fact, they and their friends
smashed the Actors’ Union, and founded in
its place an association with objects that
are not unionistic. The prospects of also
smashing such unions as the Musicians’ and
Theatrical Employees’ are remote; never-
theless the fight goes on, and the recent can-
cellation of the State registration of the
former union was followed by an attempt
to form a new musicians’ union. It was,
in fact, mentioned that the union had been
formed, and intended to apply for regis-
tration ; but as only a dozen musicians have
shown any interest in it (five of them as
watch-dogs on behalf of the established
union) its prospects, like those of the Crown
Prince of Sausagedom, are not bright. The
allegation has been made that one firm was
willing to finance the new organisation and
help it on its way. As a result, there has
been much friction, many unmusical trade
unions declaring that if the theatre man-
agements want fight they can have a whole
cargo of it. These are not the brightest of
iu?n S tbe show business, and the Trades
Liall potentates reckon that the withdrawal
ot a large percentage of patronage from
h (>s ti hi in an <i ge m cuts and its transfer to
those theatres which display no hostility to
the unions would be a mighty argument.
A MIXED VIEW OF THE WAR
IN SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
“Screen’’: Two new picture theatres are
on the point of opening in Bourke-street,
Melbourne—Hoyt’s and the Victoria. They
challenge each other for size and beauty,
although neither has quite the sumptuous
decoration that Williams bestowed on the
Melba and the next-door Britannia. St.
George’s Hall has disappeared in Hoyt’s new
“palace,” which even towers above the
Theatre Royal adjoining. The Victoria re-
places Parer’s Restaurant, but the liquor
license is kept alive in a small way, which
gives it rather a pull over other “movies.”
The only other picture house with a liquor
license is the unlucky National Amphi-
theatre, built by Brennan and Co., at the
east end of Bourke-street. It is occupied
at present by Hoyt; and other picture people
will succeed him there, without brilliant
hopes. The Victoria will be conducted by
Ihe former lessee of the Star Pictures, at
the Waxworks; but the Star will go on
under new management. Thus Bourke-
street, in a week or so, will have seven big
picture shows every night—the Gaiety,
Melba, Britannia, Hoyt’s, Victoria, Star and
National. Can Bourke-street stand it? And
can the picture supply stand it? Already
the associated houses are not able to
keep to their rule of complete difference in
every place.
From London: Passed out, at 43, Louis
Meyer Moniat —known as Louis Meyer—
artist and journalist turned theatrical man-
ager. It is only five years ago that he began
speculating in plays. His one big prize,
“The Glad Eye,” was an adaptation of a
French farce. Arthur Bourchier had
acquired the English rights, but afterwards,
losing faith in it, sold the thing to Louis
Meyer for a trifle. Meyer gave it a trial
trip in the provinces with poor results, and
came to the conclusion that Bourchier was
right. A friend, however, prevailed upon
him to give it a chance in London. It
caught on immediately, was attacked by the
wowsers, and ran for a year. A native of
Birmingham, Meyer came up to London
early, began press work, became one of the
proprietors of London Opinion, and after
his acquisition of “The Glad Eye” money-
spinner, went in for horse-racing and other
extravagant and exciting recreations, which
played the deuce with his never xobust
health, and the end came that way.
“0.K.”: Faulkner Smith in Melbourne
is running a series of monthly “national”
concerts, which began, of course, with an
all-Irish entertainment. The next will be
all-Scotch, and then Wales will get a look
in, presumably. After which the platform
will be clear for an Anglo-Belgian or Franco-
Russian concert. The word “national” in
concert business hereabout rarely is asso-
ciated with the word “English,” and never
with “Australian.” What’s the good of foist-
ing upon this country the sort of “nation-
alism” expressed in ballads of dead and gone
racial animosities, such as “Scots Wha Hae”
or “Blue Bonnets over the Border”?
“Sketch” (from London) : Carrie Haase,
who had one of the longest parts in “The
Dynasts” at the Kingsway Theatre, is tak-
ing a holiday after being about 10 years
away from Australia, and leaves for the
other side shortly. She was the original
Anne in the provincial tour of Shaw’s “Man
and Superman,” and as an understudy got
her first chance when she acted Julia Nielson
roles in London.
Beaumont Smith has written a Maori
play which he threatens to try on the in-
habitants shortly per medium of his co. at
present in the Islands playing “The Glad
Eye,” etc. Incidentally, he has announced
the name of the children’s play which he
recently bought from Arthur Adams—(long
breath here) —“Snookey-Ookums.”
From Lincoln Springfield, editor of Lon-
don Opinion :
“J.F.H.’’ (B. 24/12/’l4) states that Mark Ham-
bourg has got £5OO damages from us for calling
him a German. Permit me to put you wise. It
was not London Opinion, but a deadly rival, of not
half our excellence, which trod in this particular
puddle. As I have been in the habit of meeting
Mark at the Club poker table, I was well informed
about his Russian birth, his British enthusiasms,
and his Satanic luck; and the moral that seems to
stick out here is the advisability of editors playing
poker as often as possible.
Novelist H. G. Wells, a recent recruit to
Protection, makes confession :
The writer is a Liberal and a Socialist, and thus
far has done his best to oppose tariff reform. He
has no fanatical belief in Freetrade. No Socialist
has. Since all Socialists believe in the fullest assist-
ance and control of industry by the State, it’would
be absurd for them to repudiate the idea of con-
trolling exports and imports.... I am certain that
in the new view of the need of Protection, I do not
think alone. A situation has arisen in which the
almost immediate adoption of Protection is essential
to our national security..
Heine knew his countrymen and saw a
long way, but not far enough. He wrote
To a certain degree Christianity softened the
brutal battle-ardor of the Germans. Yet it did not
destroy the instinct; and when the Cross, the talis-
man that holds it in check, is broken there will be
a new eruption of ferocity. Then Thor will come
forth with his gigantic hammer and smash the Gothic
cathedrals into ruins.
But Thor is not content merely with
smashing cathedrals. He is raining his
blows on the farms and homes of people
whose country he undertook to protect.
He is burning libraries, looting the
homes of niillionaire and workman alike,
bombarding nursemaids and babies, dropping
bombs on old-age pensioners, and trying to
blow up hospital-ships. Men are not im-
pelled to this sort of thing by ardor of
battle. The motive force is just the plain
devilishness of the ape.
“Form Fours” : There is a lot of patriot-
ism running to waste just now. One recent
dark night our platoon (grand old word
“platoon”!) of grave suburban rifle-club-
men, mostly stiff in the joints and rotund
in the middle, listened to a drill instructor
while he explained the best way to dodge
shrapnel when hotly charging a position.
There wasn’t one of the whole lot of us who
didn’t know quite well that it was all pure
guff; yet there wasn’t a ripple on the face of
any of the 50 humbugs. What we really did
need was a hint or two on how to advance
on wasteful State Governments that continue
to fire away borrowed cash as if there were
no shortage of golden ammunition. If the
“Home Guard” would storm Holman’s
treasury and lock up the book of blank
P.N.’s, it would be doing good work that
lies ready to its hand.
“Huffin Guilin”: Re Geo. Newman, of
Blenheim (M.L.), and codching records. (B.
18/3/T5). “Jimmy” Lyall drove Cobb and
Co.’s big coach between Hamilton and
Harrow (Vie.). 60 miles, six days a week,
from 1872 to 1896, or thereabouts —450,000
miles in all. Then he started on his own
account, and has been driving his teams
in Western Vic. ever since. His son, “young
Jimmy,” has helped to tot up the fam-,
ily’s mileage, which is now well over
1,000,000,' and going strong.
From the ruins of a motor-car which
bounded off the road near Manly (Sydney)
on Sunday, George Redmond, news editor
of Sydney Sun, was borne away dead, and
“Snowy” Baker, director of the many Sta-
diums, was carried, badly battered, to the
nearest hospital for inspection and repair.
Redmond, a Melbourne University man, moved
to Sydney about three years ago from the
Argus. His tragic passing at 37 years of
age will be deplored by a very wide circle
of friends.
The most interesting veteran at the recent
Melbourne gathering of veterans was not
the old lady of 93 from Sale, nor Mrs. Glad-
stone (86), who sang a “Hunting Song,”
but Carr Boyd, the tough old bushman who
in the early UO’s followed the track of
Burke and Wills. The still-ebullient old
—“Potjostler” of scores of ancient
Bulletin pars.—got the most excited when
talking about the Victoria Desert. “Desert!”
he snorted. “Desert! Why, it’s a lovelv
land, where even the niggers get fat and
sleek and long-haired.”
“Hamer”: The principle of One Big
Union is being strenuously preached by the
Sydney Worker, which is an indication that
the A.W.U. is intent upon bringing the
thing about. Already many minor unions
have sought amalgamation with the big fel-
low and have been eagerly admitted, and if
it announced that it was open to all-comers
it would no doubt rope in thousands who
already are members of other societies. But
it shows no desire to make a quarrel that
way with the different craft unions, its
desire, being to get the members of each
organisation to come over as a body. The
chief hostility comes from the officials of
the smaller concerns, who don’t like the risk
of losing their small glories and, in some
cases, emoluments. The vice-president of
the Tinkers’ Union, for instance, would de- ■
fend his title with his wife’s blood. It is
his one distinction, and as long as he holds
it he has a vague hope of reaching the
Upper House. He may howl against heredi-
tary titles and knighthoods as meaningless
things, but his own vice-presidency is quite
a different proposition. For all that, the
way union after union, at one time un-
friendly to the idea, have sought for amal-
gamation, shows that the One Big Union
idea is growing, and the days of small
organisations appear to be numbered.
AN OLD AIR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What summer night do you recall to me?
What memories—what tears?
Was it that voyage across a moonlit sea ?
Each note an old wound sears.
What friend now lost to me was near when you
Called to my heart “Awake! ”
And wove a thread of sound the minutes through
That Death alone can break ?
Strange is the magic of old haunting airs
With minstrel-cunning wrought
That send a fairy-call through Life’s affairs,
To wake the sleeping thought.
Veiled figures throng and beckon in my dream ;
Dear eyes look into mine,
While I, bewildered, watch the passing stream
With misty eyes a-shine.
It rends my heart. Yet I am ever loath
To let the music cease.
Honey it brings and wormwood ; they are both
Blended in draughts of peace.
What joy to listen on Thoughts’ borderland
To echoes sweet and far !
To trace old footprints dimly in the sand,
And see again Love’s star!
Somewhere between To-day and Long Ago
Are dreams I cannot share,
Nor find, till to my heart, insistent, low,
Steals some belovGd air.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

IF BILIOUS OR CONSTIPATED TAKE
“CALIFORNIA SYRUP OF FIGS.
Harmless “fruit” laxative cleanses
stomach, liver and bowels without
griping or discomfort.
An acid stomach, clogged with fermenting,
ill-digested food, means that the liver is not
doing its duty. Nature provided ripe fruit
juices to keep the liver up to its work.
“California Syrup of Figs” is a deliciously
refreshing fruit laxative which is always in
season. You can keep it at hand, and it
will be an unfailing friend whenever the
tongue is coated, bowels sluggish, breath
foul, throat dry or sore, stomach full of
wind, and head hot and aching. You can
take a dose whenever it is needed, a,s safely
as you could eat fresh fruit. In a short

SOCIETY
The long argument between employers and
employed on the question of day baking in
Sydney has ended at last in favor of that
plan. There have been about a dozen
threats of strike and a score of conferences
over the matter; and some time ago a
shandy-gaff agreement was fixed up under
which work was to commence at 2 p.m.
After trying this for a while, the master
bakers declared that the name of the day-
baking idea was dough. The men still said
it was cake, and wanted more of it. Now
the employers have agreed to 10 a.m. as
starting-time, which means real day baking;
so the controversy ends, for the present at
any rate.
THE MEDICAL BAPTISM.
JACKY (to vaccinating doctor): “You christ'n this pfella, no catchit smallpox, eh?"
Echoes of the big waterside strike that hit
Maoriland in the exports a year ago still
rumble round Auckland province. One echo
is about the bill. When trouble started
Massey consulted the Farmers’ Union, and
the Auckland branch thereof scoured the
Waikato turnip paddocks and byres for
special constables, who were to be delivered
in the city freight paid. City members of the
union who farm in ledgers and depasture on
cash-books undertook to supply the needed
pieces of 4x 2 ironbark to bash the wharf-
lumper with, and patriotic merchants under-
took in a spasm of recklessness to “pay the
bill” when the bashing was over. So at
much expense the country hurled forward
regiments of mounted farmers, who joyously
ran up gigantic bills for horse-feed, trans-
portation, badges, beer and getting their
whiskers cut. When the disturbance ended
Massey was approached with the bill, but
replied with a horrible silence. As the mer-
chants couldn’t find their memory, and had
grown deaf in both ears, the farmers’ union
woke up in the soup, and after long immer-
sion whispered from the tureen to the raging
creditors that the country centres would pay.
So the country centres got bills in cartloads ;
but still there’s nothing doing, and the
anguished shopkeepers are saying most un-
kind things about farmers’ unions. Also,
many farmers who lent horses to the special
constables have never seen the nags again,
and are beginning to go sour on the organisa-
tion. Altogether, it seems to be one thing to
settle a strike with biff and another thing to
pay the bill.
* * * *
The murder of the old grazier Escott at
Black Springs (S.A.) had features which
stirred Adelaide to its boots. One of the
first items of intelligence sent in was that
Escott had been slain while reading his
prayer-book. This was given great promi-
nence in the evening editions; and the morn-
ing dailies, scenting possibilities, rose mag-
nificently to the occasion. Special reporters
were mobilised, photographers thrown into
the firing-line, process-engravers called on to
do their worst. The ’Tiser burst out on the
second day with a page of pictures—there
was a plan of the ground floor at the home-
stead ; here was the place where the victim
fell; that smudge near by was the actual
bloodstain. And to round off the picture of
gore there was a nice illustration of the
window through which the murderer fired,
and the hole made by the bullet. So far, all
was shuddersome and delicious. But the pic-
ture of the well down which it was supposed
the miscreant threw his gun was a dismal
failure. Still, even a Yankee fliekergraph
couldn’t make much of a success of a deep,
dark well; so there’s no reason for the Bony-
thons to feel downhearted.

Knibbs has a new table showing the but-
cher’s bill for last year. Australia had 159
fatal industrial accidents and 4912 suffi-
ciently severe to cause disablement for more
than 14 days, as against 185 and 5030 re-
spectively in the previous year.' N. S.
Wales had 61 of the fatal and 3238 of the
less serious accidents, the cause of Ma’s
remarkable affluence in the matter being the
prevalence of the mining, especially the coal-
mining, industry. Throughout Australia 112
of the fatalities and 3937 non-fatal serious
accidents occurred in and about mines, the
biggest toll being easily secured by the coal
business. No managing directors were
killed, or maimed, or bruised. Taking other
industries, it appears that no bookmakers
were smashed up while going to work, money-
lending was carried on with immunity from
disaster, and not a single bishop was killed
while earning his meagre daily repast.
“Dl” condoles with the Chief Sisyphus of
N.S.W.: —-
The new Chief Secretary is bent on the metamor-
phosis of Sydney. There is to be no more loitering;
no more crowding round shop windows; pedestrians
are to keep to the right—Elysium is to be laid at
the city’s feet. The citizens would feel gladder if
they had not heard such things a dozen times
before. There was a determined move once to ex-
tirpate the shop tout. It is still impossible to walk
in certain parts without being grabbed by an in-
sanitary-looking waster and implored to buy a slop
suit. Spitting on the pavements was to be checked.
Everyone spits who feels like it, and the Law
never objects. During the fashionable spitting
hours (the lunch recess and the time when the
shops close) the outsides of many city pubs are a
fair imitation of the Mazurian Lakes. The pavement
in one part of Pitt-street is the assignation place
for the city’s youth and beauty. Lovely women and
gallant men stand there by the hour, and the mere
passer-by has to fight his way through or use the
motor-infested gutter. Yet there was a time, long
passed, when this crowd was told definitely by the
police that it was Doomed. The truth is, no absurd
or evil practice is ever doomed in Sydney. For one
thing, there are too many of them. If a Chief
Secretary, hungry for reform, confined himself to
one lonely objective—say, to persuading the City
Council to keep the streets more or less sanitary—
he might hope to achieve something. He wouldn’t
succeed, of course; but at least he might hope.
Justice Gordon (N.S.W.), sitting in
Divorce, told a witness last week that all
persons, excepting the King, could be called
upon to tell all they knew about a matter
before any Court, unless it could be clearly
shown that to do so would put the witness
in peril. The witness before Gordon, J., had
been called by a husband who was petition-
ing for release from his marriage contract.
She, on the ground that he had made dis-
orderly proposals to her in her house, refused
to support his case. His Honor committed
the lady to the charge of the sheriff as a
preliminary and adjourned the Court for
half an hour so that she might consider the
position. At the end of the interval
she gave in. It wasn’t possible for her
to repeat the classic performance of the
man charged with murder who got off by
marrying the Crown’s chief and only definite
witness. Which, by the way, recalls that
his Honor apparently forgot that exception.
A wife isn’t bound to tell all she knows
about her husband, and a husband isn’t
bound to tell even half of what he knows
about his wife.
When Germany got to know about the
doings of the N. S. Wales Unnecessary
Commodities Committee it was most
annoyed. The following is a clipping from
the Frankfurt Bltjtwurst :
God punish England! Amongst the many savage
races she has bought with her money to fight us, are
the people of New South Wales, in the Continent of
Australia. She paid these people £18,000,000, dis-
tributed amongst several “States” or tribes; and,
there being no money in the country, the bribe was
readily accepted. The manner of people that these
Allies of Britain are (God punish her!) may be
gathered from the doings of a committee formed by
the white officials for the protection of the natives
by the regulation of the price of necessities, which
(owing to our successful blockade of British ports,
and the fact that this savage country depends for
nearly all its manufactured goods on imports) are
scarce. The committee has decided that news-
papers are not a necessary commodity (as of course
they are not in such a country), and that the price
of soan needs no regulation; but beer and spirits
were amongst the first things that were declared neces-
sary, prices being fixed for all the drinks of the
country. It is savages of this sort that the British
would hurl against us in their efforts to destroy
German kultur!
MILESTONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The annual row between the Anglican Archbishop
of Sydney and the Agricultural Society about open-
ing the Show on Good Friday is in progress.
The years reel by like telegraph-posts,
And ev’ry day is the same to me ;
They’re all alike as a herd of ghosts ;
New calendars shed their > leaves with glee ;
Life’s merry-go-round whirls fast and free;
Submissively to the draught I bow.
I never can tell where I should be
Until I come to the Easter Cow.
The Bishop and she are ancient foes ;
Each year the battle is joined anew
He fronts her bravely in sacred hose,
And fights the fight of the stuffy pew ;
His words are many, his vict’ries few ;
The cow is brave as a cow can be;
He only gets in a fearful stew;
But both of them tell the time to me.
Another year to the Past has flashed;
Another post to the rear has fled ;
So many more bright hopes are dashed ;
So much more grey in my nodding head—
The time is coming when I’ll be dead.
llovv many more years will Fate allow?
We’re one lap nearer our earthen bed—
The Bishop’s clinched with the Easter Cow!
N.S.W.

A scholarly Englishman sends his philo-
sophical views from peaceful Worthing, on
the coast of Britain :
We old people are living in a kind of nightmare
—a nightmare of submarines and aircraft. The
young people take it easily enough, and the way
the male youth has risen to the occasion is certainly
amazing. But I wish to God it had come after my time.
It is the last war in the old style; in fact, I doubt
whether war as we understand it will be even possi-
ble 20 years hence. Suppose a thousand or so air-
craft over London or Berlin at once. No constitu-
tion could stand it —as the old farmer said of Hell.
After all the talk about the wonders of
Modern Warfare on land and sea, it is re-
freshing to hear from an elderly observer who
thinks of this war as a comparatively old-
fashioned foolishness which will be reduced
to insignificance by the aerial hostilities of
the future.

“Billy Blue”: The S. M. Herald has
been repeating the oft-repeated old mistake
that the vine was first introduced into Aus-
tralia in the year 1815 or later, and it adds
that the point has been thoroughly investi-
gated by painstaking searchers. It may
assist the Herald’s painstaking searchers to
know that the historian Collins, who arrived
here with the first fleet, tells us that by
January 30, 1788, some ground having been
prepared near his Excellency’s house, the
plants from Rio Janeiro and the Cape of
Good Hope were safely brought on shore,
and the new settlers had the satisfaction of
seeing the grape and fig take root and
establish themselves in the new world.
They must have done well, too, for there is
on record a letter from Mrs. John McArthur,
dated March 18, 1791, in which she says:
“The grape thrives remarkably well. The
Governor sent me some bunches this season
as fine as any I ever tasted.” Then we have
one of Governor Phillip’s last despatches,
dated October 16, 1792, reporting that he
had three acres of a vineyard at the Govern-
ment House grounds at Parramatta, and
that Philip Schaffer, a free settler, had
planted out a vineyard of one acre. Schaffer
had a grant of 140 acres on the north bank
of the Parramatta River, now Subiaco. This
property was known as “The Vineyard” till
the late ’3o’s. Schaffer came as a superin-
tendent of convicts; he was an agriculturist
from Hesse, but could - not speak English,
so on March 30, 1791, lie was granted the
above-mentioned land, and became the
first vigneron of Australia. Governor
King reported, on August 7, 1803, that
he had 30 acres at Castle Hill ready to plant
and later that he had about 12,000 vines in
the first and second years’ growth.
The coming referendum in South Aus. as
to closing all bars at 11, as at present, or at
six, or any hour between that may get a
majority, has brought out a neat pictorial
joke. Adelaide hoardings are all familiar
with the whisky poster of the Scotch guest
who turns liis head away while the host,
pouring from bottle to glass, says des-
perately, “Say when, mon!” The remark
was too appropriate to local conditions to
be missed, and now an addition to the
“legend” makes the picture i-eply “Eleven
o’clock, please.”

“Chudah”: The Turk throws away not
only Constantinople, but also Palestine.
What then? Many a zealous Zionist has
visions of Palestine as a neutral independent
state, in which the Jews, with fair play to
all, would gradually become the most influ-
ential, even in point of numbers. The
Egyptian Gazette, voicing this aspiration,
writes that the Zionists have now their
grand opportunity. But they will never
carry out the ridiculous project of the ex-
tremists, to squeeze all the Jews back. For
one thing, Palestine wouldn’t hold them.
Hirsch founded the Colonisation Society with
10 millions sterling capital. Palestine was
the chief, but not the exclusive, aim; for
lamdies have been transplanted from East-
ern Europe to Argentina and elsewhere
However, the movement has done more for
1 amstine than any other place. There were
only 4000 Jews in Jerusalem in 1841 • now
7^oo0 ar< T <!o ’n°T, V 1 a total Population of
70,000. In all Palestine there are 150,000
inn nnn uarter of the whole. Of these
100,000 have come from Europe since 1887.
All Jewry would relish the Palestine focus:
yet_ some nervous Christians doubt whether
their holy places would be as safe with the
Hebrew as with the contemptuous Turk,
who says; ‘You can have your little bit of
nonsense _■ —as long as it doesn’t get in the
way of his own thefts.

“Hamer”: Shoplifting is pretty much a
habit nowadays, like betting on horses or
having afternoon-tea. And the difficulty in
dealing with the professionals with whom
the habit is a business is intensified by the
amateur efforts of some of the Yery Best.
So there is very little giving in charge. Most
shops (nearly all the big firms have private
detectives to watch for light-fingered cus-
tomers) are satisfied if they have the stolen
property returned. That in letting the thief
escape they are “compounding a felony”
seems to be generally overlooked. The mana-
ger of one big emporium who had hastily
given a customer in charge received a severe
shock the other day. He sent a representa-
tive to the court with a message that the
accused was the wife of one of the owners,
and he did not want to go on with the
charge. The indignant magistrate insisted
upon the manager attending the court, and
told him that, while he could not be forced
to give evidence on the charge he had laid,
it was_ competent for the police to proceed
on their own account and subpoena him as
a witness. There’s a harassed manager to-
day who would allow the boss’s wife to get
away with anything but the safe.
A DIRGE OR A WAIL, OR SOMETHING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They are spouting on the platform,
They are droning in the Club,
They are clacking o’er their knitting,
They are skiting in the pub.
They are talking Big in nations,
And they won’t put in the peg
Who‘don’t know the map of Europe
From a dog’s hind leg!
Oh 1 they talk you blind and silly,
And their buzz would addle eggs
Whgn they speak of “Mons” and “Lily,”
Add Liege (pronounce it “Legs”).
Wherever they may drivel,
They will aye come back again
By Way of Schleswig-Holstein
To “Awl-soeks-Lawrain.”
The chattering Red Cross ladies
Are disposing of it, too ;
They’re jumping at conclusions,
As wimmen always do !
It’s “My deah, you know, the Allies,
I am ve-ry much afraid,
Would have lost this war if Turkey
Hadn’t come to our aid!”
And the Flappers fluff and snivel,
Where the washy tea is poured ;
They pretend to have a cousin,
Or something else “abrawd” ;
They are perfectly sus-certain,
In spite of Pa and Ma,
That something must have happened
To the dear (choke) Australiah!
And suburban fathers thunder,
The family cheers to win:
“Why don’t England make reprisals?
Why don't she just wade in?
We ain’t satisfied, I tell you,
With the conduct of the war!”
And he writes demanding reasons
From the ed-it-or.
And the editor is sitting,
Mild and patient, even now,
Fingers cramped and body weary,
Aching eyes and throbbing brow;
The proofs are piled beside hiai,
And the office map hath he—
He is back at school am 1 learning
flis ge-og-ra-phee.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
WHY WEDS THE WARRIOR?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne clergymen admit an increase in wed-
dings. “Scores of young men who did not dream of
marrying a few months back have married after
being a few weeks in camp,” one clergyman says.
Curious persons are wondering why.
They wonder why our soldier Bill,
Who lately shied at Cupid,
Has knuckled down to Mistress Jill,
And lisped the fatal words, “I will!”
He once considered stupid.
What is there in this martial stress
That makes a man’s resistance less?
Is’t strict Obedience learned in camp
That helps him to be mated?
Or is it that the careless scamp
Whose morals were of vagrant stamp,
With courage stimulated
Assumes that, since he’s in for strife,
He may as well take on a wife?
He splices her some weeks before;
Then after brief love prattle,
He marches off to shed his gore,
And face the fiery stress of war,
The shriek and roar of battle.
Who wouldn’t wed as gay and breezy
When getting out of it so easy?
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

“Tridit”: In answer to “J.J.C.’s” (B.
4/3/’ 15) clamor for information as to the manu-
facture of a damper, let him know that the knack is
in the kneading. I’ll have to let it go at that, be-
cause the knack is not explainable. At a construction
camp on the Grafton-Casino (N.S.W.) railway the
argument on how to make a good damper raged furi-
ously for weeks. Finally a test was arranged. Two
men were handed a dishful of mixture and
kneaded it for exactly the same time, with as nearly
as possible the same motions. The dampers were
baked in the same fire for the same time. One turned
out a jim-dazzler; the other a fair strike-me-dead.
The method of kneading dampers differs with the
operator. Some use fingers only, others punch the
dough. One chap stirs it with a spoon till it is
nearly ready; another mixes it a little at a time.
But, like the good scone-constructor, the good damper-
engineer cannot tell how he arrives at his result.
“Jupp”: Talking of flies reminds me. I
was on the N.S.W. North Coast some time ago, and
the pest was so bad that a strange horse couldn’t
feed without being in agony. But the horses on
the spot have the strength of things. One day I
saw 40 running in a paddock. They split up into
groups of 20. Each group made itself into a circle,
each horse with head near the next one’s tail. Then
they started feeding, carefully swishing the flies
from each others’ heads at the same time. When
each horse had eaten all the grass within reach in
front of him and pulled the hairs of his neighboi s
tail through his teeth, two horses from the first
ring ran quickly oyer and made themselves a part of
the second ring. Thus each horse now had a new
spot in front of his face, and the feeding went on
afresh. When this gave out two more hopped over
from the smaller ring to the larger, and so it went
on. When only four horses were left in the little
ring, all hands went to another part of the paddock
and made up two fresh rings of 20 each, and then
proceeded as before. This they kept up till they
all had a feed. I didn’t stop to see what they did
afterwards.

“Yarram Yarram” : I butt in to put
“Boronia” wise about the “cobbler” fish. “Cobbler,
by the way, is a corruption. The correct name is
“cobra,” bestowed, I presume, on account of the
fish’s sting being likened unto that of the Indian
snake. In appearance the fish is very much like
the ordinary gurnet, but is greyish brown, with a
plentiful supply of stings or horns • as sharp as
needles, distributed over the body. The head alone
has six stinging points, the prick from any one of
which will cause great agony if not treated at once.
He may lurk about the shallows of an evening, as
“Boronia” was informed, but I am inclined to think
he does so only by way of a change. I have always
encountered him myself in comparatively deep water
and in daylight. It was in George’s Bay (E. Coast,
Tas.) that I first made the cobbler’s acquaintance.
He just pushed his card a couple of inches into the
meat below my thigh. I only escaped a few sleep-
less nights by enlarging the puncture to produce
profuse bleeding, afterwards bathing the wound in
salt water. This, I think, is the only antidote.
* * * *
“'i’igilans”: The fact that it will he thrown
into the dustbin doesn’t lessen the value of the
suggestion made by J. T. Beckett, Chief Inspector
of Aborigines (N.T.), that the northern Binghi
should be given military training. Beckett is abso-
lutely correct in his opinion,that the northern black
is the smartest hand with horses and cattle, and
deadly with a rifle. He’s
all that and more. Binghi’s
penetration and judgment
in emergency are as envi-
able as are his quick eye
and ear. The coastal
natives are born warriors
—big, powerful fellows,
capable of great endur-
ance. They are rarely
under sft. 10in., muscular
and well-built, especially
on the Adelaide and Alli-
gator Rivers, where food
is plentiful. There is a
strain of Malay blood in
their veins, which no doubt
accounts for their fighting
proclivities. A native in
these parts thinks nothing
of carrying a 501 b. bag of
flour or a 701 b. bag of
sugar a hundred miles or
so on his head. 1 have seen
a party of blacks, women
amongst them, carrying a
ton of flour in this manner.
The Territory natives do
not know their own
strength. They make fine
police troopers, and should
also make good soldiers.
The scheme should be to
train them for home de-
fence. What’s wrong with
a native army up north as
we find in Africa and
many other lands? It
could be recruited not
only from the Territory,
but from Queensland and
Kimberley also. There are
fine natives in West Kim-
berley—shearers, bullock-
drivers, pressers and rouse-
abouts. Some of the big-
gest natives in Australia
are found in the Leopold
ranges and along the nor’-
west coast. The De Grey
blacks are likewise splen-
did specimens. I have advocated utilising the blacks
for military purposes for some years, and the out-
spokenness of Beckett makes me hope that official-
dom will get sense one of these fine days.
“Mary McC.” : Town planners and others
who are enthusiastic about the improvement of our
suburbs suggest the abolition of the front-garden
fence. This is all very well; but as a first step in
the direction must come the abolition of the front-
garden cow. While people keep the family milk
wandering about in thei suburban street, the pickets
are a necessary safeguard. The cow business is
much overdone, and something drastic is required.
No matter how great the nuisance, you are not
allowed to shoot the animal, and your only redress
is to drive her to the pound. What I advocate is
registration of every piece of potential milk, and
the refusal of a license to any person who cannot
prove that he has enough grass to feed the proposi-
tion all the year round. In my own select suburb
there are people without an eighth of an acre of
back yard who pretend to keep a cow. Naturally
the public falls over the animal twice a week in
the darkness, and the rest ef the time she is con*
strueting butter-fat out of somebody’s cactus dahlias.

“Outcast”: The cocoanut crab is pretty
numerous in San Ohristoval (Solomons) and man-
ages to do a good deal of damage on the plantations.
Climbing the trees, he selects his nuts, pierces the
young ones through the eye and drinks the water.
The old nuts he husks with his powerful claws and
smashes them by bombing them .on to the ground.
The story goes that if the shell is broken smoothly,
the crab eats the flesh, but if the edges are
jagged the nut is left and another procured. The
natives tie grass round the tree the crab has climbed.
As it descends backwards, it appears to imagine
when it feels the herbage that it has reached the
ground and so loosens its hold and falls and breaks
its neck. Another native narrative is that • these
cocoanut crabs on moonlight nights hold dances. Two
of the largest and oldest crabs take up their positions
and beat time with their claws, while the rest of the
assembly circle round in a dance, waving their
claws as a nig. might wave his dancing club. This
I’ll vouch for, that the crabs do go down to bathe
on the beach on certain nights.
* * * *
“Outcast” : “Machete,” writing on the Bird
of Death in Papua, leads me to an island in the
Banks Group, where a kind of divination is prac-
tised. A man who wants to learn the future repairs
to a certain place and takes note of the bird he
first sees. Owls and hawks are regarded as good
omens, but any red-crested or black birds are the
advance agents of death, the former betokening death
in battle, the latter death by illness. On the same
island the kingfisher is called “the bird of evil,” and
its special function is the carrying of bad news.
Should a kingfisher rest near a man it is a sure sign
of trouble, and it is customary to ask, “Is so and so
dead?” The bird replies by jerks of the head. No
one will kill a kingfisher on any account. All its
relatives might come along and wipe out a man’s
friends and leave nothing but his creditors.
“C.F.E.”: Is the betel nut any relation to
the betel leaf? In Ceylon, on tea and rubber
estates, where the labor is mostly Tamil with a few
Cingalese, a “chew” from the leaf is made thus:
A piece of areca nut, wrapped in a betel leaf, in
turn wrapped in a piece of tobacco leaf, is inserted
into the mouth. Then a dab of lime from a small
tin is licked off the forefinger. The expectoration
is red, the lime turning the juice of the betel leaf
to that color. The teeth, in time, become black.
The juice of the betel leaf acts as a stimulant,
enabling tea pluckers of all ages, from very small
girls to old women, to pluck steadily from 6 a.m.
till 5 p.m., without an interval for food. Has the
betel nut any other virtue than its intoxicating
power ?

The customs authorities at Venice seized two truck
loads of “beer” from Berlin, consigned to Tripoli.
Ninety-two barrels contained captured French rifles
and ammunition intended for the Arabs.— Cable.

WET WEATHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When all the hills are swathed in mist
And even- hollow’s brimming o’er,
And tinkling rillets through the grass
Adown the slopes tumultuous pour ;
When trees are streaked with sombre lines
And. crystals tremble ’mid the leaves,
And jewels hang upon the threads
Of frosted film the. spider weaves —-
Ah, just to live! for life is fair
As clear, cool drops abundant spilled ;
As clear and pure as grassy cup
The silver-shedding skies have filled.
When the wet scrub breathes out a scent
To stif the blood in languid veins,
And the grey nimbus, drooping low,
Hangs ghost-like o’er the sodden plains ;
When rain flies glinting on the wind,
Nor ever bird or beast is seen,
But. all is fragrant solitude
Arrayed in ebony and green—
Ah! just to walk a lonely road
; , All in a scented, silent world !
To see the sparkling diamond-dust
On fragile petals half-unfurled !
When all the bush is dark and sweet
And filled with voices murmuring low,
Vague laughter mixed with soft complaint,
The waters whispering as they flow;
When puffs of foam with yellow tinged
Drift by the heads' of rushes tall,
Or quiver on the tawny flood
Below each chanting waterfall^-
Ah! just to dally with the wind!
To, hear, the, water splash and sing!
Life is a sweet, a keen, a gay,
A rarely precious, joyous thing!
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.

“Eddyson”: It was McAdam, the circus
man, told me of the enterprise of Bartholomew Phinn,
of Drogheda, Bungaree. Phinn interviewed McAdam
in the travelling menagerie by the cage of big mon-
keys, and said he: “Yer the hid man iv the Zoo ye
have here, I’m told.” “I am,” said McAdam.
“Wan would know a great deal iv wild brutes iv the
field, ye are, are yeh?” “I know beef from a bull’s
foot,” said McAdam. “Come closer, will yeh now?
Whisper,” said Phinn—“have ye an ould lion t’ spare
ar all?” “I have not!” ejaculated the circus man.
“Nor an ould tiger?” “What the Devil would you be
doing with a tiger ?” “Anny ould wan would do.
'Tis no mather at all if ut’s wor-rn out par-rtly.”
“I haven’t an old beast in the menagerie.” “I’d not
think it onreasonable did yeh ask twinty-five shillin’
or twinty-seven-an’-six fer a lion, or a tiger, fer the
mather iv that, ve’d have no further use fer.” “There
isn’t a lion or a tiger here worth a penny less than
70 pounds.” Phinn gasped. “Sivinty pound!” he
said. “Man, but ut’s a sore heap iv money fer a
kind iv cat, so it is. ’N’ have yet nothin’ second-
hand, nothin’ in the way iv a rampagin’ wild baste
would howl in the night ’n’ terrify?” “I have a wolf
I’ll let you have for eight pounds.” McAdam pointed
out the wolf. “ ’Tis nothin’ but a poor, sick dog,”
said Bartholomew Phinn contemptuously. “I'm nadin’
a tirrible, great, desthructiff baste would carry off a
cow.” “Look here,” said McAdam, “what the deuce
is your little game?” “Whist, man. I’d not have
the wor-rld know iv ut! ’Tis a sacrit bechune me
’n’ mesilf, but I have the nate pub back on the timber
thrack t’ Danny’s Flat, ’n’ there’s nothin’ iv business
doin’. The dthrink I’m never sellin’ would be the
rooin iv me, ’n’ not a man rests in me house frim
Sathurday t’ Sathurday. Will yeh note the differ
wid Andy Clarke owns the Big Butt Hotil biyant be
the Spur-r, him doin’ the marv’liss shwift bar! ’n’
bottle thrade, ’n’ that overrun wid boarders he is
they’re shleepin’ hung t’ the roof iv the bar-rn in
string beds, ’n’ for whoi is all this but becaze he
have the Tantynooly Tiger visit his disthrict occa-
sional t’ advertise it far-r ’n’ wide, ’n’ bring the
shportsmin ’n' the sightseers frim all the cor-rners iv
the earth ez far ez Sydney! C’u’d I get holt iv an
ould lion cheap, lame ’r not, I’d lave him loose in
the thick timber adjacent t’ me house t’ roar be
night, ’n’ catch a ca’f wanst in a whoile. Be so
doin’, the disthrict would be advertised over all the
big papers to Melbur-rne ’n’ Geelong ’n’ the fine
towns further, ’n’ the shpor-rtin’ min ’n’ the shar-rp-
shoothers, ’n’ the manny simple people do be runnin’
ever afther strange sights t’ see would come throopin’
to me hotel, thrade would be lookin’ up in me bar-r,
’n’ there’s no knowin’ I might sell me property fer
ez much ez six hunthred pounds befure the lion was
took.” McAdam grinned an unaccustomed width.
“You —; —• old fool,” said he. “You could do all
you want with a calf and a carriage rug.” “Would
the like iv that roar at all ?” said Phinn. “Get a
megaphone, man, and do your own roaring.” Phinn
looked McAdam closely in the eye for a full minute,
then turned on his heel, and left the circus. “Within
five weeks,” said McAdam, “the metropolitan papers
were telling hair-raising stories of the strange monster
in the bush at Danr.y’s Flat, and already I’ve sent a
customer on who’s thinking of giving six hundred
for Phinn’s hotel.” I was very grateful to McAdam
for his story. I had been preparing to go up and
have a pop at big game in Drogheda myself.

the country, and was lent a policeman new to the
business to swear the witnesses. He managed well
enough in the morning after the senior-constable had
primed him ; but during lunch-time he forgot some-
thing, and the first witness he swore after lunch had

the evidence you give before this Board is the whole
truth an’ nothin’ but the truth, God strike yer!”

“Te Babarantiko”: If “Lonesome” (B.
4/3/’l5) means the Kingsmills he has never seen
there a single “canoe made of flakes of wood sewn .
together with human hair, capable of carrying 40 to
50 persons.” Such canoes no longer exist in the
group. Before the white man brought tools, canoes
up to 11 fathoms long were built. Slabs of titai
were laboriously chipped out and shaped with
tekatati-shell axes, and neatfy sewn together on to
the ribs, keel, stem and sternpieces with finest hand-
made coir string. Fleets of sailing canoes, built on
the same beautiful lines, are still used, but there are
certainly none over 30ft. long—unless they have been
built recently. The old, tedious method of building,
with a neat inset of folded strips of dead pan-
danus leaves to serve as caulking between the edges
of the pieces as they were sewn together, is falling
into disuse. Now, as a rule, four redwood boards,
12in. x lin., and usually 20ft. to 27ft. long, are bent
to the frame and fastened on with 2in. . galvanised
boat-nails. The modern craft, instead of keel, has
flat bottom. Old- or new-style canoes are rarely
over 2-Jft. wide amidships, and the carrying capacity
is slight. In the Marshall Group ocean-going canoes
as large as a small schooner, and carrying a pan-
danus-leaf hut amidships, are still built and used.

“Werrimee”: “Rag” (B. 4/.3/T5 ) revives
the old fallacy that snakes swallow their young.
He makes a further error in stating that “all forms
of lizards, snakes and amphibious-; saurians are egg-
lavers.” Nearly all Australian venomous snakes,
including water-snakes and sea-snakes, are viviparous.
Two exceptions are the brown snake (Diemenia
superciliosa) and the whip snake (D. reticulata).
The black snake produces about 20 live young ones
at a time, and the tiger and superb snakes over
30. The young ones do not require the care or
protection of the mother. They are able to look
after themselves from birth. Most of the harmless
snakes (as the blind snakes, fresh-water snakes, tree-
snakes, green and brown snakes, and the pythons)
are oviparous. The pythons (as the carpet and dia-
mond and the northern rock-snakes) incubate their
eggs, and are the only specimens known to do so.
The eggs (usually about 30) are stuck together in
the form of a pyramid, and the snake coils round and
round them, its head resting on top, the object being
to keep the egg-shells moist, as they would otherwise
shrivel. Some of the shink lizards are viviparous.
The single young of the shingle-back—a large lizard
common in the dry interior —is brought forth alive;
so also are the six to 15 young ones of the southern
blue-tongued lizard (Tiliqua nigrolutea).
“Force of Habit”: The boss had been
driving teams along the road for years, but he
bought a car last week and took us all for a drive
on Sunday. It was a scorcher, and he pulled up at

“Billy Ballyooly” : I hear, on pretty good
authority, that there’s something of a drought at
present in the Riverina and thereabout. If there is,
I’m off to see what the country round Wyalong looks
like when it’s dry. The only time I ever saw it was
when I was driving a bullock-team. The cargo con-
sisted of one boiler. We had just had a spell of wet
weather, and all the mud in N.S.W. had been washed
on to the Wyalong-road. The rapidity with which
Bill of Berlin is getting to Calais is a circumstance
compared with our rush through that mud. In three
weeks we covered one hundred yards, and we only
knocked off digging trenches, swearing and belting
the bullocks when it got too dark to see what we
were saying.
“Lagani N.” : Every mountain stream that
I’ve happened on to in Papua has had splendid
speckled trout and catfish ready for the taking, and
the taking was this wise. A couple of boys, armed
with short spears, would dive into the pool and
frighten the fish out of it into the gravelly shallows,
where all the other boys would be waiting to pounce
on the prospective gorge. Some of the trout must
have weighed at least 101 b. After being gutted, they
were placed on a rack of green saplings, over a fire
to grill. When cooked the skin peels clean off, and
the snow-white glistening flesh can be shaken in two
luscious slabs from the bone. Fish not immediately
required are smoke-cured on the grid. Cassowary,
wallaby and pig are prepared for next month’s
tucker in the same way.
“Paddymelon”: Don’t let us miss the
chance of securing a good, technical education to
the coming generations of bullock-conductors. I
object to any ass rising up and saying that the
engineering of bullocks is a dying industry. So long
as we have cheap-labor fiscal-dodgers in power,
Stringy Bark and Greenhide will continue to be the
mainstay of the country. Let us, therefore, start a
fund for Roads Scholarships for bullockies, and send
’em to Turkey to pick up the “language” that will
be presently used by the natives there to the Ger-
mans. As the foundation for a good standard work
on advanced bullock driving I don’t think we are
ever likely to see it beaten.
“Mil” : Re “X.P.D.’s” potters of Port
Moresby (B. 25/l/’l5). The male doesn’t do any
pottery—that is woman’s work. “X.P.D.,” who saw
the bucks “lash sailing canoes together to make
gigantic rafts,” was looking at the lakatoi fleet
which has sailed its 600 miles to barter pots for
sago every season for a few hundreds years pro-
bably. Right back to the memory of the first white
man, anyhow,' freight of pottery westward to the
Gulf of Papua on the one monsoon; freight of sago
eastward on the other. Primitive ceremonies of fare-
well and placatory dancing go with the westward run
of the lakatois, which, clumsy as they look, can sail
close to the wind. The big mat sail, shaped like a
lobster’s claw, is full of beauty, too, seen against the
light.
“Fliuderino”: What’s all this fuss ner-
vous Westralians are making about bats? There’s
no need for it. If the beasts infest a house all the
occupier need do is to introduce an energetic snake,
if there is anything in the food line that a snake
likes better than a bat it is two bats. In Northern
Queensland, where bats breed like rabbits, some
people consider their humpy incomplete if it does
not include a carpet snake somewhere up in the
rafters. Once, at the Yarrabah Mission Station, I
was shown an eight-foot bat-catcher asleep on a
crossbeam in the big schoolhouse. But that was
the last I saw of him, as a few nights later a 1
couple of hungry binghis purloined him and roasted
him for supper. It was an irreparable loss-—or at
least would have been if there hadn’t been a million
or so other eight-footers in North Queensland.
“Outcast”: In Papua, by the -legend, tlie
crocodile has the power, through the wizard, of taking
on the shape of a young man or beautiful maiden,
and in parts of the New Hebrides this power is
assumed by a kind of sea snake. It is generally
seen in a dim light, when any young man returning
from fishing or the gardens is liable to glimpse an
attractive girl beckoning and alluring him. Brown
Brother is apt to be shredded with uncertainty.
There is a chance the maiden may be real flesh
and blood ; on the other hand, she may be the mae,
and if he yields to her invitation, he comes to a
bad and unduly sudden end. However, there are cer-
tain tests by which Lothario may find out the
character of his charmer. For instance, if her tongue
is a brilliant scarlet, or if her elbows and knees bend
ever so slightly the wrong way, she is a bad lot, and
should be passed with the utmost agility. Should a
nettle tree be at hand a mne will accept an invitation
to sit upon it. When you observe that she is not
being troubled about the sting, you let out a yell
and volplane for home. The possession of a croton
leaf is a sure spell for safety, which makes things
easier, for I don't suppose a chap can ask a prospec-
tive best girl to sit on a stinging tree in order to
make sure she is IT. She might be offended.

“Hare”: Damper; ingredients, plenty of
elbow grease, 21b. flour, 1 teaspoonful salt, 2 teaspoon-
fuls sugar, add water and knead for not less than a
half-hour, and as much longer as you like. The longer
you knead the better it will rise, and cook in fine
ashes after the fire has been burning all day. You
want to leave it in the ashes not less than an hour.
If you follow these instructions you will have some-
thing fit for the gods or other blokes who rouise.
Damper making to-day is a thing of yesterday.
HIS CRIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A tall, old man he is, and fine,
With quite a kingly bearing,
And like a soldier of the line
He passes, little caring
Though greeted oft with groans and hoots,
And sometimes cats and broken boots.
He’s done no one the smallest harm,
He’s clean and decent living;
His manner has an Old World charm ;
He’s generous in giving;
But neighbors eat the hens he owns,
And children follow him with stones.
What has he done? He's British bred,
No German-born exotic ;
The cornet tunes he plays in bed
Are truly patriotic ;
But that man has no sympathiser
Who wears his whiskers like the Kaiser.
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

WHAT MAKES THE WHEELS GO ROU
LEWIS &WnlTTy MAKERS
A HOME REMEDY FOR DYSPEPSIA.
In view of the well-known fact that pro-
bably over 90 per cent, of all stomach
trouble, dyspepsia, indigestion, flatulence,
etc., is either directly or indirectly due to
acidity, or as it is commonly called “sour
stomach,” it is evident that correction and
prevention depend almost entirely on neutral-
ising the acid and stopping the fermentation.
For this purpose there is nothing better than
a half-teaspoonful of ordinary bisurated
magnesia in a little water immediately after
eating or whenever pain is felt. This imme-
diately neutralises the acid, stops the fer-
mentation, and permits the stomach to pro-
ceed with its work under normal conditions.
Owing to the widespread use of bisurated
magnesia by physicians and in the hospitals
and for greater convenience in carrying,
most chemists now supply it in the form of
compressed tablets, two of which are equiva-
lent to a half-teaspoonful of the ordinary
powder form. Care should be taken to get
bisurated magnesia, as other forms are lack-
ing in its peculiarly valuable properties.*

The other day a man charged in a Mel-
bourne suburb with using bad and abusive
language in a public place described him-
self as a theatrical critic. Poor fellow, of
course he was only blowing off the stuff he
couldn't put into his notices.

Alec Peacock, the cack’yng Premier of
Vic., was in his youth suggested as a candi-
date for the Church. Ballarat’s bishop of
those days tried very hard to make Alex-
ander James a clergyman; but Peacock’s
mother was against it, maiiJy because she
did not wish to give two sons, to the church.
The late Archdeacon Peacock, of Warrnam-
bool, was Alexander’s brother.
Tim Coghlan. the most readable Statis-
tician Australia ever produced, and for 10
years past Agent-General, is escaping from
that office after serving the Ma State in
one way and another for 42 years. In Lon-
don he has accumulated “large personal
interests,” and one of them, the big Marconi
enterprise, will for the future have most of
his attention.
Australia’s old friend Inclement Wragge
thinks he has settled down at last, and gives
his final address as Auckland (M.L.). Now-
adays he devotes his time to the cultivation
of an extensive tropical fruit garden.
Experiments in the growing of bananas,
pines (35 varieties),
and the host of tropic
fruits have been so
successful that the
garden is expected to
be one of the sights of
the North before long.

Died under the shadow of the Pyramids
Major Frank Parker, a son of that Stephen
Parker, knight, who was once Chief Justice
of W.A., and himself a. barrister. He
served in the Transvaal disturbance under
Major Moor, and when that officer was killed
took command of the Westralian unit. The
unpleasantness over, he was for a time Reg-
istrar of the Criminal Court at Johannes-
burg, but returned to Perth, pursued Costs
and acted as Crown Prosecutor until the
bugles sounded in August. Meningitis
gripped him at 40, and made a widow and
orphaned a son.
Though Tim Coghlan is throwing up the
Agent-General’s job, N. S. Wales will still
keep his pot boiling. There is a pension of
£6OO a year coming to him.
Robert Dougan, who crossed the border
at Ash field (Sydney) last week, sailed from
the Ou'd Sod 64 years ago with a pick in
quest of a fortune at
Ballarat. He didn’t
find it, so rushed
away across the
Pacific towards the
Frazer River, sailing
in the barque Orestes
—“B nil y” Hayes
master. It was on
this trip that Hayes
put into Honolulu,
and the young Irish
digger saw a seaman
hoist in the rigging
the shirt which
brought on board an
armed party from the
warship Calypso. The
voyage was continued
with another master,
Hayes remaining be-
hind. The Frazer
proving a duffer,
Dougan doubled back
to Australia to become
an alderman and eventually a mayor in his
more helpless old age. He scored 82.
A human skull has been found, with tlie
remains of a saddle, in wild country between
the Burra (S.A.) and the Murray; and the
local Oldest Inhabitant suggests that the
fate of ; a once much-talked-of Captain
Arrara is cleared up at last. He came from
South America, nearly 60 years ago, with
mules for the Burra mine, got lost in the
scrub thereabouts, and was never heard of
again.
Governor Galway, of South Aus., is the
most unconventional sportsman that ever
dropped into a vice-regal seat, lie never
willingly misses a race-meeting. He golfs.
He gets up scratch cricket matches with
villages near his summer residence in the
hills. He goes round to all the suburban
bowling-greens and plays matches there too
—-to the great delight of an historic game
that hitherto has not had Government House
patronage in S. Aus. Also, entering for
two events in the recent S.A. tennis tourna-
ment, he proved to be a sound, old-fashioned,
defensive player, whose persistent lobbing
was proof against anything but hard volley-
ing. With Mulholland, his private secre-
tary, he got through two rounds of the
open doubles; and, with golfer Gunson,
pushed into the final of tlie Veterans, where,
however, he ran against Dr. Hone, a cham-
pion of years ago. with the inevitable result.
“S.” : I have met one German who utters
no complaint. He is a suburban shoemaker
and appears to be doing very well. “Der
beople pring der boots me to more as effer,”
he explained the other day. “It giff dem
der chances to told me vot a dog mein
Kaiser iss, und make show how Shermany
got licked easy. Ven der ships got sink by
der Dardanelles I got me no boots; but ven
Przemysl lie fall efferybotty bring his boots.
If doze Sherman rewairses go on dey make
of me a facdory, yes.”
Monsignor O’Brien, Rector of St. John’s,
the R.C. College within the University of
Sydney, was professor of theology at All
Hallows’ Missionary College when Cardinal
Moran invited him to come to Australia. lie
hung back —begged the Cardinal to remember
that he was already nearly an old man. But
the “old man” came, 'and until quite lately—
-28 years afterwards—-nobody was reminded
that he was very old. Professor Sheehy, of
St. Patrick’s College, will get the job, if it
doesn’t go a-field.

John Brown, the coal-mine owner,' was
the principal guest at a conviviality at
Newcastle (N.S.W.) the other day. The
crowd couldn’t have picked on a more cheer-
ful picnic companion. There was a happy
little spread, of course —and employees got
this to go on with :
If 1 have any more trouble at Pclaw Main I wilt
close it down.
Then it was the proprietors’ turn: —
It won’t be long before they will be running about
to get an Association again.
After that, to everybody in general, this
defiance
The men fighting me arc fighting’ the two best
collieries in this part of the world, with John-
Brown at the head of them,
Hooray for John Brown! What a beautiful
anarchist spoiled by a few ships and col-
lieries !
“E.” on the war in Melbourne: —
Melba seems to have expressed her feelings pretty
strong’ly about the Germanisation of Melbourne Univer-
sity Conservatorium of Music. She has stated that for
the remainder of her stay in this country she will take
a personal interest in every student of singing at the
other Conservatorium, founded by Hall, and now directed
by Hart. Considering how she identified herself with
the moneyed Marshall Hall faction a few years ago,
Melba’s announcement looks very like an intimation
that her favor goes to the Albert-street school in
opposition to the University school where Hall is
now re-established. Perhaps it was his recent wild
and wobbling address on “Ideal and Real” that did
it; for therein the Prof, suggested strong Teutonic
leanings by referring to the sin of Potsdam
as the crime of all Europe: “European civili-
sation bears on its brow the shameful brand
of realism, and is not ashamed. And Europe
to-day is atoning her desertion of hope-
crowned idealism for the cold, cruel, pitiless creed
of the realist, by the wholesale slaughter of her
youth, the destruction of her precious monuments,”
etc. If Belgium and France had been more ideal-
istic, and even less prepared for war than they were,
would the Gerhogs not have come along to burn
their cities and commit atrocities on defenceless citi-
zens all the same ? Everybody save a musical pro-
fessor who got his education and prejudices from
Germany knows that no amount of idealism in the
rest of Europe could protect it against the militarism
which radiates from Potsdam. As a matter of fact,
it is for its long-continued neglect to suppress Pots-
damned realism by force that Europe is now atoning.
One can imagine Melba, the artistic woman of com-
mon-sense, reading Marshall Hall’s blather and ask-
ing: “What does he take us for?” And following
that up, she has promised to attend once a fortnight
at the Conservatorium where Hall doesn’t blather.
TO THE INFANT FRITZ.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Leaflets have been distributed in German schools
asking the children to bring the war to a successful
end by eating no cakes or sweets and very little
of other things, and by rebuking their parents if
they should eat wheaten bread instead of war bread
(mixed wheat and potato flour).
Gorge not yourself, my little friend,
With cakelets plain or plummy;
This war will all the sooner end
The less you stoke your tummy.
No doubt your gastronomic fires
Cry out for frequent feeding;
But kids who conquer such desires
Show patriotic breeding.
Examine father’s daily bread
And if you find it wheaten,
Heap curses on his swollen head
For every slice he’s eaten.
Collect the tiny crumbs that fall
And hoard them like a miser,
And write in charcoal on the wall:
“Ach Himmel! lloch der Kaiser!”
All kids or parents, thin or fat,
Should now be self-denying.
For lack of commissariat
The Belgian folk are dying;
Invading troops laid waste their land
With “thoroughness” quite Hunnisli,
And this is wliy, you understand,
Your Gott should England punish.
If father cannot go to war—
He would if he were younger —
Beseech him, Fritz, to eat no more,
But suffer pangs of hunger.
If you be game to knock off cake
And bread that’s fully wheaten
Dad ought to chance a tummy-ache
Till England be well beaten —■
To starve, in short, for duty’s sake
Till England be well beaten.
Beat England, Fritz, ach ! that’s the thing,
For she’s the wicked sinner ;
Your Hymn of Hate with fervor sing
Instead of taking dinner.
Should patriots be overfed
With meat, and pies, and jellies,
Whilst Belgians, wishing they were dead,
Have rats inside their bellies?
Abstain, my Fritz, from tarts and buns,
Small joy could they afford you;
And He who loves all little Huns
Will possibly reward you.
And if He doesn’t, blame Him not
Nor think He might be wiser ;
Bid father take his gun and “pot”
The Gott-forsaken Kaiser,
VICTORIA.
O. K.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Insurance Office of Australia,
Ltd. (Sydney), which was founded in 1910,
and began with a deficit of £5250 represent-
ing preliminary exes.:—■
Towards
Nov., Profits. Dividends. Reserves. Deficit.
. _ £5250
1911 .. £4115 Nil £4115 1135
Dec., Loss.
1912 . . 436 Nil 1571
Adjustment Re-Insurance Go., £367 .. 1938
1913 . . 963 Nil 2901
1914 . . 5212 Nil 8113
The directors allege (though there is
nothing in the published accounts to sup-
port them) that “the whole of the loss was
incurred prior to the final confirmation of
the present Board’s appointment in May
last.” The new directors claim not merely
that they have had a better experience as to
claims, but that the expense rate has been
reduced from 58.3 p.c. of net premiums to
46.5 p.c., cutting the average down to 52.1
p.c. for the year. But 46.5 p.c. is still a
great deal too much, especially as this co.
passes on its re-assurances to an associated
office whose expense rate is very nearly as
high. It is quite true that there are one or
two first-class offices, like the Mercantile
Mutual, which show as much as from 45 p.c.
to 50 p.c.; but that is mainly the outcome of
a special selection of risks, with the result
that the Mercantile Mutual’s loss rate is
only from 23 p.c. to 35 p.c.—the actual limits
of recent years.
The fact is the principle on which the
1.0. A. was founded was all wrong. There
is only one way to economically launch an
insurance office, and that is by expanding
from one centre. A few men start a co.
with a small capital to do their own busi-
ness. Then they get their friends’ business;
and the circle goes on growing. That means
a low expense rate. When, however, a new
co. lobs in on a town where nobody ever
heard of it, it only gets business by buying
it. Or it gets business that has been turned
down by others, and up soar the claims. This
co. set out to cover Australia all at once.
Its figures show the result. No doubt in
some respects it has been handicapped. It
had two joint managers, and both of them—
well, let us merely say that they have gone.
But they weren’t fools, by any means. One
of them is as good a business-getter as there
is in Australia —perhaps as there ever was
in Australia. They had influential directors
also in Sydney and Melbourne. Unfortu-
nately, the two Boards couldn’t pull to-
gether. That, of course, is the almost-inevi-
table trouble with a new office which tries to
cover a continent all at once. The mana-
gers knew that the only chance of getting
a decent business in Melbourne was to get a
Melbourne Board with a strong pull. When
that was got, the directors there resented
the intrusion of Sydney directors who they
knew couldn’t command two-penn’orth of
business outside Sydney and thereabouts.
However, the whole thing has now been put
into the pot. There are new managers and
new directors, and there is harmony between
Sydney and Melbourne, and between this
co. and the other insurance cos. which for-
merly couldn’t stand some of joint-manager
Stockfeld’s ideas for getting business. The
machine is, therefore, practically new. What
sort of work it is going to do this paper
doesn’t know.
There certainly is room for it in Australia,
which hitherto has most unpatriotically
given a vast deal of its insurance to alien
cos. and to British cos. with alien connec-
tions. But while there is room for the co. there
is none at all for uneasiness on the part of
policy-holders. Indeed, the size of the paid
capital, which makes the early outlook for
shareholders grey, adds enormously to the
safety of policy-holders. Presently, if exes,
are cut down hard, and a better selection of
risks is made, shareholders ought to do
pretty well, too. This list shows how the
business has grown :
Net Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses. Expenses. Rate. Rate.
1911. . £25,951 £6,748 £17,537 26.0 p.c. 67.5 p.c.
1912.. 47,241 22,443 27,845 47.5 „ 58.9 „
1913.. 54,618 22,911 35,275 41.9 „ 64.6 „
1914.. 67,510 39,292 35,222 58.2 „ 52.1 „
No doubt a good deal of that £67,510 ought
to be declined without thanks. Still, a premium
income of even £50,000, derived from good
risks, would be very satisfactory considering
the age of the co.; and with anything like a
reasonable expense rate, that should easily
give an underwriting profit of 10 p.c., and
thus produce enough to pay a 6 p.c. dividend,
leaving the interest earnings to go towards
reserves. For a start, of course, even when
a profit of £7500 or more can be shown, no
dividend should be paid till the present
deficit is wiped out. And, by the way, the
deficit is really greater than the table shows,
because there is no reserve for unexpired
risks. In other words, the premiums re-
ceived in (say) December are included as
revenue for 1914, whereas the risk runs till
December, 1915. To cover this risk would
probably call for £22,500, which amount
should therefore rightly be added to the
deficit.
The Re-Insurance Co. (Sydney) :
