This co. is an offshoot of the 1.0. A., and was
formed ostensibly to carry the latter’s re-
insurance—that is, to take risks in excess of
what the parent might think it a fair thing
for one office to hold on any one line. There
was no good reason for it. If the old co.
wanted to be justified in taking more risks
all it had to do was to increase its own capi-
tal. What actually was done was to in-
crease the capital by creating a second co.,
and thus creating at the same time a second
lot of standing charges and running exes.
The expense rate of a re-insurance concern
of the kind ought, nevertheless, in any case,
to be low. This one’s isn’t, as the following
small taole shows :
Net Pre- Ex- Loss Expense
Feb., miums. Losses, penses. Rate. Rate.
1913 .. £8,558 £4,855 £3620 56.7 p.c. 42.3 p.c.
Dec.,
1913 .. 8,527 4,243 3627 49.7 „ 42.5 „
1914 .. 11,781 10,701 5220 90.8 „ 44.3 „
The past year’s exes, include £261 of bad
debts; even without that, however, the rate
is extravagant. The directors are, therefore,
not justified in saying that the deficiency on
the year’s operations “is due entirely to the
abnormally high loss ratio experienced in
common with all other cos. during 1914.”
The loss rate was abnormal, certainly,
amounting to 90.8 p.c. of the premiums. For
comparison, three representative cos. may
be taken —the Queensland, with head office
in Sydney ; the Colonial Mutual, Melbourne;
and the New Zealand. Of the first, the loss
rate last year was 51.9 p.c.; of the second,
46.1 p.c.; of the third, 62.3 p.c. These ratios
were higher than usual, but on the average
only about 3 p.c. higher. The facts, then,
are: (1) the Re-Insurance’s loss ratio was
abnormally high compared with that of typi-
cal offices; (2) the jump last year was out
of all proportion to that shown by typical
offices. There is nothing very comforting in
those conclusions. The management was
either bad or very unfortunate.
The share capital is £15,317, in 10s. shares
paid to ss. Liabilities amount to £2787,
There are assets to represent the total of
these items less £1663. The assets are very
satisfactory. Four-fifths are deposits and
public securities. The balance is mostly
money out on mortgage. The co.’s policy-
holders have, therefore, very little to worry
about. As for the shareholders, nothing
much will come their way until (1) the
risks are much better chosen, (2) the exes,
are severely cut. Any co., certainly, is
likely to be hit by one bad year; but an ex-
pense rate of 42 p.c. for a re-insurarice co. is
little short of disgraceful.
* * * *
The Life Insurance Co. of Australia :
Total Deficit,
Out- including
Nov., Income. Expenses. Claims, goings. Goodwill.
1912.. £10,989 £18,918 £3715 £22,633 £19,644
Dec.,
1913.. 23,851 19,809 6350 26,159 21,952
1914.. 33,412 23,951 7655 31,606 20,146
For the first time in its life this co.’s in-
come exceeded its expenses and claims. But
that isn’t the same as saying that it lived
within its income. To take one. line only,
the total amount of the policies in the
Ordinary department, according to the re-
port. jumped during the year from £156,195
to £321,440. Now, that £321,440 represents
a liability. The most amazing thing about
nearly all of the life offices which have
sprung up during the past few years is that
they always refer joyfully to the great
growth of policies as though the latter were
simply so many assets. They are the means
by which offices get their income, no doubt;
but they are also and more certainly pro-
mises to pay. What the cos. will derive
from them is more or less uncertain. Nobody
in this case has definitely undertaken to pay
the office £321,440, but the office has de-
finitely undertaken to pay somebody
£321,440. And in order to be able to pay
that amount the office needs to do something
a great deal more than merely provide for
current exes, and claims. It needs also to
put a very considerable amount by towards
meeting the accruing liabilities. Down to
the past year this office did nothing in that
way. Last year it did a little. If in the
current year it does more, and next year
does still more, and then goes on doing
better and better, policy-holders will by-and-
bye get bonuses. The Bulletin hopes that
they will all live long enough to do it. At
present the position is that, of £44,000 put
up by the shareholders, there is only
£23.854 intact. In other words, to get the
present business and pay the few claims that
have already arisen, the co. has consumed all
the premiums thus far received plus £20,146
of the shareholders’ capital.
Admittedly every new life office has a hard
row to hoe. And the trouble which the critic
is up against is that, no matter how strong
his natural inclination may be to give the
young pup a chance, he can’t honestly advise
anybody to put his money on the pup, when
there are so many old dogs on the hard road.
The pup may turn out a prize dog; but life
insurance isn’t the sort of thing you can
afford to experiment with. You can’t say,
“I’ll give the pup a chance, and if, in 10
years, it doesn’t turn out well, I’ll go over
to one of the old dogs.” You can’t afford to
waste 10 years. Besides, in 10 years the old
dog may turn you down.
This, however, can be said for the L.I.A.
—no. L.C.A. The share capital put up was
so substantial that for the present the policy-
holder seems to be well protected. No valua-
tion of liabilities under policies has yet been
made; that is, no actuary has yet been
engaged to determine what amount the co.
should have accumulated towards meeting the
obligations under its policies. But the
amount is certainly not likely to be equal to
the £21,000 or so of solid assets owned by the
co. (It claims £23,854. but included in that
are such whimsical items as £6OO for sta
tionery in hand.) In this respect the co. is
much better placed than almost any other of
the very new offices. It talks now of “a
vigorous campaign for Ordinary department
business, which in the directors’ opinion
offers the best field for a new and progressive
co.” Well, .that is open to question. One
thing seems certain: a name counts for
much less in getting an Industrial or Provi-
dent policy involving the collection of a few
pence a week from the wharf-laborers’ missus
than in getting an Ordinary policy for £lOOO
from a business man. In any case, not even
the A.M.P. or the M.L.C. can go beyond
what may be called its natural limitations
and preserve a decent expense rate. Nor can
this or any other young office ; and the limi-
tations in such cases are naturally very
narrow. It is better to get a small amount
at a reasonable cost than a large amount at
an unreasonable cost. It is all very nice to
read of vigorous campaigns. French and
Joffre would probably tell you that patience
is a virtue, too.

Well, it’s a lucky bank that can come (through
six months of mixed war and drought and
show almost the biggest profit on record.
And it is luckier still if it can do so after
making internal provision for all possible
losses that ought reasonably to be provided
for. In fact, it is coming to this. On the
outbreak of war everybody who had any re-
gard for the public safety said: “Now, don’t
harass the banks. They need a hand —give
it to them. Keep them strong, so that pre-
sently when help is wanted they may be
able to give it.” That was the right attitude.
But presently there will be a suspicion that
the banks are getting the best of the deal.
This, anyhow, isn’t a time when banks ought
to be making more money than usual. It
isn’t a time for squeezing the last possible
i per cent, out of customers. It is rather
a time for giving concessions. If banks show
that their present policy is fetching them in
more money than ever, they will suffer for
it presently.
Compared with 12 months ago present
figures are as follows :
Feb., T 4. Feb., T 5.
Notes and bills . . £5,847 £5,086 Dec. £761
Deposits .. ..1,919,117 1,910,862 Dec. 8,255
Liquid assets .. 599,140 780,423 Inc. 181,283
Advances .. . . 1,740,319 1,555,641 Dec. 184,678
Premises ... . . 55,094 58,003 Inc. 2,919
Thus a profit just about equal to that of 12
months ago has been made with a substan-
tially-reduced risk ; for £184,678 of advances
have been turned into liquid assets, and the
money is either held or has been repaid to
depositors. Comparatively, the position is,
therefore, stronger than it was a year ago.
And, positively, it is strong. The liquid
assets exceed 40 p.c. of the liabilities; and
that is enough to meet any but the most ex-
ceptional contingency. Further, the public’s
money in the business is less than £5 for
every" £1 of the shareholders’ own funds; and
that is a couple of pounds below what is re-
garded as a safe amount to borrow. The
bank is, therefore, very well prepared to meet
either a sudden emergency or a long, pro-
found slump ; and at the same time the mar-
gin of profits over the dividend paid suggests
that the former could stand a severe cut be-
fore the directors would have to do anything
unpleasant.

From one point of view things are not as
good as they look in that first column. In
1911-12, when the profits were £18,665, the
capital for the greater part of the year was
under £100,000; for the past year, when
the profit was a shade less, the capital was
£164,133. And more than that, while the
total liabilities in the earlier year were
about £57,000, the co. is now responsible
for the repayment of upwards of £67.000.
The weight of these liabilities was no doubt
responsible for the passing of a dividend 12
months ago; and it seems to be with some
misgiving that the directors now pay a
modest 5 p.c. These are not the results
that one likes to see from an Australian
manufacturing business. The fact is wages
have got ahead of the Custom tariff; more-
over, the perf;ectly-mad borrowing indulged
in by two or three of the State Governments
—especially the N.S.W. Government—has
created an industrial boom and produced
entirely artificial wages and conditions;
and manufacturers and other employers
are handicapped in consequence. To
some manufacturers it hasn’t mattered
a tin-tack. People must buy hats;
wherefore a hat manufacturer said a
while ago: "They can boom things till wages
are up to 30s. a day if they like so long as
they push the tariff up to allow me with
my 305.-a-day men to compete with
foreigners at 35.” Rut there are other
manufacturers, like Metters, who sell things
that the public doesn’t have to buy ; and a
rapid increase of prices means diminished
trade. Besides, with a lagging tariff, it
means the purchase of foreign goods. When
the borrowing bubble breaks —and it is only
being propped up now —things are going
to be bad for a lot of people who won’t see
that the prosperity of the past few years
has been artificial. But for manufacturers
it isn’t going to be all wormwood.
The Bulletin’s own view is that
Metters, Ltd., put too much into land and
buildings. This comparison shows how
much and a few other things:—

Land and buildings, £88,402 —more than
half the capital. A co. can’t do that sort
of thing and expect to show a big return on
capital at the same time. If, for instance,
it earns 6 p.c. net on the money it has in
city warehouses, it is doing remarkably
well. Factory premises are rather different;
you can’t expect trustees and such like who
are looking for gilt-edged 5 p.c. investments
to put up suburban factories to suit you.
Portion of Metters’s £88,402 is factory pro-
perty ; a lot of it is city showrooms, etc.
This must have a steadying effect on profits
for many years to come ; and the fact that
so much money has been borrowed, partly
because it seems necessary to carry very
heavy stocks, must also steady things up—
especially in a time like this when money
is going to be dearer and trade generally is
probably going to be poorer.

When you are not a borrower in Maoriland
you are a lender; and this co. is in the
market to be either one or the other. Its
own liabilities are £359,788, and its “mort-
gages and other securities” amount to
£524,621, exclusive of business premises,
bank deposits and a few trifles. The posi-
tion as well as 10 years* movement is shown
hereunder: —
1904. 1912. 1913. 1914.
Capital and
reserves . . £136,791 £181,861 £196,996 £199,578
Liabilities . . 287,885 312,324 342,678 359,788
Mortgages, etc. 409,335 467,279 504,454 524,621
Freeholds .. 3,500 12,308 12,453 12,923
Interest owing 5,479 6,097 6,655 7,337
Liquid assets. . 6,360 8,500 16,113 14,483
This sort of business may be quite safe; ail
that The Bulletin can say is that, on paper,
it doesn’t look it. Here is a co. which has
undertaken to repay £359,788, and its liquid
assets amount to only £14,483. The balance
is in “mortgages and other securities.” The
bulk is in mortgages, many of them fixed.
But even if they were not fixed, how could
the co. hope to get in money in periods of
profound depression such as Maoriland has
had before, and must have again? If the
deposits are fixed also for long terms, well
spread, things might pass, though even then
it is doubtful whether it would be possible
to quite safely run the business on so small
a holding of liquid assets. But if the
deposits are liable to be called for in large
amounts within a year, the Maoriland policy
of nailing them down in mortgages is going
to produce trouble again some day. Assum-
ing that it will never be pushed and will
always have time to realise its securities, of
course this co. is all right. Its liabilities
are less than £2 for every £1 of its own
funds. Assuming that its assets other than
mortgages are worth 20s. in the £, and at
once realisable, then there would be £524,621
of mortgages to pay £325.045 of debt. That
is to say, if every £lOO of security brought
only £62 there would be enough for all the
creditors. A very high value has been
put on many Maoriland properties of late ;
but even so, given time to realise them,
there are not many that wouldn’t fetch a
lot more than two-thirds of their present
value. And this co. isn’t at all likely to
have advanced up to the full present value.
In fact, though the war and other things
must have suggested caution, the manage-
ment feel so well satisfied with its securi-
ties that of £5OO set aside for depreciation
contingencies a while ago, it has written
back £2OO as not being required for the
mirpose. Lucky co. if it can’t see a possi-
bility of losing £2OO anywhere!

The Australian Provincial Assurance Association
Limited still continues to make steady progress, and
business continues to come in satisfactorily in all
departments. The directors feel confident that, not
only will the amount of new business written for
the current financial year be considerably in excess
of the preceding year, but that the cost ratio
will be considerably reduced and quite satisfactory;
whilst the renewals received for the first three (3)
months of this financial year exceed those for the
corresponding three months of the last financial year
by 300%. The Queensland Branch collected 84% of
the expectancy of the February renewals, which pro-
bably approached something like a record in this
respect, and reflects great credit on everybody con-
nected with that branch. There is no doubt that
this office is making steady progress, which shows
the energy and enthusiasm displayed by all those
connected with the Association.*

This year’s report is highly creditable to
everybody concerned. While copper and
copper-gold mines all over the continent
were shut down on the outbreak of war, the
low-grade Wallaroo and Moonta managed to
keep its people together. The mining fields of
Australia usually gibe at the Moonta man
as a poor Cousin Jack prepared to take any-
thing Cap’ll Hancock offers him. But in
the long run the Moonta miner is as well
off as most and better off than many. It
is true lie never makes £1 a shift except by
accident; but things have to get shockingly
bad before he is turned adrift. The bowel-
less habit of the London co. is to shut the
mine down in times of low prices, and have
a share-rigging furnace campaign when the
metal market is favorable. Wallaroo and
Moonta doesn’t do that. The co. works on
co-operative principles to this extent —that
wages move up and down within certain
limits with the copper market. Certainly
there is room for a difference of opinion as
to whether the upward movement doesn’t
stop too soon.
The co.’s principal trouble when war
started was that its market was gone. By
means of Protection and the Hunnish work-
man’s habit of keeping his nose to the grind-
stone, Germany collared the electrical trade,
and “Wallaroo” copper followed it. The co.
had therefore to instantly get busy to find a
new market. It succeeded. Meantime, how-
ever, copper had slumped badly ; but with the
co-operation of the S. Aus. Government
(which made advances), the bank, and the
men (who accepted a 20 p.c. cut) work was
kept going. Presently things improved, and
in February the 20 p.c. cut was reduced to
10 p.c. Even with the 20 p.c. cut in force,
and with things going well in the first month
of the second half of the year, the directors
estimate that of the total profit of £39,997
only the £9997 was made since June 30.
The average realised for the copper was
£6l 6s. 9d., as against £69 15s. 9d. the pre-
vious year. This fall of £8 9s. would have
carried away all the surplus but for a saving
in the cost of production. The figures as to
that for some few years past have been :
1907 .. £73 17 9 | 1911 .. £5l 12 5
1908 .. 54 16 8 I 1912 .. 56 18 9
1909 .. 53 3 11 | 1913 .. 56 15 10
1910 .. 54 11 2 ! 1914 .. 51 18 3
The refined copper produced at the smelting
works was 7022 tons, against 7112 tons the
previous year.
The co. now says almost the last word in
mechanical concentration. In the days
before flotation and other recent improve-
ments the figures were poor: last year the
recoveries, excluding those from old material
re-handled, were 95.8 p.c. of the contents at
Wallaroo, and 93.8 p.c. at Moonta. In-
cluding old material the average was 93.6
p.c. And possibly in time a part of
even the 6.4 p.c. may be recovered by cemen-
tation or leaching. During the past year
the Moonta dumps, which nowadays are
kept well scarified, yielded 754 tons of metal
by this very inexpensive process. No esti-
mate of ore reserves is* ever made. A few
people would probably shiver if it were other-
wise. Plans are, however, nowadays pre-
sented, showing, at any rate, how thoroughly
the ore has been cleaned out from the sur-
face down to 2000 ft. in those parts where
it has been found of payable grade. It is
a question now of continuous prospecting;
and that means money, and there isn’t much
to waste, with ore averaging in the case of
Wallaroo a shade under 4| p.c. copper, and
at Moonta a shade under 4 p.c. And there
is no fat surplus of accumulated profits to
comfortably sit back against either, for
though liquid and semi-liquid assets over
liabilities are placed at £171.973, no less
than £84,174 is in that doubtful line “Stores,
fuel and forage.”
* * * *
The Colonial Sugar Co.’s second co. is to
be registered in Fiji, where there isn’t any
beastly Labor party. The idea is to give
the new concern a capital of £3,500,000, of
which £3.250,000 will be represented by 6
p.c. pref. shares issued to present C.S.R.
holders —share for share. The co. will take
the other £250,000 in ordinary shares. The
chairman repeats that the new arrangement
won’t increase the profits; in fact, the .pre-
sent 12J p.c. div. is to he cut down to 6 p.c.
until £195,000 —a year’s pref. dividend—has
been set aside and invested outside the co.
The effect of the proposed transaction will
be to double the capital without calling up
anything from the shareholders; so that a
6 p.c. div. on the new shares and a 64 p.c.
div. on the old shares will be equal to the
present disbursement.
* * *- *
Amalgamated Zinc hasn’t had the same
luck as the Broken Hill Prop., and no Yankee
offers it can get for zinc concentrates are
tempting enough. Therefore concentrates go
on accumulating; and 50,000 tons are already
dumped. The big question was whether
the co. should stop work and dump the raw
product, which is supplied by the North and
South cos.: but big Baillieu and his co-
directors decided that, as long as they can
finance it. it will pay them better to keep
at work and save a second handling.
The prospectus of the Australian Pictorial
Newspaper Co. is issued “for private circu-
lation only.” The capital is £125,000 in
100,000 ordinary and 25,000 deferred shares ;
the object, to establish a ha’penny illustrated
daily paper in Sydney. Figures published
relating to the circulation of cheap illus-
trated papers in London are highly attrac-
tive, and on that account alone the project is
worth considering. The fatal defect in the
present scheme is the basis on which the
shares are submitted. The 100,000 ordinary
shares are offered to the public, who will
be allotted one free deferred share for every
10 ordinaries. The other 15,000 belong to
the promoters. These deferred shares carry
half the profits in excess of 10 p.c. payable
on the ordinaries. In other words, the people
who put up all the money in this hazardous
speculation are limited to 10 p.c. plus seven-
tenths of any excess profits, while the pro-
moters who put up no money at all get (1)
lucrative billets and (2) three-tenths of
the profits in excess of 10 p.c. Further, the
directors may proceed to allotment on 25,000
shares, and the promoters take just as many
shares in that case as if the whole 100,000
were put off. Deferred shares of this sort
were a trick of the last London company-
floating boom, and earned such universal con-
demnation that nobody would now think of
offering them there; and they are a good
thing to suppress on this side also.
* * * *
Dr. Wade, a London oilman, who was
imported to advise the Federal Government
concerning the oil-fields of Papua, conducted
a geological survey of about 1000 miles of
territory before pronouncing an opinion. In
his report to the local Government on the
likelihood of finding rock-oil in South Aus.,
just issued in Adelaide, Dr. Wade has ex-
pressed an opinion unfavorable to petroleum
being found in South Aus., and has done it
without any geological survey at all. Cer-
tainly he qualifies his dictum as to the
south-eastern district, where there is, he
says, “a faint possibility” of oil being
found. How any geologist can express an
opinion worth a pinch of dust in the ab-
sence of geological plans or other data about
the rocks, is a mystery. There are, by the
way, only two geological maps in existence
in the South Aus. Mines Department: one
relating to the Tarcoola goldfield, and the
other dealing with the brown coal area near
Leigh’s Creek. After all, the drills alone
can tell whether oil exists in the sands below
the limestone, say 3000 or 4000 feet down.
Majorca Alluvial (Vic.) has held
what is delicately described as a
stormy meeting. The bone of conten-
tion was the payment to one of the direc-
tors of 500 shares “as commission for influ-
encing the raising of capital.” The discus-
sion turned on the question whether a direc-
tor should be paid for services rendered dur-
ing its formation. Certainly, provided he
hasn’t been already paid indirectly. Most
mining cos. are floated with difficulty when
launched without a current boom; and
whoever influences cash into the co.’s
bank account is surely entitled to
brokerage. The chances are that nothing
would have ever been heard of this squabble
if the manager had not been a brother of
the director. A stranger would have kept
the transaction regular.
■*•***
Want of water has cut battery work down
at the New Golden Fleece, Bendigo; and
the question is asked again whether the
show has a chance. All that can be said
definitely is that on 20s. stone the co. has
made small profits. The grade looks like
improving. If it got up to Sdwt., an air
of prosperity would pervade McColl and
Rankin’s board-room like that noticeable
when New Chum Goldfields entered the list
of div. payers. Two levels —620 ft. and
650 ft. —are being used to work the Golden
Gate shoot on the pitch from 580 ft. The
crosscut at 650 ft. is in likely-looking coun-
try for spurs, and beyond the east back the
co. should have Bft. or 9ft. of crushing
material to handle. So far Bendigonians
haven't done any Perseus hunt after the new
Golden Fleece, but popularity would follow
developments either at the bottom level or if
deeper sinking showed the way to another
saddle.
Andrew Stenhouse, great old warrior, is
the king of Tindal’s, Coolgardie, the show
that is always going to do something- but
never does it. Its trouble is low-grade
stone developed at No. 3 level, and the
presence of so much silver in the gold that
the value is reduced to £3 6s. per oz. To
have a show the co. must cut expenses, or
open up better stone north at No. 3, or
punch into No. 4 level in the hope that depth
will bring improvement. Receipts just
balance expenses, so there is some induce-
ment to persevere. Also there is plenty of
room ; for 20 years’ work on the mine has
only resulted in three levels being
developed.
No one can find fault with costs at Edna
May Central (W.A.). To treat over 9000
tons for a shade over 15s. Sd. per ton is
good work, even though the workings be shal-
low, the mining easy, and the plant new.
The formations carry mostly more than less
kaolin. Therefore everything is passed
to the mill. The dissatisfied management is
alleged to have set apart a special shift to
fervently pray for solid fjuartz. Taking the
profits shown, the supplication should be for
more gold. But in any case they expect to get
that when the No. 3 level is opened ; for does
not A. E. Morgan testify “that the physical
features of the vein, so far as we have ex-
plored it, show the quartz bodies to be very
wide and continuous, and, as we attain
depth, the ore bodies will become more con-
solidated and more massive”? As quickly as
bad air permits a winze is going down, so
that the acid can be applied to the rumor that
the face of the Edna May Co.’s 225 ft. level
shows 2oz. dirt, pitching into the Central.
A. E. Morgan meanwhile has, his eye on a
kaolinised deposit near the shaft. This is
of fair extent and can be sent to the battery
at a trifling cost, to serve as a pot-boiler till
certain probabilities can be demonstrated.
These are (1) that the Edna shoot pitches
into the claim, and (2) that when in tight
country the shoots of stone have payable
gold contents. Skirmishing near the surface
hasn’t settled either point.
* it * *
Mararoa, Norseman, getting low with
ore reserves in its own mine, has cast a net
round a claim near Weston’s, on which it
is spending a hundred or two under working
option. Then it has snapped up, for the
liberal figure of £4OOO, the Hardy Norseman,
east of and side on to its own Mararoa lease.
The purchase was made after manager Hen-
derson had tripped to Adelaide to take coun-
sel with his Board. The Hardy Norseman
will be joined to the Sydney Norseman,
already held by the co., which now possesses
a big length on the main Norseman line, near
where the Viking is yielding rattling profits.
Henderson reports a shoot 100 ft. long and
sft. wide, worth 50s. per ton, being proved
at 170 ft.. with the face in ore. So Mararoa
apparently has something more substantial
to chase than when it went prodding round
at the Ruby Well Options.
The big rise in Empress shares has sur-
prised most people in Westralia. The show
produced about £15.000 worth of gold up
to the end of 1904, and then lay idle
till 1908. Between that time and the present
it was first optioned and then purchased by
a Melbourne co. The nominal capital
(£30.000) is in 60.000 10s. shares, of which
45,000 were offered and are reputed to be
in the possession of the public. The vendors
took £lO,OOO out of the proceeds, and as the
little that was left has since been expended
calls are being made to keep going. Nothing
has been found since flotation to warrant the
rise, and apparently the market is going nap
on the manager’s optimistic belief that at
250 ft. the reef when opened up will average
3oz. The mine has tertainly furnished some
rich returns, but there is no definite evi-
dence that it will be permanently good. The
district has so far proved poor at depth;
Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) is credited in
London with having made a working profit
last year of £62,000, or about £16,000 below
that for 1913. Fully £38,000 has to be
deducted for development and £12,500 for
redemption of debentures, and as several
other items of expenditure have to be taken
into account there won’t be much left for
shareholders. Monthly profits have, however,
been showing an improvement lately, and
in view of this .the Board, which has the
reputation of being rather venturesome, may
take something out of this year’s earnings to
help pay a div. on last year’s results.
* * * *
“Long Gully” : The Bendigo boom of 1870-1
was a boom. Macartney’s book about the
old goldfield says that in the last six months
of 1870 and during 1871 over 1550 cos. were
floated. with a nominal capital of
£18.000,000. The Garden Gully cos. reached
a value of £2,000,000 in the market. Alto-
gether, 30,000,000 shares were issued in the
18 months; Macartney states that they aver-
aged on ’Change ss. each, or £7,500,000 in
all. No fewer than 707 mines were gold-
yielding, and the output was 5000 oz. weekly.
In 1870-1 76 cos. naid divs., mostly from ore
mined above 400 ft. The deepest was Coll-
mann and Tacchi’s 720 ft.
The flotation process as developed by Aus-
tralian metallurgists has caught on rapidly
in U.S.A.. which last year had a bumper
harvest of 533,373 short tons of lead and
360,000 short tons of spelter. Enormous
quantities of tailings and slimes have been
stacked in Missouri, and these are now being
drawn on. This makes it doubtful whether
Australia need expect to find much of a
permanent market for its zinc concentrates
in the U.S.A.
* * * *
South Aus. has got on to opal at last,
somewhere far north in Stuart’s Range. The
Government Geologist pronounces it “in all
respects similar” to White Cliffs specimens,
and produced by the same “desert sandstone”
formation. S.A. has any quantity of this
latter, but so far it had produced nothing in
the valuable-mineral way.
Golden Horseshoe Co.’s litigation with a
German firm has been hung up pending the
decision of the House of Lords in an Eng-
lish case of dealing with an alien enemy.
When the case came before the Perth
Supreme Court Chief Justice McMillan,
whose judgments are rarely upset, agreed
with the Horseshoe’s contention that a
patent granted in the West to Sulman and
Teed was worthless, but was against the
co. in regard to the validity of a Common-
wealth patent granted to Diehl and others.

at Broken Hill. I have had lengthy experience and
can endorse every word other nurses have sa-'d about
Clements Tonic. In forty years I have ha/ the care

WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, March 29, 1915.
My Dear Arini, —
G.-G. Ferguson and Excellency Helen
have mentioned, in writing, that they will
be at St. Vincent’s Hospital concert on
April 10. The Strickland family will pro-
bably be present in bulk, as the domestic
gods are on their way to the Rose Bay camp
from Moss Vale. Mrs. Hugh Ward is put-
ting a head and tail to the programme.
Miss Rose Scott purred gently over Mrs.
Henry Dobson behind the teacups last week.
The party was by way of patting the cheery,
imposing-looking Tasmanian on her shoulder
for representing the National Council of
Women in a recent confab, in Rome. I am
still too dazed by the tale of amendments and
resolutions to know what the exact result
has been. Anyhow, William 11. has not shown
any signs of reformation as a result of the
unfurling of the ladies’ peace umbrella, and
is still the bad boy of the whole Potsdam
family. At the tea party, ere the company
had assuaged its thirst, Mrs. MacCallum
threw a few welcome remarks at Mrs. Dob-
son, who bowed her bonnet in reply. Also
present: the sisters Fry; Mrs. Sara
Nolan, the W.C.T.U. pillar who would
fall upon the last beer-pot and smash
it: Mrs. Jim McGowen; Miss Annie
Parkes, a daughter of the one-time pic-
turesque Premier and once his secretary;
many lights of the Nat. Council; Mrs.
Burley Griffin, wife of the Canberra archi-
tect, a tall U.S. lady with an aesthetic figure
and a sparkling manner; and Miss May
Mathews, a toiler in the Women’s Labor
vineyard.
U.S. Consul-General Bray, who is depart-
ing for Singapore, is first having much
fatted chicken slain in his honor. The Mil-
lions Club, which feeds so many official
visitors. » gave him a mid-day snack. A
political feast at Parliament House is also
on the list. So the U.S. citizen with the
unctuous manner will have several chances
of saying what he doesn’t think of us, in a
voice that seems to percolate through clotted
cream.
The Political Labor Conference, which
has a big feminine chorus, meets this year
in Easter week. One motion on the agenda
paper is that old Government House ought
to go back to its former use as a shelter for
State Governors, the vacant Cranbrook to
be then utilised as a convalescent hospital.
There’ll be wigs on the green if this resolu-
tion is passed and handed in to the Cabinet;
but a few prominent Labor women are sup-
posed to be strongly in favor of it.
Captain Selby Smith, who fell in action
the. other day, was A.D.C. to Governor
Strickland during his Westralian term. He
married Miss Drummond, a cousin of Lady
Edeline, some years ago.
Offley Wakeman, of the Grenadier Guards,
the very mild young man who floated On to
the Strickland Help department during a
tour round the world, slid off it to join his
regiment a while ago. The last English
mail brought word that the flaxen-haired
Guardsman was then just off to meet the foe
in the once-pleasant land of France.
The Rajah of Bong (or somewhere), who
is wandering here as plain Mr. Pudukota,
waves aside official attentions, and pays
his way as an ordinary tourist. He is so
keen to see more of us that he is likely to
have some bumpy moments on our worst
roads. Society, which yearns to clasp a
genuine Rajah, hasn’t been encouraged in
this process. The Indian gentleman didn’t
come here to be stalked for Mrs. Leo
Hunter’s menagerie, and he dodges the social
baits with conspicuous success. Judging by
the name he has taken he comes from the
fastnesses of Mysore, beyond Bangalore—
which is the Bourke or Canberra of India—
and I shouldn’t be surprised if he has seen
bumpy tracks before he came this way.
Keith Brown, who belongs to the new
corps of young men just turned out from
Sydney Uni. with stethoscopes, represents
the third generation of medical Browns in
the once vice-regal village of Parramatta.
Over 60 years ago, grandfather Brown was
the kindly Government medical officer of
the old town; father W. S. Brown nowadays
tends the many local Government institu-
tions between golf and bowls; and now a
youngster is ready to carry on the high
family traditions.
Captain Carr, R.N.. who went down in
the unfortunate Bayano, used to navigate
these waters in the little Psyche. He was
a very popular tar in the days when naval
hops turned a cruiser’s guns into deck orna-
ments draped with bunting.
A serious gathering at Bishopscourt the
other afternoon took the question of the
Ladies’ Home Mission Union to its best
fichu front. Archbishop Wright was in
charge of the meeting. Mrs. Archbishop —a
buxom lady with very dark, bright eyes —
welcomed the visitors in the remote official
way in which a lady dignitary of the Church
always does shake the laity by the hand.
His Gaiters’ sister, Miss Wright, and his
daughters, less aloof than mamma, passed
round refreshments with vociferous hospi-
tality. A limited selection of local Society,
which had torn itself away from bridge and
other home comforts, looked down its nose
while parson Yeates detailed life in the
slums. Mostly it was a sordid tale of the
Wild Girl of the Family and the heathen
Chinee. A collection for the Mission Fund
put a silver top to the proceedings.
Wonder who’s the Australian barrister
that gave up £5OOO a year to enlist in the
first Australian expedition'? He has been
buying carpets at prices that made an
Egyptian bazaar-dealer wish a few more
Tommy Kangaroos would come his way. The
Daily Chronicle’s special Cairo correspon-
dent, who went with him to the non-bargain
counter, appears to have been almost as much
impressed by his affluence as the dragoman
who chaperoned the shopping expedition.
Three Egyptian carpets—two valued at £5O
apiece, a third at £lO0 —the result of the
morning’s shopping, ought to be just about
unpacked by now.
The girl with a retrousse nose is going, to
look her best in the new cocked hats, which
are small and turn up everywhere. Thus
everything will be en suite, as they say in
the upholstery business. Some of the round
black headgear is rather larger than a
small tart, and it sits on youthful craniums
with a pronounced tilt to one side.
Dr. Gilruth is passing the time of day to
Sydney friends as I gum this up. He re-
turns to Darwin and his N.T. friends and
enemies forthwith.
Ellen Terry’s tragic struggles to see half-
inch print ought to be safely over by now.
On leaving Australia the famous woman
went to the U.S.A.; but she had to stop short
in her tour, and go to New York for an
operation for cataract. The poor lady’s
panting efforts to get enough light for her
readings here made those entertainments a
nerve strain for most of the audience. There
is no cable of disaster, so the cataract has
probably been removed with success.
Mrs. Keith Jopp’s Sock Fund committee,
with Mrs. Harry Finn on the look-out for
your immediate contribution, is rushing to
a grand climax. By April 24 the knitting-
generals hope to have organised a huge con-
signment to send away to the 50.000 pairs of
Kangaroo soles that are likely to be stamp-
ing over foreign soil in May. Packets of
boracic powder, to dust on sore places, go
with each woolly comfort. Boracic, thank
goodness, shows no signs of emulating the
recent crisis in cream of tartar.
Mr. H. D. Baker, a one-time U.S. Consul
here, has just acquired the rocky billet of
special Commercial Agent for the Stars and
Stripes in Petrograd.
Fanny Durack, N.S.W., who is the world’s
champion mermaid, will only go to
the U.S.A. for the July carnivals provided a
strictly amateur atmosphere prevails. The
passionate desire not to make money at sport
is a beautiful thing from a feminine point
of view, but I asked “Snowy” R. L. Baker
what he thought about it. The owner of
four motors and many other desirable assets
said with a sigh that he, too, was an amateur
once. “There’s a kind of glamor about it in
youth,” he added, “but it runs into a lot of
money and doesn’t pay the butcher.” If
Miss Durack would break into the profes-
sional ranks, there’s a sure fortune, it seems,
waiting to fall on her lap. The Movies
alone would put furs on her shoulders and
pearls around her neck. But for the
present, anyhow, the Sydney mermaid is
inclined to turn her back hair to the voice
of the siren.
It was “Snowy” Baker who gave Annette
Kellerman her first diving lesson. It was on
a Sunday afternoon in the old Woolloomooloo
Baths, after the men had gone. Annette, then
14, climbed up to a 45ft. high plank,
and R. L. Baker poured instruction into her
ear. She missed her cue or something, and
turned over in mid-air, falling flat on he r
back. Her alarmed instructor ran down to
pick up a corpse, but found instead a- very
live Annette, clamoring to try it again.
Which she did, making-three perfect dives
in succession. This determination has
pushed the Australian mermaid into a
world’s cluSMnion lady-fish, with a salary
only expressed by several figures.
Mrs. Ellis Rowan’s water-colors, now
showing in Anthony Horderns’ Gallery, were
formally introduced the other afternoon by
Francis Suttor, M.L.C. Manager Gillett’s
spectacles flashed at this ceremony, which
occurred about half an hour after the com-
pany had “done” the pictures in the usual
record time. Mrs. Rowan’s brush wanders
over acres of Australian flora in this exhibi-
tion of about 140 pictures. She tracks the
shy wild blooms of tropic jungles to their
fastnesses, and serves up banks of orchids,
fields of native heath, and groves of wattle,
extra large. The artists’ home is at Mace-
don, Victoria, and various studies of the
vegetation of the fashionable Mount are a
consequence.
The heights and the depths of Vaucluse
shelter a number of successful city men.
The ladies of the Red Cross Society have dug
these people out, and have put the spurs into
them in the interests of patriotic effort- —and
money. There is quite a lot of money, which
is not being given by our wealthy men, and
the market is also not overstocked with male
effort; and the Vaucluse women are out after
both. Apart from the usual weekly socks
and pillowslips they are running a big art
union, which is now on a fair way to fur-
nishing a motor ambulance, and their next
by-product is to be a Gigantic (with two
G’s) Red Cross Carnival, to be held .at the
Stadium, Rushcutter’s Bay, on the night of
April 13. For this event, which is being-
smiled upon by the whole Red Cross move-
ment, the charmers have roped in the Sta-
dium, and R. L. Baker, and Massed Bands-
men, and Swordsmen, and Acrobats, and
Bayoneters, and Riflemen, and Lightning
Sketeliers, and Hugh Ward, and the theatri-
cals of the Williamson and Tivoli circuits.
Even the Governor, though so far he hasn’t
promised to do as much as a Turkey trot.
Dr. and Mrs. Ashburton Thompson and
her daughter Norah Simpson are going to
England. As chairman of the State’s Health
Board in the time of the bubonic plague,
Ashburton Thompson, who is an authority
on the subject, as he is on leprosy,
helped to strangle the beast most effect-
ually. His stepdaughter Norah, who dab-
bled in art in Datillo Rubbo’s studio, had a
little souvenir thrust into her keeping the
other day; and fellow students who clus-
tered round wished her a rosy future with
her brush.
Mrs. Ethel Phillips Fox, after working as
energetically as a nest of ants for the artists’
war fund, has been called on to go slow.
Sitting at the receipt of custom in the R.A.
Society’s hot-air chamber left her a very
limp artist. A limited number of tickets
are still for sale, though the business-like
Francis Chapman, father of the youthful
Evelyn, has been selling them by the
cwt. No artist gets even a tram-ticket out
of this show, which is still spread out in
the R. A. Society’s rooms. The subscrip-
tion system appeals to philanthropists, who
will swell with pride when they see the
framed results on the parlor wall.
At the tableaux in which the art students
are going to help the artists swell their War
Fund, Florence Rodway’s golden hair will
be a-hanging down her back. She is to
stand (or sit, maybe) robed in fluffy white
of the early Grecian bath-sheet period, with
a large gold sun behind her. As “The Golden
Fleece” I suppose she is Mrs. Jason looking
for the rain which the thirsty Riverina is
gasping for just now.
After all the columns of print which the
debutante’s white plumage provokes in the
press, Man in the abstract is apparently
u'nmoved. He believes that a debutante is
something young and good to look at, but
he is not certain about the color of her outer
shell. Therefore artist Julian Ashton puts
“The Debutante” (now hanging on the R. A.
Society’s walls) into a blue gown, sticks a
pink rose in her hair, and adds a touch of
green to one shoulder. There’s no reason
why she shouldn’t wear these clothes, instead
of the usual white shimmer. It’s an artistic
digression from feminine tradition.
Mrs. Ivirkham (Kate Rooney) writes in
enthusiastic adjectives of the personal
appearance of “Little Billy, ”. otherwise
Ivirkham junior. After alluding to her
Treasure —who is still blissfully ignorant of
the terrors of cutting back teeth —the Aus-
tralian singer mentions such small affairs
as musical engagements. Kate recently
appeared at the initial performance of two
plays in connection with the Irish Theatre
in America. This was at the Central Opera
House, New York. After folding up iter
letter, the popular contralto was to travel
to Boston for a short season. She hopes
to bring Little Billy to Australia this year
to see his grandparents.
The wedding of Esther Harris, only
daughter of the lawyer who has such a
pleasant way of telling Sydney Judges the
unpleasant things the police know about
their prisoners, took place in Essex some
weeks ago. The groom, who follows the
usual occupation, is E. Vernon Hine, of the
Royal Fusiliers.
Optimists hope to rake in about £5OO to
the Belgian fund as the result of the Jewish
children’s fete in the Manchester Unity
Tlall. Consul Watteeuw opened the show.
The rush for cash on the part of the juven-
iles in charge is something to be remembered
in a chastened spirit by mere Gentiles. The
combined ages of president and vice-presi-
dent might total about 28. As a youngsters’
patriotic attack on the public purse, this
show, which raged for two days, is going to
take a lot of beating.
Mrs. Parker, a sister of Kitchener —she
used to live in Maoriland —has been laying
down the law (you expect a firm attitude in
K. of Iv.’s relative) as to the value of clubs
for the wives of soldiers and sailors. At
Chatham the lady appears to have wiped the
carpet with any other lady who ventured to
disagree with her. She claims that these
circles —mostly of tired women, with little
children clinging to their skirts—show great
aptitude at sewing and knitting for the
cause. The speaker quoted the experience
of Australian women who run similar
clubs. One Australian woman—who would
be cast into outer darkness if I breathed her
name —could tell Mrs. Parker something. At
a big gathering at the Royal -INaval House,
there were two volunteers to do what they
could for some patriotic sewing-spree. And
even those two admitted they would have to
learn how to use knitting needles.
Frances Dumaresq, an Australian M.A.,
B.Sc., took the London Teachers’ Diploma
at Bedford College (Eng.) a while ago.
Nowadays everybody is supposed to throw
something into the common stock-pot, so
this blue stocking is working among the
Belgian refugees in camp at Earl’s Court,
opening up to them the mysteries of the
English language.
Another Australian, Miss Ethel Tucker,
M.A., daughter of the Melbourne professor,
has lately won a First Class in the Classical
Tripos at Cambridge. It sounds awe-in-
spiring ; yet Miss Tucker is enjoying excel-
lent health, and means to put many more
stitches in her particular brand of stocking.
In the February issue of the Royal
Colonial Institute Journal secretary Boose
gives about three, pages to his recent
Australian tour. A guarded reference to
various Ladies’ Leagues the wandering
Boose encountered includes particular men-
tion of the Adelaide branch of the Victoria
League. The English reader will decide that
Australia is a land flowing with honey and
whisky, for the number of banquets and
smaller feasts mentioned by the traveller are
legion.
Captain Harold Peek, who belongs to a
Maitland family, has won a delicious pro-
mise from a Lancashire lass —Miss Mabel
Hacking, the only daughter of parents who
live at Ilenfield House in the manufacturing
county.
Ilayley Lever, the Adelaide artist who
has been on the other side for some years,
has got away with the Carnegie gold medal
for his picture. “Winter, St. Ives.” The
big canvas, painted from the artist’s own
doorstep, lias been on view at the National
Academy of Design in N’York.
Some of the exhibits for the Red Cross
stall at the Easter Show have a
tendency to under-rate the chills of
an English summer. Many of the Belgian
youngsters’ frocks are made of cottons suit-
able for our own sunshine. England, which,
like the north of France, doesn’t have its
summer properly aired till the end of June,
can do with lots of warm clothing for over
nine months in the year. In the Red Cross
show, one feature of the doll exhibits will
be miniature Belgian Nightingales in pale
grey robes, with red capes. They’re made
up as pincushions, and you can stab them
with a packetful of pins and they won’t
squeal.
Artist Watkins has just finished a large
portrait of ex-Minister for Education Car-
michael. It’s a good likeness; but the
most striking thing about it is the canopy of
figtree which rises behind the sitter —the
pale green ghost of the slaughtered Moreton
Bay herbage that used to shade the utter-
most end of Macquarie-street.
Mrs. Tom Raine is now the hon. caretaker
and organiser of the Belgian Babes Kit
Society, in place of Dr. Mary Booth. The
latter has resigned to take up other work,
and goes to England. Miss Raine is hon.
secretary at mother’s elbow.
Pianist Yvonne Leverrier (who is Mrs.
Chauvin in domestic life) and brother Frank,
K.C., are in mourning for their mother. The
lamp flickered out gently the other day, just
before it reached the 78th milestone.
Elsie Greene, a daughter of the late landra
wheat-grower, has caught the khaki, feyer.
She is working it off in a British Hospital
for Belgian and other wounded soldiers at
Southampton.
Mrs. Harry Chisholm and daughter Sheila
fled from London to Egypt a few weeks ago.
Young Chisholm was in an early crop of
Australian khaki that was larded on that
variegated patch.
Solicitor Whiting, of Sydney, recently un-
packed his baggage in London. With him is
his sister, Mrs. Chambers —wife of a de-
stroyer’s skipper who has been very busy
since the guns began to boom. The Sydney
lawyer has taken seriously to the goose-step
with the Inns of Court Corps.
Artist Thea Proctor keeps her pencils
sharpened for action in a Chelsea studio.
The handsome Mosman girl was one of the
exhibitors at a February show of the Modern
Society of Portrait Painters.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, March 29, 191 A
My Dear Yarrie, —
Belgian Flag Day hit Melbourne in the
right place and made it gush cash as Moses’
rock gushed water. It was a day of mad-
dened trams. (Melbourne trams and old
gentlemen have an extraordinary capacity
for fury.) People would stand in the way
of the venerable arks till they gnashed their
bells and so made a lane for themselves
through the crowds that listened to the auc-
tioneers who gabbled in the busiest thorough-
fares. The workaday folk who couldn’t afford
to loiter had to travel blocks out of their
way. Melba began the day by giving £l3 for
a threepenny flag. Then she mounted an
auction block and offered cigarettes at any old
price. A foolish rumor that the lady was
going to sing ran through the streets, and the
stampede to hear her under the worst condi-
tions for next-to-nothing was immense. The
long-handled parasols now carried by the
fashionables did considerable damage to eyes
and tempers. In a sudden hen-rush these
absurd things, carried crosswise at the slope,
are amongst the deadliest weapons known to
civilisation.
The Tivoli people went at the job for all
they were worth. They sang in the Block
for charity from 10 o’clock to six, and then,
for charity’s sake, knocked off. During the
wild hours of song a corset shop offered some
ducky pink body-armor for auction outside
their door, and dragged golden pounds from
the scrambling men. The real fun will be
when the owners give their sweet gifts away.
What women will do for love is nothing com-
pared with what they will do for corsets.
The highwaywomen were full of energy
and resource. They were rigorously restricted
to beats, and the ones that were allotted the
shady side of Collins-street nearly froze.
Lesly Madden had the Royal Bank corner to
herself, and was impressive in her Rule Bri-
tannia style; she was hung about with flags
of every size, and her hat was surmounted
by three puce-purple ostrich plumes like the
royal trade-mark of Wales. Lady Stanley
wore one of her hats, and drove
round the bazaar-like streets during the after-
noon, buying flags at hold-up places. Irma,
the daughter of the lath Leon Caron, was a
particular success in a cream and pink outfit
and a motor-car, wherefrom the pretty little
girl sang various national anthems with a
large, sweet voice, and sold Belgian flags at
auction. Starting in front of the newspaper
war-bulletin boards, she was subsequently in-
vited to the Stock Exchange. Her own col-
lection-box was worth £l3O odd at the end of
her toil, and there had been other boxes cir-
culating among her audiences. It was viva-
cious Irma’s day out.
The hand of sympathy goes out to A.D.C.
Fane and his pretty wife, who have just
had news from the Cold Country of the
death of one of their quartette of babes.
Troubles did not come singly in this case,
for Captain Fane has a bullet in his lung,
and his fa mil y acres have been comman-
deered for Defence purposes.
Vanda Clarke, of the rich Mount Schank
family, has said “Yes” to the devoted Nor-
man Hodges, who, you will remember, did a
little Gent-helping to pass the time while
Vanda was away on her “finishing” tour.
Now Norman is going to the war, and the
engagement and wedding-cards are fluttering
round together. The wedding is to happen
early in April. Vanda (how that name sug-
gests moving !) brought several moth-banquets
of clothes from foreign places, so she is not
likely to be bothered much in preparing her
trousseau.
As might have been expected, Vanda
Clarke’s engagment ring is_ a sparkling tri-
umph. It has gumleaves of small diamonds
surrounding two monster brilliants of the
size that can only be afforded by heiresses
and bookmakers.
The 50 artists who have given pictures to
be gambled for at five guineas each showed
their wares in the Upper Athenaeum for the
first time on Thursday. Lady Munro Fergu-
son got off a long and inspiring speech about
Red Cross and Art, while standing at a
central table directly in front of a huge
Teague painting of Queen Bess. Bess was
upholstered heavily in gorgeous gold velvet
and jewels; the vice-regal lady looked much
cooler in tussore, with a pink rose in her
tulle hat. Fox stood modestly at her Ex.’s
elbow, and a small child, with vivid red hair
decorated with a big blue bow, sat well in the
picture. I don’t know who the important kid
was. Perhaps she was a Belgian refugee on
exhibition. A long, sleek Gent. Help kept a
portrait-smile alight all the time while the
big organ in the Town Hall next door purred
away like the reawakened Sosh Tiger. All
the artistic High Lights were grouped in a
graceful semi-circle. Mrs. Edward Dyson
made a startling entry, with a black-velvet,
bonnet on her fair hair and a big red bow
on her parasol.
After the show her Ex. descended the
stairs with the air of a female conqueror,
and dived into a little grey motor with a gold
crown. The expensive contrivance immedi-
ately snapped its doors on her and rushed
her off on more Red Cross business.
Cecil, a son of Henry Berry, was married
the other day to Miss Merritt, a daughter
of Major Charlie Merritt, of Brighton. They
tried to do it quietly, but the marriage of
one of the important Berry clan couldn’t be
furtive no matter how craftily the partici-
pants strove. Later on Mrs. Merritt gave
a shivoo at the Francatelli to welcome her
daughter home from the mooning. A big-
crowd mangled their beautiful dresses
squeezing up the Francatelli steps to storm
the private breakfast-room. Mrs. Howard
Berry looked a superb vision in something
or other, and Mrs. Jack Scot Sharp was
dazzling in something else. Some Gibsons
from the Apple Island were also in the
friendly squash.
The young Cholmondeley lord who married
Bess Tobin, a Melbourne heiress, before
hurrying off to the war, has put into a
Boulogne hospital in a damaged condition.
He hopes to be about again and taking new
risks in the spring.
Gordon Chirnside, brother of the Ama-
zonian Gladys and son of the lady who is
now Mrs. Hodges, has been made A.D.C. to
General Birdwood, the Kitchener man who
is bossing the Australian troops in Egypt.
Of the pair, one rather expected to hear that
Gladys had a military job. The fine, upstand-
ing sister has always kept a solicitous look-
out for her rather delicate brother.
The Spowers, who live in Toorak and on
the Argus, have farewelled Alan, the only
son, who is going to Europe to volunteer his
services. He learnt how to stand in line
and form himself into a hollow square at
Geelong, where he got his first taste for
fire-eating as a Grammar School cadet.
One of these days Pattie Scott (who was
Pat. Lindsey Russell before marriage hap-
pened her) may produce a bright and sooth-
ing novel with some rale Irish color in it—
the sort that sells in Mayfair. The rushful
lady writes from London to a Melbourne
friend :
I’ve just finished two new books (unseotarian,
thank Ilivens!), and am off to Ireland for a whole
six weeks, to be the guest of the Countess of Clan-
earty at Garbally Castle, in the south-west country.
I should get some interesting- material The war
has smashed up a great many hopes and dreams of
author and authoress —hard luck for them! So far,
I haven’t suffered. Ward, Lock and Co. have about
trebled my earnings. My hubby has a prac-
tice here; also, a surgery in the sacred vicinity of
Harley-street. We’re living in Hyde Park—rather
charming, but threatened with bombs, etc. And
to think I once nearly fell in love with a German!
M.v hubby has smashed his left arm rather badly,
and that’s finished his chance of being trotted off
to France.
The rest of the letter contains complimen-
tary references to Australia, whither the
writer hopes to trip before Christmas. Hav-
ing- apparently wrestled with the demon of
sectarianism in her, and got the monster
down, Lindsey Russell ought to go ahead in
the light-satirical Avay. She has the pen of
the ready romancer and a fine contempt for
conventionalities.
Mrs. Henry Stead has motored over to
Sydney for a brief three-days’ visit to her
soldier brother. She has relatives by the
dozen in the fighting line, but this brother is
the only one she has been able to hug in
farewell.
The Wrixon dame’s reverend son-in-law,
Smith Macßain, is going off to the war on
soul-tending duty. He had his baptism of
fire (shellfire, not hellfire) in South Africa,
where, as shepherd to an Australian contin-
gent, he was voted a jolly good fellow by
his lambs.
You really cannot be too careful in cases
of this kind. A man got himself engaged to
a girl in a small Vic. township some weeks
back, and a few days ago he married her.
It was some wedding, as we say in the Low-
lands, and the little chapel was to be appro-
priately decorated with all the vegetables in
season. Judge the state_ of mind of the con-
tracting parties and their blood relations on
finding this legend set up conspicuously
above the pulpit: “Abandon hope all ye who
enter here!” The culprit pleaded good in-
tentions, but the fact remains that she, too,
had had a bid for the bridegroom.
General Public is an ungrateful boss.
After slogging away for months as secre-
tary to The Bungalow Art Union, Mary
Brenan is asked through the press various
questions such as _ “What became of ticket
02261 ?” But Miss Brenan doesn’t quail.
She has all the fine points of the art union
business at her fingers’ ends, having dealt
with numerous Druids’ galas under her late
father’s jurisdiction. After all, the proof
of success is the profit, and it is understood
that some £3400 will accrue.
Louise Dwyer, a daughter of old £ £
Smith, gave her Scotch husband a novel
birthday-present last week. Instead of buy-
ing him a box of cyanide cigars, she arranged
a motor picnic for 300 poor children and
took the many-happy-returns man out to
witness the rejoicings. He said it was the
best he’d ever had.
Fred Rowe’s daughter Jessie, who put up
her pigtail and went into grown-up dresses
in order to marry Mr. Richards, of Colombo
(first door on the right as you go in), is
over here for a holiday. She took a run
down to see mother, instead of having the
usual Kipling holiday in the hills. Her
husband bosses the steamy wireless station,
and is too often on duty to be much of a
home comfort at present.
The Carts and Kafts—no, no! The Arts
and Crafts, who are on the job in aid of the
Belgians, are quite cheerful about the pro-
spects of next week’s auction. May Vale
has given them an interesting gift for sale.
Ic is a souvenir of the first performance of
Barrie’s “Little Minister” in London, at
the Haymarket—a portfolio containing 17
sketches by Phil May.
Mrs. Tom Ledingham has always been
keen on helping the home for insolvent dogs.
However, this year the money is to be
diverted and sent along to the needy Bel-
gians. A bazaar is now being plotted, and
will happen (d.v.) on May 8 at a private
house in Toorak. Mrs. Ledingham has her
coach-house stuffed full of toys and art pro-
ducts for a special stall.
The zeal of Miss Nyulasy in laboring to
help the Belgians has been rewarded by
receiving appointment of Mistress of the
Ice-creams at a coming show where the
microbe of ptomaine will be the only guest
to receive the cold eye of disfavor. She is
also organising a patriotic bridge party,
whereat the winners, having been honorably
decorated with flags, will hand the losers’
money over to relief funds. This is a new
idea, and your Johanna hasn’t yet seen the
scheme in working order —but the effect is
said to be fearfully exciting.
Down at Hamilton and thereabouts, a
dusty country cousin tells me, even the
magpies are dying of drought. It isn’t
thirst they are perishing of, but hunger. Why
can’t the poor things come to town and live
on the superfluous afternoon cake-crumbs
their human sisters let fall?
Esther Patterson opened her picture show
on Wednesday in the Centreway, and had
such a crowd of feminine and other visitors
that the pictures were mostly blotted out.
(Art must always be counted socially success-
ful when it is obliterated.) Mrs. Andrew
Fisher arrived and brought a flattering mes-
sage of regret from Andrew, who was away
in Brisbane. Mrs. Pearce tried to look at
the paintings and mentioned that her Sena-
tor husband had promised to look in with a
cheque book on his return from Sydney. Mrs.
Josiah Thomas was another visitor. One
dame who bought on a wholesale scale took
a great fancy to a particular girl study, but
implored the young artist, as one generation
to another, to “please paint out that cigar-
ette !” Mrs. Dwyer could not put in an
appearance, but, like a dear woman, she sent
flowers and a moonstone pendant, for luck,
to the young artist.
Ruby Dixon, the singing girl from St.
Kilda, is to marry this week. The bride-
groom-elect is Roy Goodison, whose late
father used to deal in softgoods in Bendigo.
On Friday night Lloyt’s. the picture
people (who is really one people-—dentist
Arthur Russell) gathered an audience by
invitation for the first performance in the
new screen-palace next to the Theatre Royal
in Bourke-street. The place is as gorgeous
as you might expect, and Freddie Challings-
worth, the director —a vision in an evening
dress-suit of the Immaculate brand—made
proud remarks to the guests, and the guests
hadn’t any difficulty in dulv applauding
them.
At a recent function Mrs. Pickles, of Syd-
ney, the handsome mother of airman Stanley
Pickles, related proudly how her son fell
300 ft., broke his leg, came out of hospital in
only three weeks’ time, and at once resumed
his' job. This sort of triumph makes the
mothers of mere groundlings throw their
bonnets behind the piano and resolve never
to go tea-drinking again.
Reggie Power, who is Rupert Clarke’s
son-in-law as well as himself, has given up
the job of Master of the Melbourne Hounds.
I understand he intends to get away back
to his country acres arid wring the soil for
shillings until it screams for mercy. There
was another rumor that he was going to the
war; but that seems to have died away like
a summer breeze.
From “Rosna,” in London: —
Melbourne artist Dora Meeson Coates is one of the
officials who enrol and direct London’s women police
volunteers. The she-Robert gives two hours a day
to training and two more to duty in such places as
the chief of the Robertinas may direct. She isn’t
supposed to chase the bad burglar, or dog the
wily murderer, but to keep a friendly eye on women,
children and other incapabies. George Coates, Dora’s
husband, after serving as a special constable, is now
driving a motor ambulance in the great Bill-broking
business on the other side of the Channel.
The widow of the late Judge Butler, of Welling-
ton (M.L.), has just died here. The family came to
Britain four years ago, as the younger portion of
it had ambitions to attend to. The one son, who
has been studying the higher architecture, has turned
warrior and gone with the Seaforth Highlanders.
Second-daughter Marjory, after a year or so at the
Tree School of Dramatic Art, slipped into the pro-
fession by way of a provincial tour in Greek drama.
She also toured lately as Lady Wetherbv in “The
Walls of Jericho,” and Mrs. Rosmore in “The Lion
and the Mouse.”
Birmingham is suffering an invasion of mumming
Australians at the moment. (If you can’t talk in
war terms you’re no one these days.) W. S.
Percy has his own company playing “A Cold'
Douche,” while the panto, raging opposite contains
Fred Leslie, Ivy Shilling (who plays second boy as
well as principal dancer), Hope Charteris (princi-
pal girl), George Millar, and musical-director
Howard Carr. What more can any Birmingham
want ?
The hunting folk are likely to topple if
they start chasing the smelly animal as
usual at Easter. The going is now so hard
in the usual hunting districts that heavy
rains are needed to make the game worth
playing. If there is no rain, the animal will
have to be left alone or pursued by bailiffs
in cabs.
The Buchanan who married Miss Alawa
Robertson and all her possessions is one of
the glad sights of Melbourne these dull days.
He turns out in full Highland costume and
escorts liis wife to the door of her female
club of an afternoon. Then with his tribal
blanket over his shoulder the hare-kneed
hero marches away with imaginary pipers
squealing before him. It is all very splen-
did. but I really don’t know why he does it.
The women of the Repertory Theatre Club
are kept busy these days preparing for a
Belgian sale of gifts on April 9. This is an
addition to their tveekly Red Cross parties.
And, still unsatisfied, some members are
arranging entertainments for Broadmeadows
Camp. The Club, as a whole, is contem-
plating a public reading of Ibsen’s Peer
Gyn.t,” by Rose Quong, with Greig accom-
paniment played by Eleanor King. (Inter-
val for refreshments and congratulations.)
And now the knock-out! “The Trojan
Women,” by Euripides, is being set to music
especially for the Reps, by Marshall Hall 1
Most of the characters in this are women, so
you can imagine the excursions and alarms
that will precede the burst.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.
“Albion”: “Vigilans” (B. 4/3/T5) is talk-
ing through his neck when he reckons that
Dick Stewart, manager of Mitchell Downs
station, drove two emus in a rickshaw dur-
ing the drought of 1900-2. The notorious
Dick never drove emus, but he often drove
a bullock and a mule harnessed together
in a buckboard.

A GOOD REMEDY FOR
SALLOW COMPLEXIONS.
Everyone may have a clear and bright
complexion, free from furrows or wrinkles, if
they will only take a little daily trouble in
the following manner : Buy some Puritol wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply every
night, lap a little cold water bn the face ten
times with the hands every morning, allow-
ing it to get thoroughly round the closed
eyes, and pinch the skin very gently across
all wrinkles. Puritol Wax Soap, which
leaves a fine film of wax on the skin, should
be used ; this acts as a protective during the
day. This treatment arouses the dormant
nerves and blood vessels, and undoubtedly
accounts for many of the clear complexions
so much admired by those who do not yet
know the secret.* t 3
