POLITICAL
POINTS
Put in a few plain words, the judgment
of the High Court in the wheat case is that
trade between the States must be permitted
to pass free, but that any State has a per-
fect right to stop it.
Some good thing may yet be done by ex-
publican A. A. Farthing, semi-Ministerial
M.L.A. for East Melbourne. He has been
indicated as the possible bringer-in of a
Totalisator Bill when the long-loafing
Assembly assembles. Thanks to party gov-
ernment and fear of the wowsers, Labor
members plead that they can hardly be ex-
pected to introduce a Bill if anybody else
will. Liberalism is feeling the same qualms.
So, as Farthing is the nearest approach to
a professing Independent in the House, he
may be induced to do what dozens of others
would rush if they weren’t afraid of getting
the whiskers of their precious parties
singed.
Attorney-General Hall (N.S.W.), submit-
ting his State bakeries proposal to the kind
consideration of the people, explains that of
course the trade will be spot cash. Why?
Isn’t it a good thing to get into debt?
DER TAG
More about what may happen to the eyes
of Australia was let out the other day by
a bankrupt who stated that, at the age of
20, he opened shop as all optician in Sydney.
He had picked up something about the
business while in Perth, “and no qualifica-
tion is necessary to practise in Sydney .” He
“did well in the business; his turnover for
one year amounting to £2500.” It seems
about time that the State Government ful-
filled its promise to see that some qualifica-
tion is necessary.
A co-operative farm has been started at
Mordialloc (Vic.), under the auspices of the
Women’s Political Association. The Pank-
hurst miss is giving advice. So is Vida
Goldstein, as well as various other promi-
nent advertisers in the Political Woman
movement. The concern is run by a pound-
a-share association, and the farm-laboresses
are to receive “a small salary, with a
bonus.” Six women are already on the
small salaries. It is proposed to open a
central depQt for the sale of produce such
as eggs, onions, violets, tomato sauce, horse-
feed, pot-herbs, knitted socks and plum jam.
This, then, is the Pankhurst-Goldstein
scheme of salvation for women —small sal-
laries in the present and vague bonuses in
the future. The Bulletin seems somehow
to have heard of it before. In fact, it
seems to have heard of it associated with
bitter curses.
A DIFFERENT CRAFT.
STRANGER TO DISTRICT: “ 'Sense me callin
you brotherbut I see you're one of we.”
COUSIN JACK INHABITANT: "I'm what?”
S.T.D.: “ You're a Mason .”
C.J.I.: “ 'Ee's all wrong. I'm a hinjin
driver.”
The other day, while opening a new in-
fants’ school at Richmond, Victoria’s . Edu-
cation Minister Livingston also unveiled a
portrait of Robert Bell, a former scholar
who was killed in the Sydney-Emden action,
and expatiated on “the sacrifices we were
ail called on to make for the sake of the
Empire.” Then he added that he and his
fellow-Ministers favored increased education
facilities, but it “couldn’t be done out of
revenue. We must borrow.” Even the
portrait must have grinned.
The advocates of the totalisator believe
they can get a Bill through the Vic. Assem-
bly if all the Labor members vote for it;
so the bookmaker recognises that it is time
to get in somewhere where he can deal with
the Labor movement. It is only a coinci-
dence, of course, that at a recent meeting
of the Port Melbourne branch of the P.L.C.
the list of individuals proposed for member-
ship included the name of an energetic mem-
ber of the Bookmakers’ Association.
The dispatch of the Expeditionary forces
threatens a new problem, or the intensifica-
tion of an old one. Australia has always suf-
fered from an unequal balance between
sparsely-populated country areas and bloated
metropolitan districts, and the inequality
becomes more pronounced as the country dis-
tricts are drawn on to provide most of the
recruits. This would not matter much if the
cities were engaged chiefly in turning the pro-
duct of the country into manufactured goods ;
but a monstrous proportion of city people
are engaged in merely handling goods and
making them dearer without increasing their
value. The time may arrive when it will be
necessary to start a new campaign of enlist-
ment, and put men into training for work on
the land in order to keep the backbone of the
country from degenerating into a myth.
Cocks, M.L.A., an ex-Gaud Mayor of
Sydney, by virtue of that and his glossy tile
appeared before the Victorian Housing Com-
mission the other day and made some char-
acteristically Cock-and-bull remarks. The
gist of them was that the slum was right
enough; it was the slum dweller who was
wrong. “Get hold of him and make him
work.” said Cocks, who went on to lament
that the Sydney slum inhabitant is “unem-
ployable and a wastrel,” had “lost his self-
respect and didn’t care what happened.”
Having delivered himself of this ponderous
observation. Cocks went out to have a cigar
and forget that he hadn’t really touched the
problem; for the problem consists of the
enormous number of children who, as long
as there are slums, will grow up in their
foetid atmosphere, with scarcely a hope in
the world.
Peacock’s Greater Melbourne Bill follows
that of the departed Watt to a large extent,
but varies in one important point. It hands
over to the larger body control of tram-
w T ays, lighting, fire extinction, water supply,
sewerage, markets, rivers and creeks, parks,
abattoirs, etc., as in the earlier measure; but
as to the constitution of the Council, Pea-
cock divides the metropolis into 30 districts
of nearly equal population, and each is to
return one member on a one-ratepayer-one-
vote suffrage. This is less advanced than
it should be, but is far ahead of Watt’s
plural voting proposal. Possibly the near
approach of the reversion of the cable-tram
system may assist the Bill through Parlia-
ment ; but the equal suffrage principle will
first move the House of Fat Landlords to
tears of bitter woe.
What a shirker Propputy is! The shirk
that created the infamy of Hospital Satur-
day, and sent out a mob of young girls to
bandy jests with old and hardened sinners
in the street, in order that by cadging Willie
Smith’s loose coppers they might save
Propputy from paying for the upkeep of
hospitals, finds its parallel everywhere, and
nowhere more noticeably than in connection
with the war. Seventy-five per cent, of the
men who have gone to the front have no
attachment at all to Australia except the
very close and dear one of children; yet
they are willing to give their lives, while a
cheque for three figures is beyond the
patriotism of by far the most of the men
to whom Australia stands for a big income.
The Northern Territory proposal to ap-
point a couple of members of the House of
Reps, to keep in touch with things up there
and be special advocates, is on the lines of
one of the earliest movements towards Re-
sponsible Government made by N. S. Wales.
Just as the Territory feels that it is not
understood in the Federal House, N.S.W.
felt that it was neglected or misunderstood
in the British Parliament. Joseph Hume,
leader of the Radicals in the Commons, was
the first to suggest the appointment of some
member to look after N.S.W. interests.
Daniel O’Connell, as an alternative, sug-
gested that some prominent Australian
should be sent to England by the colonists,
and found a seat in the Commons. The
name of W. C. Wentworth was mentioned,
and O’Connell promised to provide him with
a seat for an Irish borough. At first Went-
worth was agreeable. Resolutions were
carried at public meetings in Sydney that
the deputy’s salary should be £lOOO a year,
paid out of colonial revenues; but Bourke,
at that time Governor, refused to allot
money for the purpose. Later, Wentworth
backed out, and Bulwer, an English M.P.,
was appointed. Bulwer was kept primed
with N.S.W. intelligence by the Sydney re-
formers, and Governor Bourke forwarded
petitions and other formal documents
through him. His services were of the high-
est value; but he was greatly impeded by
Edward McArthur, a son of McArthur, of
Camden, who, though not a member of Par-
liament, held an office in the Lord Chamber-
lain’s department, and was appointed by
the Sydney Tories to act as their mouth-
piece. The Northern Territory, starting off
like N.S.W., is likely to continue the analogy
by puzzling Parliament with contradictory
statements, for there are many Tories there
or thereabouts who want miles of freehold
and heaps of cheap labor.
NOT UNDERSTOOD.
THE TEMPERANCE ADVOCATE (whom circum-
stances have forced to put up at a pub):
“Er, will my bedroom be near the bar ?”
MINE HOST: “It will not! An’ if that’s the
sort of wummon fare, out you go! "
Denham in his first address to the jury in
Queensland says that “Liberalism and
Socialism have the same object in view.”
Joe Cook, who claims to know all about
it, insists that L stands for Liberty and S
for Suicide. Australian recent experience
is that one stands for £ and the other for $.
Tim Coghlan’s term as N.S.W. Agent-
General ended on March 31, but he will carry
on until such time as Holman can make
up his mind whether to be or not to be.
And he will have to do it pretty slick or
be deafened by the clamor of those who
themselves pine to be.
THE MAN WHO'S GOING 10
SUFFER MOST.
Victoria’s Price of Foods Board has at
latest tackled the price of milk. It hasn’t
finished the job yet; hut what it doesn’t
seem to have noticed is that (1) the milk
market is quite unaffected by the war; and
(2) if it fixes the sum which the farmer
may demand for his cow-juice, it should be
equally its duty to say what he shall pay
for his fodder, seed oats and maize, manure,
implements, and. above all, land. The
Board tells the farmer what it considers
the utmost he may ask for wheat and butter
and milk. Does it think it would like to
tell the landowner the highest figure he
may ask for his acres? And if not, why not ?
The war is still raging; public feeling is
bitter against the Gerhog; very heavy penal-
ties are awaiting the man who is discovered
even attempting to trade with the enemy.
Yet Attorney-General Hughes says he has
in hand the prosecution of about a dozen
people who have been willing to take all
the risks. And about a dozen cases have
already been dealt with, and heavy fines
imposed. If this happens under present
conditions, what chance is there of stopping
the trade in German manufactures after the
war, except by a tariff much higher than
the goods have to climb over at present?
ANDREW FISHER AND THE DROUGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In every instance a drought has been followed by
good seasons, improved flocks and herds, and pro-
ductivity generally. I have no doubt that Nature
will take the same steps after this dry spell.
—Andrew Fisher.
If your tanks are dry and your land is parched,
Don’t worry about it at all;
Keep your eyes aloft and your backbone starched —•
Next season the rain may fall.
Don’t think of the mortgage that soon falls due,
Or a season’s wasted toil;
llemember the green of the grass that grew
Years back on the same brown soil.
If the time is short and the danger near,
And our hosts are ill-prepared,
Don’t shake with alarm or blanch with fear,
For our flags are nicely aired,
And we talk of making a railway line
To carry to God-knows-where
The half-trained troops that we know will shine
In thle battle of Fool’s Despair.
Don’t think of the size of the foeman’s fist,
Or the weight of his marching boot;
If the worst befall we may still exist
In despite of rapine and loot;
For the times may change and we yet may stand
To wallop the foeman out,
And luck may ransom a wakened land
From, Fisher as well as drought.
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.
THE LOOSE-MOUTHED GRENADIERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Send the boxers to the Front. — “Biceps” (Argus
correspondent).
The great number of young men at the races not
in khaki. —“Australian” (another of them).
Australians are very slow in volunteering. Any
old fool.
Some talk of Alexander, and some of Hercules,
But never were such heroes as those heroes at their
ease
Who chase the other fellow to fight away their
fears—■
The Generals-on-Paper of the Loose-Mouthed Gren-
adiers.
In vestry and in synod their splay lips froth and
bleat;
They hate the stalwart citizen who walks along the
street
Upon his lawful labors, while they give three
British cheers
And flap the flag and shout their brag—the Loose-
Mouthed Grenadiers.
And some are parsons, some are ancient maids with-
out a man,
And all are snobs who want their names among the
“also-ran,”
The cads who chase advertisement by giving ginger-
beers
To the troops who sail to fight the wars for the
Loose-Mouthed Grenadiers.
All through the land they skite and brag, they fight
by word of mouth,
At Toorak and at Collingwood, and Melbourne that
is South,
While their General, the Argus, leads —with jingo-
istic tears —-
The charges at the Melbourne Club of the Loose-
Mouthed Grenadiers.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The simple faith of the punter is really
touching—-to McMoees. It rises superior to all con-
'-siderations of experience, to all the whispering
reminders of busted trust wafted down by the moan-
ing winds of the past. And so the fatuous crowd
which takes material for guidance at second-hand,
-and gives ready ear to the fugitive, elusive tip, is
rushing in as usual at the early doors with cash to
risk about this, that or the oilier nag for the Don-
caster and Svdnev Cup. The odds on offer are ruin-
ous, of course, but what matter ? Both events are on
paper as open as the Western plains; but, still,
what matter ? The crowd with money to burn is
readv to take the risk, and the children of Israel wax
fat and glad. For the Doncaster Wooralc, by brilliant
galloping in his work, rules at the moment of wilting
a pronounced favorite, Traquette having been deposed
from that position in consequence of her disappointing
display in the Rawson Stakes at Rosehill. Unless the
filly can greatly improve on that form next Saturday,
her chances in the big mile are worth nothing - . The
writer of this paragraph believes she will, and that
wherever Woorak is Traquette will be in front. The
race has, however, been almost invariably one ot
disaster to favorite popular selections. Not since
Wakeful’s year lias a favorite won, and in 25 years
the favorite has only landed the bacon twice. Spurn.
Flash of Steel, Giro and Jmshi, lower down in the
list of probable starters and on the wagering card,
look more likely than the heavily-burdened, shorter-
priced idols to furnish an investment a little less
blitheringly insane than most. On Ulva’s Isle money
has been consistently heaped for Sydney Cup. This
son of Positano has’ on the whole, shown fine track
’form, but one gallop late last week made many good
judges reflect that 9st. is a good, solid weight to
hump along over two miles, with Carlita, Moonbria
and Tan liter running behind. The Bulletin, weigh-
ing everything with strict detachment, goes bald-
headed with the tip that on the Doncaster and Cup
the general public will get it in the neck, while
Noses wins in a walk.
jc. * * *
“0.K.”: At time of writing five Victorian
possibles in the Sydney Cup arc interesting propo-
sitions, though not one of them has a commanding
chance in comparison with St. Spasa (who, as a
voung 4-year-old ought to have improved a lot since
he won the Metrop. ), Ulva’s Isle, Cisco and Carlita.
The Victorians are Hush Money, Onazer, Sheriff Muir.
Wallachia and Wishing Cap. The Wallace colt.
Wallachia, and the mare Hush Money are in the Don
caster Handicap as well, hut the breeding of the
one and the conformation of the other suggest that the
two-miles Cup will suit them better than the Don-
caster mile. Hush Money has always run as though
10 or 11 furlongs were as far as she cared to go, but
she is just the cut of a stayer, and if she shapes
only decently in the mile race she may still be worth
considering on Easter Monday.
“0.K.”: Although the colt is under sus-
picion of being a rogue, I would as lief take my
chance with Woorak as with anything else in the
Doncaster Handicap where the more sober-going
animals want a bit of luck to help them. Backers
of Woorak can at least hope that, with Derby weight
on his back, he may get away with the lead and
keep it to perhaps the finish. One thing is certain:
the colt has never met Traquette on such favorable
terms as in the Doncaster Handicap. The filly has
to give him 41b. instead of getting a sex allowance.
If I were bound to back an alliterative double I’d
take Woorak for the mile race and Wishing Cap for
Sydney Cup. The sturdy Victorian gee is nothing
if not a stayer. It isn’t the weight that will beat
him, but his slowness off the mark. In the last
two miles of the Australian Cup he must have been
giving Lempriere fully 75 yards start, yet he fin-
ished second. Given a muddy track and a fortunate
get-away, Wishing Cap might actually win the Syd-
ney Cup, though he rarely wins any sort of jace.
John Wren’s 2000-guinea purchase, Gar-
lin, started earning oats on Saturday by capture
of a handicap event at Hawkesbury, where, carrying
9st. 51b., he ran clean away from Royal Laddie,
Toast, Hobble Skirt and other useful goers. The
time given for the mile, lrnin. 39sec., suggests that
the watch was first away. Carlin was solidly backed
for the Doncaster last week, and after his win at
the Hawkesbury punters were busy bearing golden
offerings to Israel.

*■ * * *
This paper arises from the ruins to
wearily remark that Fanny Duraek has broken
another record. The smash occurred at Newcastle
on Saturday, in the Ocean Baths, and the smashed
was the property of Miss Vera Neave (Eng.). Since
October 8. 1913, that young woman held the world’s
record of 7min. 52sec. over 500yds. Fanny, as before
said, has shattered it; hut she was good enough to
replace it hv one branded 7min. 32 3-ssec. Inci-
dentally, Miss Duraek will shortly show Uncle Sam
what sort of a salmon she is, provided arrangements
are made to preserve her amateur status during a
tour proposed by “Snowy” Baker’s agent (Unmack)
at San Francisco.
•¥■ * * *
Another Australian has ambitions to swim
the English Channel (if it isn’t filled up with sunken
German warships the morning after “The Day”).
He is Gunner E. S. Loney, of the R.A.A., Fremantle.
The other day he swam from Guildford to Perth, a
distance of about 18 miles, by way of practice.
* * * *
“Over”: The howl that lias arisen in
N. S. Wales and elsewhere in Australia against the
dullness of cricket has caused Ma’s Cricket Associa-
tion to hatch a clutch of schemes to shake some life
dnto the game, hut none of them are likely to per-
ceptibly improve matters. The experiment likely to
cause the most interest is that of lengthening the
over to eight balls. This will start next season if
The other States agree. The objection raised to this
‘is that it will be too hard on the bowlers. Maybe
it will on young or “rough” bowlers, but to the
'generality it should he a help, especially to the man
“on the job.” Still, it’s doubtful if ericket will he
“brightened” by its adoption. There’s no question
that play in Australia in 1914 was below standard,
hut it seems to me that in making that estimate the
critics overlook the fact that the public’s sporting
tastes, like many of its other tastes, are changing.
The stereotyped games that satisfied grea't-granfer
are going to receive a shock —-some of them have
already received it. Observe what happened to the
old Rugby game when League football sparkled into
■SN. S. Wales a few years ago! And see how baseball
is winning through to the front! The Australian, in
Short, is turning to the sports that suit his nature,
and cricket is bound to be made aware of the fact.
Tlie new system of reckoning the position
of teams in Sydney’s first-grade cricket competition
comes in for criticism. The end of the final of the
ordinary rounds showed that North Sydney and
Western Suburbs were together, with 65 points each,
although the former hail won four single innings
matches as against five won by the latter, each
winning two matches outright. Also, University and
Sydney, who were next, were likewise together with
60 points, the former having won six games (one
outright and five on one innings) and the latter four
games (three outright and one on one innings). The
team with bowlers who can get an opposing side out
quickly apparently scores a big advantage; but the
points allowed for draws and single-innings’ defeats
also count. Each match won outright is worth
three points; two are allowed for a single-innings
victor}'; and one and a half to each team when a
draw is plaved. It was anticipated that the big
advantage of' the full win would encourage fast play,
but it seems to foster slowness. Thus University,
with 331 to get on the opening of a second Saturday
against N. Sydney preferred to play for a safe draw
rather than risk “going for the runs,” which might
have been obtained. With a draw ’Varsity was
certain of playing in the A division for the next
round. And that’s how it turned out.
Canowindra (N.S.W.) is vociferously
cheering Stanley Kirkland, a youth who this season
has accumulated 1042 runs in 17 innings (five not
outs), an average of 56.83 per innings. There are
only three centuries in the list- —which tells of the
evenness of the plugging.
Most of the Vic. tennis players stayed on
in Adelaide for the local championships; also Rice
and Parker dropped in from Sydney, and the former
proved a little too good for all the local men. Rice
beat Fitchett (Vic.), 3 to 2 —a very close thing—
and Parker, 3 to 0, and won the final, 3 to 1, from
R. V. Thomas, who had beaten Roy Taylor, 3 to 0.
Rice and Taylor got together for the doubles, and
beat Raws (Vic.) and Thomas. The Vic. ladies were
altogether too good. It was Miss Waxman’s week out,
and she won from Miss Sharp (the holder) and Miss
Stewart, Vic. champion of only the other day. In a
first-class doubles handicap Clem Hill met Whitty in
the final. The batsman beat the bowler. Veterans
FANNY DURACK.
were prominent all the week. Even the champion
Rice is a veteran. Playing with their own sons as
partner were Roberts, Thomson, Hone, Rowe and
Fitchett —this last a Vic. inter-State pair, the junior
member of which gave Rice his hardest game of the
week. On the other hand, the new blood which S.A.
rather badly needs was seen in Schubert, a_ 14-year-old
who won not only the under-17 championship but
also, with a moderate handicap, two men’s events as
well.
N.S.W. Lawn Tennis Association is going
to be over-run with inter-State visitors this month.
Victoria, Tasmania and Queensland will each for-
ward a consignment for the Ma State champion-
ships. S. Aus. has not made up its mind, but its
inclinations are entirely favorable. A Maoriland
team is also expected. The proceeds of the inter-
state matches will give the Belgian Fund a lift.
Melbourne Argus (26/3/T5) : “Finishing
a game in this way is about as satisfactory as win-
ning a steeplechase after the opposing horses have
fallen at the jumps.” That was a reflection of the
paper after seeing Mrs. Brook’s method of winning
A class singles (Vic.) at croquet.
From first to last of 20 solid rounds at
Svdney Stadium on Saturday Albert Lloyd, a lithe,
sinewy iad who learned what he knows of stoush in
the tent of a boxing showman round the country
districts of N.S.W. and Queensland, showed the way
to Walter Coffey, a latelv-iniported U.S.A. middle-
weight. Coffey, who belongs to the Lippe kennel,
has in his kit a special punch—a low, driving blow
into the region where an opponent keeps his dinner.
He used it not long ago in various American cities
with great unpleasantness. This and a fine big heart
, he brought along to the Stadium, but in the stressful
hour which followed he somehow or other mis-
laid the thump. It was by virtue of a fine grit
alone that he weathered the storm. The endurance
of these Yanks is really remarkable. Coffey was so
continually punished that by the eighteenth round
he was practically done, but be kept gamely to his
task, and in. the twentieth, though so badly rattled
that his legs almost, refused their office, he came
on and saw the disturbance through. Lloyd, who
was making a first appearance in the ring, was vigor-
ously cheered, and some enthusiastic admirers found
themselves so elated by his win that they fell on his
neck and kissed him. But the applause for Coffey
was no less cordial. He certainly made a gallant
fight of it.
Lloyd’s showing, on the whole, was good. He
was much the faster of the two, the better boxer
and far the cleverer at close quarters. With training
—he has had very little —and intelligent instruction
he may yet go a long way. But he has several bad
habits in the ring', and a style that will certainly,
unless corrected, get him into serious trouble. Ilis
attitude is far too tense, with the result that his
deliveries lack power. Lloyd’s first business, there-
fore, is to learn how to box with freedom, to get
down off his toes and to use his head to think with.
Apparently he seems to imagine his cranium can
bo employed as a third hand, and he is not altogether
too scrupulous about the use of his elbow. Coffey'
felt the weight of the Australian’s head far too
often on Saturday. Unless Lloyd attends to these
matters with pious intention and considerably recon-
structs himself, then he can look forward to the
certainty of becoming as tiod-up as a draper’s parcel.
Being yet in the plastic stage—he is only' 21—and
having the physique and instinct for the game, the
immediate pursuit of scalps may advantageously be
postponed until he cures his defects.
* * * *
Coining events: Ees Darcy and Demlin;
Fritz Holland and Jeff Smith; Hardwick and Mick
King. Of these the first scarcely appears a very
judicious arrangement, for though Demlin is a plucky,
capable boxer, few people would expect to see him
cope successfully with Darcy. The engagement
between Holland and Smith, however, will be looked
forward to with anticipatory pleasure by followers of
biff. Holland has been rather unlucky in recent
appearances, but Smith’s reasons to quarrel with luck
have never been particularly substantial. After his
disturbance with Holland Smith will probably next
be matched with Darcy, the meeting lately arranged
having been broken off in consequence of a demand
from the American corner that adjudication should
be by a Board of Three instead of a single referee.
Brisbane was provided with another brief
and melancholy assault at the Stadium on Saturday
night, when Frank Ellis devoted some attention to
the late Joe Russell. The reception he was given
when he climbed into the ring was stirring enough to
make Joseph come back, if anything could; but
Joseph was too deceased. Ellis played round him
like a cat with a tortoise, excepting in the- third
round when he received some sour body dope. Early
in the fifth round Russell was passed a two-horse
clout on the cranium and went down; rose again;
was supplied with its twin ; down; up ; biff! Towel.
vc *' *
On the other hand, ivielbourne Stadium
had a 20-round show supplied by Messrs. John Wil-
liam (vulgarly known as “Jack”) Jannese, ex-ama-
teur bantamweight champion of Australia, and since
1913 a much-commended pro., and George Pearson.
Jannese, on points, had it all over the other high
contracting party, and if his weapons had been a
trifle more indelicate the K.O. would have thrilled
the congregation. Pearson was floored in the fifth
and seventh round, but managed to revive and go
the whole distance. He was greatly in arrears when
the balance-sheet was made up. * *
The seuliing-revival fever in N.S.W. has
abated, and only one item on the big list of pro-
bable matches is anything of a certainty. That is
the mixed double-sculls championship, which will be
paddled on April 24 between the Kemps (brother
and sister) and Pearces (brother and sister) for the
title. The Towns-Felton squabble about the stake
seems as interminable as the hoop of a beer-coffin;
Ben Thorough good and Billy Ripley are still sparring
for a tussle for the heavy-boat championship ; George
Jack and W. Paddon are in disagreement as to
where to hold their race; and other challenges are
hung out to dry.
As Felton doesn’t seem overwhelmed with
anxiety to row Charlie Towns for less than a £2OO
stake, the latter has cast his eye upon the bashful
William Webb, champion of Maoriland, whom Hannan
is trying to coax into clamping down the match ten-
tatively arranged between them for May on the
Wanganui. Towns is eager to race while the time
is ripe, and offers to take on Webb for a stake of
£l5O at Sydney, and allow him some small amount
for expenses, the difficulty is that discretion is the
better part of Webb.
Victoria and South Aus. Baseball Asso-
ciations have decided not to support the venture of
the N.S.W. Assoc, to bring out Stanford (U.S.A.)
’Varsity baseballers for a month’s jaunt in Australia
at a cost of £1650. That settles it.
* * * *
A hint as to the way baseball is creeping
into public favor in the Ma State is found in
the balance-sheet of the ’ball Association. The
credit balance was £ll6 better than in the 1914
season. For the coming session St. George will be
lifted into the senior division, and there are indica-
tions that the entries in the lower grade will be
more numerous than hitherto. The game appears to
have picked up a portion of the support which fell
away from the Rugby Union when that body’s power
of attraction was so heavily undermined by the
League. Also, the flatness of cricket during 1914-15
drove numerous people to seek distraction elsewhere,
and a considerable proportion of those not driven to
hard drink gave the diamond game a trial. Since
when they have used no other.
At the championship meeting of the Vic-
torian amateur athletes on Saturday an attempt was
made by R. H. Watson to break Thorpe’s (N.S.W.)
time for the 1000yds.—2min. 15 4-ssec. He con-
tinued to the far tape after winning a tear-away half-
mile, but failed to do better than 2min. 21sec. In
addition to the half-mile he won the quarter-mile
championship. The club championship at the meet-
ing was collected by University (21 points), fol-
lowed bv East Melbourne and Carlton (18), Caul-
field Tailyho (14), and St. Stephens (9).
J. O’Farrell, who defeated champion A. ,T.
McDonald for the 10-mile Victorian amateur cycling
championship recently, carried the colors of Eastern
Suburbs (Sydney) in the Dunlop inter-club premier-
ship last year. He is a youth who is likely to dis-
tinguish himself in the rush for Australian cham-
pionships. * * *
Even the .Flying Pieman’s record has
been fractured. Sixty-seven years ago that all-round
sport jumped 50 hurdles, 2ft. high and 10ft. apart,
in 6min. 25sec. ; collected singly 50 stones one yard
apart in 14 minutes; walked a mile in llmin.;
wheeled a cart half a mile in 1 llmin. At Bathurst,
during St. Patrick’s Day Sports, Frederick Jennings.
52 years of age, tore into the Pieman’s games for all
he was worth, with the result that the Flying party’s
reputation suffered by llmin. He was wal-
loped in every department—smin. 18sec. chopped off
his cart act, llmin. off his mile walk, 3min. 55sec.
off his hurdle stunt, and 30sec. off his velocity in
collecting rocks to bounce on Huns’ heads or some-
thing. In fact, his name in future will be the Mud
Pieman.

* * * *
Coming after liis recent displays of
hazards off the red, George Gray’s match with Steven-
son looked very like an ordinary gate-money game.
The side-wager of £5O was too small for words.
Gray has recovered his lost ‘’form,” and being able
to make breaks of 1000 and upwards with ivory
balls, he will always be suspected of playing light
with any English cueist who keeps close to him in
a game of 18,000 up. His mastery of the losing
hazard stroke from baulk gives him a crushing advan-
tage over the best of the artists who can beat him
at top-of-the-table play.. When Inman and others
beat him, months ago, he was all to pieces. He lacked
the nerve necessary for long-sustained efforts in the.
first half of the game, and naturally he was unable
to get going with his macliine-like stroke as the end
grew near, and his opponent was far ahead. But now
he should always have his opponent in trouble before
half the distance is covered. The man who has to
sit still and get cold whilst Grav makes three or
four hundred hazards in succession is practically
beaten. And Gray, when in fair form, can hardlv
fail to achieve one or more of these disheartening
breaks during the first week of a long match.
THE TRUTH
ABOUT INTERNAL LUBRICATION.
The public who have been using an
internal lubricant in the shape of some form
of mineral oil or petroleum jelly to relieve
constipation are finding out that this whole-
sale lubrication of their internal organs is
destroying one of Nature’s most vital fluids
■ —the bile. Nature finds its work being done
by a foreign agent, grows less and less active,
and at last ceases to put forth any effort.
Just as a strong arm put in a sling and
given no exercise becomes weak and useless,
so the continual lubrication of the internal
organs by mineral oils will surely weaken
and reduce the flow of bile. It destroys the
natural movement of the bowels, because it
supersedes the flow of the bile ; and, there-
fore, the taking of Liquid Paraffine in any
of its forms is a source of future danger to
anyone so foolish to be carried away by
what is nothing but a most unfortunate fad.
Again, there is an exceedingly important
feature in connection with the taking of
mineral oil in the system which many have
never given a thought to, and undesirable
results are certain to follow if this practice
is continued. Is there any doubt that the
cells of the stomach through which we draw
the elements of nutrition also draw and ab-
sorb into the system this mineral oil? There
is no doubt of this at all, and also the walls
of the intestines which are covered with the
lubricant absorbs this oil as well. The
entire internal. system is, therefore, absorb-
ing mineral properties foreign to the human
system —properties that cannot help, sustain
or nourish the body. Is there any medical
authority who values his reputation who
will say this is a wise or safe course to con-
tinue?
To put it as clearly as possible, the liver
can be compared to a big sponge full of
holes. It becomes covered over with a slimy
substance, and these holes or ducts, from
which the bile flows, become stopped up; con-
sequently the bile is forced back into the
system, producing two direct results: (a)
a derangement of the entire system, and (b)
constipation, because the bile cannot act on
the waste matter in the intestinal canal, and
it remains in the system. This is what is
commonly known as torpid liver. Something
is wanted to get at the cause —something
■ that will act on the liver and open up these
ducts. Not something that will simply carry
off the wmste in the system, leaving it in just
the same state to repeat over again just what
has taken place. No poisonous mineral pro-
duct will take the place of the bile given
each human being by an All-wise Creator to
carry the waste matter out of the system
and aid in the digestion and assimilation of
our food. People resorting to this mineral
oil —either in ignotance or not, and practi-
cally saying we have no further use for the
bile, we have something to relieve constipa-
tion, that is all we care —will pay for their
ignorance or impudence in thinking they can
improve on the Creator’s work.
Sometimes Nature only requires a little
assistance to get over an obstruction, and if
something is used to merely give that assist-
ance and not usurp all Nature’s functions
and take the entire operation out of Nature’s
hands, then valuable help is being rendered.
This is where the invaluable properties of
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are apparent.
They do not usurp Nature’s functions, de-
stroying all their natural activity, but when
Nature needs help to carry its often too
heavy burden, caused by over-eating and
drinking, then Dr. Morse’s Indian Root rills
assist Nature. They act on the stomach,
and, above all, on the liver, causing the
stopped-up ducts to release the flow of bile
that has been withheld. It is this flow of bile
that causes the bowels to act, and any remedy
that ignores this fact is causing damage to
the great human mechanism. It is the con-
stant and regular flow of bile that aids iu
the digestion and assimilation of food, and
when this flow ceases from any cause the
system at once is affected throughout, and
constipation, of course, is the inevitable
result. , _ ..
For nearlv a century Dr. Morses Indian
Root Pills have been in use as a remedy for
liver ailments. They get at the cause. They
open the ducts, and allow the bile to flow,
aiding and assisting Nature, not in any
degree superseding or weakening the natural
functions. A pill occasionally produces regu-
larity and good health, and. with reasonab e
care regarding overtaxing the digestive
organs with either food or drink, a healthy
condition can be maintained. This is the
secret of good health in man. woman and
child, and there is no getting away from it.
Any fad that is based on the principle that
it can take the piace of the bile is a fraud
and a danger, and should be
where it belongs—back into the oil tank fo
lubricating machinery, not into the human
system.*


When Language FaiIs—ELAND’S WHIPS!
WVt
3 rcu: “I say. Bill, it’s your turn to yard the
horses to-morrow. Get a move on bright
and early.”
I>jll : Bight-o, Ted, but I’ve got no whip.
Bend me yours, will yer V”
lim: Yes, Bill, but take care of it. I don’t
want to lose that whip.”
NEXT DAY.
Bill: “I got the cuddies in, Ted, but
I’ve lost the bloomin’ whip!”
Ted : “Look here, Bill, you’ll have to
spar up for another. I had to
send to Walther and Stevenson’s
in Sydney for that whip. It was
one of Eland’s. You know George
Eland—he was a musterer like us
blokes, and learned how to use a
whip before he ever made one. I
wouldn’t use any other whip than
Eland’s. They’re beauts —all
Kangaroo—square dinkum. Don’t
ring the changes, Bill. Eland’s
whips have saved me language!”

THE SECRET OF A HAUNTED HOUSE.
Some years ago near Bendigo
I did halt on a country road
Prepared to camp like a weary tramp
Neath the roof of a worn abode,
That rookery old said a yokel Bold
Is haunted by noisy sounds,
Take my advice and you think twice
Tis a risk worth a thousand pounds.
Tis dueed rot I fear it not
Said I as I crossed the road,
Prepared to rest and make the best
Of a night in the accurst abode ;
In the front room I lay hushed in gloom
When aroused by a rattling sound
I struck a light and braced up to fight
The haunter who prowled around.
Like a silent thief I did softly creep
To fathom the mystery out
The cause of the din was a sheet of tin
In the roof a flapping about.
. ...O. Back: Not well enough done. . . .C. 8.: Weak
defence made weaker by versification. ... T.P.T.:
On the contrary, observe our respectful grief! We
lift our hats as the poem’s funeral passes.... Effie
C.:
I cannot brood another day!
My heart is brimming o’er !

T TAVE you ever stopped to consider what it has meant
to your nation—to Australia—at this time of war
to possess successful Manufacturing Industries ?
The possession of Small Arms and Ammunition Factories, Engineering and
Coach Works, Boot and Hat Factories, Woollen Mills, Jam and Biscuit
Factories, Meat Preserving Works, etc., has permitted the full equipment,
clothing and provisioning of the Largest Expeditionary Force which has
ever been transported across the seas from hemisphere to hemisphere. It
has enabled Australia, self-relying, and with pride, to make immediate
and practical expression of her loyalty to the Empire.
But the production of munitions of war has not measured the full value
to Australia of her manufacturing industries. The manufacture of Harves-
ters, Furniture, Pianos, Locomotives and the thousands of other goods made
in Australia, has assisted in the flow of commerce necessary to her military
and naval life.
The importation of cheap German goods has not assisted in the establish-
ment of these manufacturing industries. Nor has the importation of German
Pianos assisted in the development of Australia’s resources.
BEALE PIANOS AND BEALE PLAYER-PIANOS
are made in Australia, and afford direct employment to over 500 Austra-
lians. The capital employed in the business is entirely Australian, the
Directors and Managers are all British, and the few supplies required for
the factories—less than is required by any other factory in the world, except
two—are secured within the Empire or the countries of the Allies.
BEALE PIANOS AND BEALE PLAYER-PIANOS are specially
manufactured to suit Australian musical requirements, and over 21,000
customers testify to their quality and durability.

Adelaide’s electric trams were six years
old tliis month, the first line (to Kensing-
ton) having been opened in March, 1909.
The Trust, which represents the munici-
palities, claims that gross revenue has gone
up from £105,000 a year with horse-cars to
£329,000, and traffic from 13,000,000 passen-
gers to 43,000,000 ; also, that it has paid in-
terest on purchase money and put an adequate
£131,000 to sinking fund, and has a balance
in hand.
Bernhard Dernberg, one time German
Minister for the Colonies, asks in the North
American Review:—
Will it be believed that a nation that has been
constantly striving tor peace, has never expanded
except peacefully, and never acquired territory except
by treaty, would go wilfully and light-heartedly to
fight nearly the whole world?
If Dernberg considers that Hanover,
Schleswig-Holstein and Alsace-Lorraine were
acquired by peaceful expansion, the inva-
sion of France and Belgium would natu-
rally seem “strivings for peace.” What the
Belgian thinks of the matter may possibly
be different.
Taking over the bakery business of the
State, in the interests of the people, sounds
a fine, large enterprise, and it has its merits.
But numberless vote-catching schemes sug-
gest that hardly anybody will benefit outside
the metropolis. As with most other of this
Government’s boons, the city will get the
cheap bread, the country won’t. Supposing,
under present conditions, the Government
can bake and supply good loaves in Sydney
for, say, 3d., the result will be that resi-
dential sites within the cheap-bread area
will be in still greater demand (to the
greater glory of the landlord), while the
unserved outer districts will be more neg-
lected than ever. In short, the cursed con-
gestion policy will get another boost. This
isn’t an argument against eliminating waste
and giving the city cheaper bread. But it
is a reason why a really patriotic Govern-
ment should pay first attention to the
cheapening of bread and other necessaries
outside the Sydney area.
“Reka”: A very old and romantic page
of Maoriland’s early history has been under
scrutiny lately, as the second volume of
Robert McNab’s records nears publication.
Somewhere about 143 years ago the French
vessels Mascarin and Marquis de Castries,
commanded by Marion du Fresne, anchored
near Kororareka, in the Bay of Islands, and
pitched camp on Motorua, now called
Marion Island, where still can be seen the
remains of their smithy. Trouble arose with
the Maoris, and du Fresne and 26 of his
men were massacred. Crozet, du Fresne’s
lieutenant, wrote an account of the affair.
This French party formally annexed Maori-
land as a French colony, and buried on
Marion Island the records of the event. The
old account says:—
On the 12th July, 1772, we sent a bottle to be
buried on Marion Island, in which were enclosed the
Arms of France and a formal statement of the taking
possession of all this country, which was named
Austral-France. This bottle is 4ft. under the earth, at
57 paces from the edge of the sea, reckoning from
high water-mark, and at 10 paces from the little
stream. This bottle was buried with all necessary
precautions.
The buried bottle has been regarded as more
or less of a myth at the Bay of Islands,
but the patient, thorough-going McNab has
made several long and unsuccessful searches
for it. Workmen have dug and delved for
him in all likely and unlikely places, and
failed to find trace of it. The main trouble
is that nobody is able to locate the high
water-mark of 143 years ago, and there are
now two “little streams” in the historic bay.
Also, as nobody can quite judge another
man’s stride, and four feet underground 143
years ago may mean 10 feet to-day, the
bottle and its interesting contents are ex-
traordinarily elusive.
A Melbourne soft-goods firm has struck
what it doubtless considers a ’cute plan to
encourage recruiting. On the wall of each
department it has hung a card inquiring:
“What will you say when your son asks
you, ‘What did you do at the war, Daddy ?’”
The Bulletin doesn’t know what it may
be in this case. But presumably if the
youngsters of the daddies employed by one
firm ask the question, the daddies employed
by other firms will be asked it too. And
about three times out of four a truthful
answer would be: “I went on helping to
pile up profits for a firm which could have
bought a dozen motor-ambulances without
making much of a hole in its petty cash,
and yet didn’t give the nation as much as
would buy a wheelbarrow.”
“Jeames”: What anguish Wellington
would have suffered had he been alive to-day!
He held firmly to the view that England
could only be governed by aristocrats. As
old Gleig, who knew him well, put it: —
It was to the influence of an aristocracy, patriotic,
moderate and just, that England owed her superi-
ority over other nations. Secrets were never
betrayed in our public offices or in our embassies.
Why ? Because they were officered by gentlemen
who, unlike the officials of other were proof
against bribes. The English army was the best in
the world, not because Englishmen are braver or more
enduring than other races, but because the officers
of the English army are gentlemen.
Of those who are now at the head of
affairs, Asquith is the son of a solicitor who
was so poor that he could scarcely afford to
send his brilliant son to the City of London
School. Kitchener’s father was a half-pay
Irish colonel. Fisher has Cingalese blood
in his veins, and his male progenitor held a
commission in an obscure Scottish line
regiment. Haldane, who attends to most of
England’s law, is “self-made,” and so is
Isaacs, who looks after the rest of it.
French’s father was a retired naval captain,
of the sort that pervades Plymouth and
Portsmouth in shoals. He brought money
into the family by marrying an Eccles. And
then there is Lloyd George, not to mention
John Burns. None of these national heroes
was at a public school—an unthinkable lapse
as Wellington would have regarded it. It is
a blighting commentary on the. forecasts of
Toryism, that Britain, in the hands of the
middle classes, is more formidable than at
any time in history,

The wheel base is long-—ll 4 inches

so great that no unpleasant jars reach
the body. All this is possible because
practically every part of the Overland
is designed and made in the Willys-
Overland factories —under our own
supervision, by our own master work-
men.

“Viyella” has uses for every member of
the family, and is unequalled for
women’s blouses, nightdresses and
undergarments; men’s shirts and
pyjamas; children’s frocks, nightdresses
and underclothing.
“Viyella” is in delightful patterns —refined and pleasing. Its softness makes it
comfortable when worn next the skin; it does not irritate like ordinary flannels.
“Viyella” is extremely serviceable and wonderfully economical. It readily
absorbs the moisture of the body and radiates it away, thus preventing chills
when there are sudden changes in the weather. It has been awarded the cer-
tificate of the London Institute of Hygiene, and is a fabric of genuine quality.
All up-to-date storekeepers and drapers stock “Viyella.” See the name on the
selvedge. If unobtainable in your locality, write to us and we will give you the
address of the nearest retailer handling it. I. &E. Morley, 56 Market Street,
Sydney; 2.50 Flinders Lane, Melbourne.

IN
BANANA LAND
Grandfather Barlow, rep. of the Denham
Government in the Leg. Council: —
I shall not go till I am kicked out.
Ex-Home Secretary Appel’s departing
words: —
It is not my nature to wait until I am kicked out.
Treasurer Barnes to a deputation :
If I am to be kicked out, it will be with a clean
conscience.
The boot seems to be always m the mind of
Bananaland’s Cabinet. Anticipatory of a
general kick-out in May?
In March of last year the French steamer
Saint Paul was claimed by David Jones
through one of his many Bananaland coastal
agents, Smith’s Rock, stationed just outside
Moreton Bay. Public outcry, roused the
Government, and it swore to light the dan-
gerous spot. After due consideration
(about seven months) two buoys were
ordered —from the other side of the .world.
Last week the first instalment- —four gas
cylinders—arrived from Norway ; also word
that the rest might turn up some time during
the year. Meanwhile the Bananaland coastal
skipper dodges rocks and prays for the quiet
life of the Dardanelles.
“Cleveland”: Boat drill, which a few
years ago was a lost art on our coasting
steamers, is steadily becoming more general.
I can remember when the call “Fire and
Boat Stations” was responded to by about
half the crew; but it didn’t much matter
that the other half Was away, as, the whole
show would be over in five minutes, while
Davy Jones smiled on and made arrange-
ments for the next batch. The days of
boats “froze” into the chocks with paint, of
rotten boat-falls, sails and sea-anchors, and
of rat-eaten oil bags, are passing, and the
man wdio pays a fancy price for the right
to stay on board a ship while she wallows
from one port to another has now a sport-
ing chance if anything happens and the
boats are requisitioned. The most per-
plexed person on board ship when boat-
stations are piped is “Sparks,” the wireless

GRINDER (with offensive candor): “Now, that there mower only [?] the graws. It
wants sharpenin' . Why, yesteday, for two bob, I sharpened the [?] they got

OLD POT: “ Yes —I've, just borrowed .”
man. I was watching evolutions one day
while men were tumbling up out of the
bowels of the ship, and presently saw
Sparks, resplendent in gold braid, scurrying
about like a rabbit, and with only the faint-
est idea of what was expected of him. He
was obviously sending out S.O.S. when he
met a grimy trimmer, who was -expressing
his opinidh of the a whole proceedings. “I
say, old man,” Railed Sparks, “could you
tell me what I am expected to do?” “Do?”
said the trimmer. “Do? Why, you don’t do
nothin’. You’re one of the bloomin’ ’eroes,
and goes down with the crimson ship.”
Oliver Cromwell Roberts, from U.S.A.,
who has been experimenting in the des-
truction of prickly pear near Dulacca for
the past year or so, began blowing his arseni-
cal fumes at the enemy last January, and
has dealt with 4000 acres to date. There is no
doubt about the mass of the pear treated
being dead. The question is : What about
the little green shoots that spring up after-
wards? Roberts’s contention is that they
are killed by the fumes of acid released from
the dying parents, and that is the main
point to be settled before Queensland takes
itself across the road and buys itself a
drink.
Denham, hitherto well-pleasing in the wow-
sers’ eyes, is taking to language. He told
an astonished Sugar Association the other
day that he “sought to do what was right,
and for the rest he did not care a d— ; n.”
From d —n to blanky and is only a step,
and'a Premier who' blankies and s in
Brisbane is lost.
“Scaffolder”: “Manx” (B. 18/3/T5),
booting out the idea that we have ever had
oil-drillers in Australia before, says the men
Queensland knows were Canadian hot-air
water-drillers. “Manx” writes both wildly
and unfairly. They were water-well drillers;
but they were also oil-well drillers. Most
of those drilling back of “the Reach” in
the ’9o’s hailed from Petrolia. They in-
cluded the late George Fair, to whose per-
sistency and pluck Maoriland is indebted
for' the striking of oil in the Taranaki dis-
fcrict. As for Australian drilling appliances,
I worked on a Queensland-built rig 15 years
ago—a steel one—and it answered splen-
didly. The first well we put down with it
was 2800 ft. deep. That and subsequent
experiences satisfied me that we can build
as good plants as the Yankees can.
Queensland Labor-leader T. J. Ryan got
home a heavy punch on the Denham Admin-
istration the other day. He showed that
there are 406 pastoral leases in the State
with respect to which resumptions were due,
in some cases as far-back as 1902 —13 years
ago! ■ Also, he mentioned the existence of
1400 pastoral holdings from which resump-
tions might be made, but weren’t. For years
past it has been the usual thing for hundreds
of would-be selectors to spend time and money
in looking over the same block of land, and
then engaging in a struggle which only one
of them could win. If Denham wants people
to believe that his alleged policy of settling
people on the land is genuine, it looks as
though he’ll have to first convince them that
Ryan is a liar. To' so restrict the number
of blocks available on this side o'f the Never-
Never that an applicant is lucky if he wins
a ballot within six months isn’t exactly
likely to promote closer. settlement.
The Road to Kum.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When was it that I took that road,
That Persian road at night—
I, part of one tall camel’s pack
That swayed from left to right?
Behind me was a bale of silks,
I guessed their crumpled sheen;
Before a sheaf of cinnamon,
And I was lashed between.
The sky was like a blind man’s face,
There was not any moon,
But still there lurked among the sands
The suns of afternoon.
Sweet was the tinkle of the bells,
No silent hour we had,
The night had set a music chain
Along the camel pad.
And as we rode towards the walls,
A deeper rhythm rang,
For somewhere in the dark below
A camel driver sang.
He wove a dream of languid eyes
Behind a glittering veil,
Of sated fingers, toying with.
The silks inside the bale.
He gemmed the dream with costly jars
Of priceless ambergris:
Of lashes that reveal a glance
As moving as a kiss.
And all the while along the path
Towards the palm-cowled wells
Tfipre was no breaking of the link
Of ceaseless camel bells.
I wonder when I went that way?
The Past is veiled and dumb —
Yet I could swear I took one night
The magic road to Ivum.
A part of one lean camel’s pack,
That swung to left and right.
Wfixn tms it that I went that road ,
That Persian road, at night 9
QTJEENSDAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 27, 1915.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
The newly-imported Governor-and her Ex.
made their first appearance at a local theatre
on Wednesday evening. “Fanny’s First
Play” was the dissipation.
Which reminds me that the Violet Paget
Co. (husband Hugh Buckler is at the Front
or thereabouts) is bidding us farewell.
Saturday will see the last appearance; then
south to Sydney town, where a season will
be managed by the energetic Violet at the
Little Theatre. On Tuesday Mrs. Fred
Barker hostessed a good-bye spread at Fin-
ney’s to the leading lady. The following
day Mrs. T. B. Steele presided over another
party, whereat our own Temple Pigott was
also regretfully good-byed.
Tea parties have been the relaxation of
the week. On Wednesday Mr. Nugent Wade
Brown nourished a couple of hundred friends
and cronies, in celebration of the marriage
of pretty daughter Christian to her chosen
Watts, who helps sheep to grdw khaki in
Central Queensland. Mrs. Guildford David-
son, formerly a Miss Wade Brown, helped
her father do the arduous hand-shaking,
while the bride hovered around in a pretty
blue hat and frock, the brighter for some
touches of deep red.
Miss Willcocks, of New Farm, who is to
be married to Dave Tait on April 7, was
tea-ed yesterday afternoon by Miss Evans, of
Ascot.
Mrs. Ralph Clifton and her daughter.
Mrs. Montefiore, were scheduled to depart
on Saturday for the Northern Rivers on
a motoring tour with young Ralph Clifton,
this being for him a jaunt preliminary to
one to the disturbance in Europe. If the
war ends before he has had sufficient excite-
ment, he is thinking of taking to flying at
one of the aviation schools.
The committee of the Queen Alexandra
Home has landed Lady Goold-Adams with
the hook baited “patroness.” At the annual
meeting this week Mrs. T. C. Abraham was
installed for another term as president.
The shrieks of way-back suburbia for tram
extensions rend the air ; but until the over-
worked track through Queen-street is sup-
plemented, the outlanders will cry in vain.
The tram co. recently got an order-in-coun-
cil empowering it to take enough of Adelaide-
street to build a double track, and the pro-
spect of actually realising the dream of a
better service so shocked the sensibilities of
the City grand-dads that they asked Premier
Denham to take back the slip of paper and
make confetti of it. They didn’t relish the
notion of being overlooked. Also they men-
tioned something about the widening of
Adelaide-street. If the newly-weds on the
fringe of the city waited for their trams till
the City Council gets to work on the widen-
ing they would put their grandchildren
into the first cars on the promised ex-
tensions. Anyway, Digby Denham looked
the aldermen straight in the eye and said
“The order n->ust stand.”
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Politicus”: Andrew Fisher’s great
strategic proposal to connect Port Augusta
and Brisbane has mis-fired; Queensland
hasn’t cottoned on to it, and Andrew’s own
ash-barrel has remained profoundly unaf-
fected. In these circumstances, with Tory
and Liberal papers hostile and Labor papers
indifferent, what’s the good of it? The
noise you heard just now was the plunk
the proposal made when Andrew threw it
out of the window.
“Rangan”: Brisbane Fire Brigades Act
dates back 33 years. It has not been touched
since. The existing regulations regarding
height, material, etc., of buildings, also
nature of stairways, windows, fire escapes,
etc., would suit a mining camp. Some day
the city will have a fire. Then the Govern-
ment will sit in the ashes along with the
two City Councils, and the regulations will
be revised with a piece of charcoal.
“Sergeant Major” calls attention: —
I’m putting the case of the citizen soldier who is
bumped out of his job by a “patriotic” employer,
A ease that will do as an instance was heard before
a Brisbane magistrate the other day, when Rhoades'
and Co., house furnishers, were proceeded against for
“having committed a breach of the Defence Act, in
that, while being the employer of Patrick Davies, a
member of the Citizen Forces of the Commonwealth,
they penalised Davies for having rendered the per-
sonal service required of him, by dismissing him
from his employment.” Some sort of a fight was
put up on the first day of the hearing of the case,
but on the second day the employer’s counsel came
along and pleaded guilty, and the P.M. said the
bill would be £2O. But it must have cost the
youngster as much as that in lost time; and why
should he suffer ? Somebody ought to recompense
him somehow. But as it is a tough job to find a
way which will be quite satisfactory, the first step
is to make the punishment so heavy that cases
won’t arise. A fine of £2O, including costs, won’t
do at all.

HERB are still a few people who ask : What is
Amiwnd ?
Amiwud is a reproduction of the Hardwoods
made in sheets (and also made in the weight
of thick paper) for inside decoration.
T
It is a perfedt reproduction. All the beauty of the
grain and the richness of the coloring are faithfully
reproduced.
You have the choice of several styles —Golden
Oak or Weathered Oak, Mahogany or Jenisero.
You can use it for the redecoration of old rooms
or in the building of new. It can be tacked on to
existing walls or diredtly on to the studs.
It can be had plain (without grain) for ceilings —
see how charming is the combination of panelled
plain ceiling with panelled grained walls in Mr.
Firkins’s residence, here pictured.
With the aid of Amiwud you
can get the refined effect of
Old World homes. You can
get that softness of finish so
much to be desired, and—this
is an important point—you can
get it at very small outlay.

Home-made Beauty Helps
and Money-saving Recipes.
By RITA MOYA.
It is quite impossible for me to give advice in these columns regarding the merits of
various preparations on the market. In general, my advice to women Who wish to retain,
or regain, their youthful appearance is to avoid the many unreliable, cheap, made-up pre-
parations. Nature provides many simple “beautifiers,” and the best results are obtained
from their use. You must not be disappointed if you experience a little trouble in obtain-
ing some of the ingredients mentioned below. They can all be obtained, however, from any
reliable chemist, and even if he does not happen to have them in stock he will gladly
obtain them for you at a few hours’ notice, if you insist—and you should insist. The
smart, dainty woman of to-day likes to know what she is using.
Sure Way of Permanently Removing
Superfluous usual hair removers
on the market I have found not at all satis-
factory. I recommend the following prepara-
tion. It is really wonderful in its action,
destroying hairs completely in a few moments.
Many women have merely a down on the arms
and face, and imagine they do not need a
hair remover. They hardly realise what a
wonderful difference there would be in their
appearance if this down, even light as it
may be, were, removed. Get at any good
chemist an ounce of pure powdered pheminol.
Mix a little of it into a paste with water and
apply it to the hair growths. Wash off after
two minutes and the hairs will have entirely
disappeared. A little non-greasy face cream
can then be applied with advantage.
Powder Not Necessary.—Few women know
that it is possible to give the complexion a
delightfully smooth, velvety appearance in a
few moments by the application of ordinary
cleminite. If you have no cleminite in the
house, get about an ounce from your chemist
and dissolve it in four tablespoonsful of cold
water. This lotion applied to the face, neck
or arms, with the finger tips, immediately
gives that peach-like bloom so much admired;
powder is not necessary, and the result lasts
all day long. For an evening in a hot ball-
room this recipe is unrivalled. You will not
get that greasy, shiny look so often seen after
a little exertion.
Natural Wavy Hair.—You hair will regain
its rich lustre and fluffy appearance if cleansed
occasionally with a mild shampoo. The very
best thing I know of for this purpose is plain
stallax, a teaspoonful of which dissolved in
a cup of hot water is sufficient for the thickest
growth of hair. Pure stallax, however, seems
rather expensive, as it is only sold in the
origma.l packages which cost half a crown,
on i. <l uan tity, however, is sufficient for 25 or
oO shampoos, so it really is most economical
in the long run. Dry, irritating or profusely
oily scalps are soon put in a healthy condi-
tion. and dull, brittle hair grows bright and
e\en in color, and will be ever so soft and
Huffy.
Hair Culture.—There is no excuse for falling
hair; no excuse for thin, sick hair, or bald
spots. No beauty of features can make up for
a distressing lack of hair growth, and the
formula which 1 purpose giving you will posi-
tively force the hair to grow. Mix about an
ounce of boranium in J-pint of bay rum,
shake the bottle well and allow it to stand for
half an hour, then add fpint of fresh cold
water and strain. Rub well into the scalp,
and in two weeks’ time look for the new
growth; you will not require a microscope to
caa If 1
To Renew Complexions without Cosmetics.
—lf the excessive user of cosmetics only
knew the impression her artificiality really
makes upon others, she would quickly seek
the means of gaining a natural complexion.
Let her acquire the mercolised wax habit, dis-
carding make-up entirely, and she will soon
have the kind of complexion that women envy
and men admire. It is so easy to get a
little mercojised wax from the chemist and
use it nightly like cold cream, washing it off
m the morning. Gradually the lifeless, soiled
outer cuticle peels off in tiny invisible flakes,
and in a week or so you have a brand new-
complexion, clear, soft, velvety and of girlish
color and texture. The treatment is so simple
harmless and marvellously effective the won-
der is that every woman whose skin is with-
ered and discolored has not already adopted it

Business, Bobbery, etc.
I .C. ’: Victoria’s Fisheries Department
is conducting an inquiry into the habits and
religions and other customs of schnapper,
and to that end has caught some 600 young
ones, decorated them with silver medals, and
tossed them in the water again. The pub-
lic is expected to let the beasts go if they
are caught this year, but thereafter they
may be taken and fried in peace if the De-
partment is notified of the animal’s number
and the time and place where caught and
the size of him. It will be all instructive,
no doubt; but what the State really needs
is reliable information about the persons
who manipulate the schnapper after he is
snared; especially the method by which he
suddenly jumps from 6d. a bushel to ss. an
ounce.
****■'
“Matey”: Casino (N.S.W.) butter-fac-
tory is experimenting with boxes made from
the local hoop pine in the hope of finding
a substitute for Maoriland white pine, which
is becoming scarce and expensive. No doubt
a measure of success will be gained if the
hoop pine is well seasoned and the boxes
stuffed only with butter which goes promptly
into Australian consumption. But if butter
for export is encased in the wood the, taint
will be there. Can’t somebody invent a
cheap and tasteless lacquer which could be
put on the inside of the boxes? Any old
timber would do then and the cow-coclcies
might be able to afford to pay their hands
another threepence or so a week.
* * * *
The oyster season at the Bluff (M.L.)
opened recently and was marked by the
frequency of the bivalve. Prices were cut
from Bs. a sack to 6s. 6d. ; half-sacks, 4s.
This works out at less than one penny a
dozen, which should be good news for eaters
of M.L. oysters in Melbourne and Sydney.-
It may even prevent the Dago restaurant-
keeper from raising the price higher than
usual (Is. 6d. a plate) on “accounta da
war.”
* * * *
“N. or M.”: There are some odd old busi-
ness signs still in Hobart, though the in-
evitable “Hole in the Wall” has long since
been filled up. The Bird in Hand, Maypole
and Dr. Syntax inns still survive and the
reconstructed Wheatsheaf nestles in the
midst of private houses. Other old pubs
are the Ship, Man of War, Sailors’ Rest
and Ye 01de» Steam Packet Inn, a queer
little drinkery by the Hunter’s Island
wharf, with its roof drawn down like a
sou’-wester. In the days of smuggling, its
position at the outlet of the creek was a
great asset. A boat could slip up quietly
under shadow of the banks and unload kegs
at the back door. The Steam Hammer is
another pub named when steam power was
a sufficiently new form of energy to be com-
mented on. Others include the Ocean
Child and the Duke of Wellington; and
Salamanca Place, near the wharves, has
its Rear Admiral Hornsby and Lord Rod-
ney. When I was a youngster Rodney, a
warlike figure, with cocked hat and drawn
sword, pranced across the front of the build-
ing named, in his honor. The sign, a huge
affair cut in either wood or sheet iron, has
since fbeen removed. The oddest and I
believe the oldest of Hobart’s pictorial signs,
the. Good Woman, remains in its old
olace. and the lady still has her head under
her arm.
*• * * *
“Rimu”: In these days of high prices for
meat and bread there ought to be some
means by which the fruit which abounds,
and which might still more abound if there
were encouragement for the growers, could
reach the tables of the people, not as a
Sunday treat or occasional luxury, but as a
regular article of diet. It is quite a usual
thing to find peaches, apricots and plums
sold retail at anything from four to 10 times
the price listed in the market report; and
even the market report is a poor indication
of the real return to the grower. Is there
any real difficulty about dealing with a mat-
ter of this kind? Laws have been passed
to prevent usury; why not pass laws to
limit the profits on pomegranates? Of course
fruit is a perishable commoditv and a decent
allowance must be made for losses ; but 100
per cent- might to be enough to cover every-
thing. Why not, then, simol.v enact that
any person who was proved to have sold
fruit at more than double the wholesale
market price should be liable to a fine of
£lOO for the first offence? The cost of a
second robbery might be £5OO, of a third
£lOOO, and anybody who was convicted a
fourth time might be stripped of all he pos-
sessed and stood naked in the street to eat
a case of over-rine tomatoes. Two or three
convictions would settle the matter. Fruit
would th°n be sold at a reasonable price,
and far from the r°t a il e rs being the losers
their turnover, would become so enormous
that they would wonder how they could
ever have imagined that the secret of the
fruit trade lay in charging prices nobody
could pay.
A FALL IN WHEAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Chicago heard that the Dardanelles bombard-
ment had begun, the price of wheat went down Is.
a bushel. Great quantites of Russian wheat are
awaiting shipment, and once these are released the
world’s price will be reduced. — Cable.
The great ships ride like sloven Fate
(The good sun spreads his love for all) —
Nose deep they move. In monstrous hate
They swing their bristling guns, and wait
The moment when, against the wall,
Their dreadful thunderbolts must fall.
Then fearful Fatmen sigh and sob —
Chicago wheat is down a bob l
Chicago town is far away,
Against the Turk the bolts are hurled;
Round Marmora the life long day
The iron death birds duck and play,
And half across the shaken world
The speculator’s nerves are curled.
In agony he grasps his fob —
Chicago wheat is down a bob!
The shells that leap in hellish glee
Athwart the smiling Orient skies
Beat down the barriers, and free
The wheat ships to the open sea
To feed the mob and agonise
The big men “holding for a rise.”
War's horrors through creation throb—
Chicago wheat is down a bob l
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

SYMPTOMS of KIDNEY TROUBLE
ARE YOU IN DANGER THROUGH
IGNORANCE?
Nearly all of our readers suffer from aches
and pains of some kind at one time or
another. Aches and pains very often mean
kidney troubles. Such symptoms as consti-
pation, backache, improper action of the
bowels, pains in muscles and points, nasty
taste in the mouth first thing in the morning,
swelling of the feet, cloudy urine, a general
feeling of weakness and irritability, back-
ache, all these are symptoms of kidney
trouble.
They are caused by the poisonous acid in
the blood which the kidneys should remove,
but they do not owing to their being in a
weak state.
To cure kidney trouble of any kind, you
must positively get rid of the cause —the

The Inky Way.
“Pen-al Servitude”: The Vic. Education
Department has made known its determina-
tion to have all books utilised in State
schools printed and published in Australia.
This is only consistency in a Protectionist
State, and let us hope it will serve to raise
publishers of Australian school books above
the necessity of cadging their material from
Australian authors, who, on the whole, are
rather less affluent than publishers are wont
to be. Every Australian writer of some
note has become familiar with the publisher’s
polite letter drawing the attention of William
Smith, Esq., litterateur, to the fact that “we
contemplate” compiling such-and-such a book
for use in the schools, and asking permission
to utilise extracts from his well-known writ-
ings. The gracious epistle always dwells
upon the honor and glory that will accrue to
Smith from appearing before so large an
audience in so fine a publication and in such
excellent company. The letter, too, conveys
the impression that Messrs. Barabbas and Co.
are in some way performing a duty to their
country and an act of charity to the people
in producing the book. I am represented in
six or eight books of this sort with verse and
stories, for which I never received a cent:
but long ago my delusion that Barabbas and
Co. gave away their books was ruthlessly
shattered. Perhaps now that the local pub-
lisher is to have a monopoly he will be in a
position to pay the local literary producer for
his wares, without running the risk of bring-
ing the house down about his ears.
“Y.Z.”: Have you noticed how many of
the hand-to-mouth papers are utilising old
goods as serviceable war material? In one
the other day I discovered a page group of
fat babies, long ago utilised to advertise
somebody’s Malted Meal for Inflating In-
fants. The title ran: “Foes the Germans
are fighting in Belgium.” Last week quite
a respectable weekly had a photograph of
a one-legged man labelled “Victim of Ger-
man atrocity.” lie happened to be a St.
Kilda acquaintance of mine who had lately
figured in a Stock Exchange fancy festival.
Another paper in an Australian capital is
using up a stock of old plates of the ruins
of ’Frisco after the great ’quake as samples
of wreckage left by the Huns. But the
cheekiest thing so far is a second-hand pro-
cess-block of a parade of draught mares at
Flemington show ground, labelled: “Artil-
lery horses for the Allies at Dixmude.”
* * * *
Another rolling-stone is on the move.
George Lee, of Melbourne Herald, has taken
his war experience to the Broad-
meadows Camp, and hopes to be among the
next lot to leave Australia, with intent to
put a head on the Sultan or some other repro-
bate.
The Mergenthaler Linotype Co., which
owns the largest machine-type business in
the world, is waxing very fat on the bones of
the solid old hand-comp. Last year it had a
profit of £615,000, and its assets now stand
at about five millions sterling. The wages of
the old hand-comp., which one time drifted
into 10,000 pubs, now go in a steady stream
down the one great sink.
The first job just-deceased Frank Bullen,
the novelist, had on the Australian coast
after quitting the whalers was that of lamp-
trimmer on the Helen McGregor, which used
to run to the Clarence. Some of his copy
fell into the Grafton paper.
* * *
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”: —
An interesting publication of a long past is the
Temperance Advocate, of 12 small pages, issued in
Sydney in 1840-1. It was edited by John Fairfax,
of Macquarie Place, Sydney, the proprietor, and
printed for and published by him “at the Herald
office, Lower George, Sydney.” .As a sub-title it had:
“The Australasian, Commercial and Agricultural Intel-
ligencer.” There is very little local matter in the num-
ber before me, beyond the advertisements, of which
there are 39. J. Reading, a relative of John Fairfax,
from whose family the present “Mr. James” got his
second name, advertises himself as at King-street,
where cards, circulars, billheads, etc., were printed,
and all the work of a jobbing office was done. Mrs.
Hordern, of 12 King-street, between Pitt and Castle-
reagh streets, has an advt. about “ladies’ and
children’s Tuscan and Dunstable hats and bonnets;
work boxes, dressing eases and writing desks.” To-
day the descendants of Mrs. Hordern and John Fair-
fax are rivals in wealth. None of the other adver-
tisers are remembered, and their signboards have
disappeared—worth remembering when you are
asked to join a company which proposes to pay big
money for the goodwill of a business which looks as
though it would go on for ever.
* * * *
A Sydney paper, which does its law report-
ing on the maniacal principle of one-man-
ten-jobs, has just had to part up £5OO and
costs for libelling a well-known business man
by putting him behind the spikes on charges
of embezzlement. The fact was that the busi-
ness man was the prosecutor; but a sweat-
ing roundsman fell over his weary feet and
took his paper with him. The system has
been a fairly expensive one so far, and one
of these bright days somebody with as good
a case as this plaintiff had will decline to
compromise, and then the system will be
more expensive than ever.
* * * *
“McNortli” : Two or three times lately I
have seen statements that the German how-
itzer shell makes a hole in the ground “big
enough to bury five horses.” This seems a
very unsatisfactory way of describing an
excavation. Few people have seen five dead
horses in one heap, and most of us have but
vague ideas of the size of the necessary
grave. I can find no mention of what sized
horses are used in measuring these holes,
and there is silence respecting the nature of
the soil shifted. Why not next time give
the approximate number of cubic yards dis-
placed? But if the ex-undertaker who is
responsible for the flowery touch will per-
sist in thinking of graves, it would be more
illuminating if in specifying the dimensions
he said it was big enough to bury a live
bookmaker (I never saw a dead ’un), or of
another that it would contain 10 wowsers,
or four goats, or a church, or a p.n. Then
we could go out with a foot-rule and get the
hang of things.

REXONA PROVES A FRIEND IN NEED.
Accidents, more or less serious, are always happening to one or another of every family. The
busy mother is not so careful of herself as she might be, and a cut or burn often results. The ever-
active children frequently hurt themselves, and come running home to show cut, bruise or black-
ened eye, and father comes from his work with a painful hurt or cut. All these injuries, not so very
serious in themselves, may become so if neglected, and it is just then that the mother welcomes a
really reliable remedy which is easily applied, and can be depended upon to heal quickly. Rexona
fills this need. There is no better treatment than to get a tin of Rexona, and apply this Rapid Healer
night and morning. First clean the sore with warm water, then apply a little Rexona, and protect
the wound from contact with the clothing with a soft bandage. The most troublesome sores will
heal under this treatment in a few days. The wise mother will always be prepared for emergencies
by keeping a pot of Rexona handy.

CURED ALL THE HOUSEHOLD.
“My little boy, aged four years,
was covered with sores on his face
and hands for six weeks,” writes
Mis. Maggie Morris, Eighth Street,
Bowden, S.A. “We could not get
them healed. His father also had
a very had sore hand; it was
caused hy the bricks and lime. It
was bad for a long time; he did
not know what to do, as he suf-
fered a great, deal with the pain.
The baby also had a very sore ear
for some months. We had tried
all kinds of ointments, but none
did them any good until we heard
of the new Ointment called
Rexona. We tried the little tin,
and found it very healing. Then
we got a large tin, which cured the
three sufferers in less than a week.
Rexona seems different to other
ointments; it is so and
does not irritate the wounds.”
