The Pale Apprentice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AT night he sits before the polished
board—
That table with the crooked, carven
legs,
lie brought from some outlandish Moorish
town, .
Almost I think he reached the rack for this.
They thought he was the Devil and a Jew,
\nd he is just the devil of a Jew,
Feels heat like I do, and growls at the cold,
And faith! cleans up his platter just as I,
lint grudges all the while his keep and mine.
And thus he wastes these haunted, twilight
hours,
When moths are in the poppy fields outside,
And the blurred ghosts of willows in the lake,
A sky of rare pale lemon overhead,
And the gold swimming luxury of the moon.
’Tis just because the moon is gold. I think
He sometimes glances at it round the blind,
But never hears the crickets how they call,
Nor the long, amorous lilt of nightingales.
Perhaps he loved a woman long ago,
Some black-eyed, bronze-curled, slender little
thing
With small, white breasts and most enticing
limbs,
Much as I love Nanette. Perhaps he, too,
Was an apprentice in a window seat,
Paid, with a long complaint, on Lady Day,
And beaten when required, or when he
dreamed
Too much of sloe-like eyes and copper curls
And little breasts, where he would like to
hang
A necklet made of that close-hoarded gold !
Gold! What is gold? That cold and clink-
ing stuff
That slides between his fingers every night.
It cannot bring the red blood to his veins,
Nor smooth one crease from his high,
wrinkled brow.
Nor steal the rheumatism from ‘his knees,
Nor give those lash-less lids, those claw-like
hands
The quality to move a wench’s heart.
Gold! He might buy a wanton for an hour,
But I misdoubt the harpy of them all
Could bear to stay an hour....for half his
gold!
The waxen candles shimmer in the board.
Deep toned it is, in some hard, brilliant wood.
Perhaps it was a worthy pedestal
Fashioned for squatting haunches of a god—
Perhaps its grain grew dark with human
blood
To buy a boon with screaming sacrifice.
I wonder who he cheated out of it.
I'll swear he never paid an honest price!
lie draws the shade across the window frame.
He will not let the laughing moonlight in,
Lest avaricious eye snould stealthily
Peer round the heavy russets of the blind,
And tell the hamlet, bringing cut-throats
here;
Or some crouched footpad, crawling ’mid the
weeds
Of our bleak garden, should grow 7 very bold
And who would fear the rusty blunderbuss
Or me, the slim apprentice, who is put
Down in the window seat, to mind his books ?
He’d look to wring my neck like some tame
fowl’s,
And scarce go timorous of the kestrel’s beak
In one who is the chattel of the Jew.
And then he’d enter, and with daggered hand
Strike in the nape of that thin, withered
neck,
A little doubtful of the spurt of blood
Since one would fancy water in his veins,
Stagnant at that. How he would run his
hands
(Perchance a trifle quicker by that blood)
Among those greedy piles of yellow gold!
And, creeping out the fox-like way he came,
Would seek the tavern and some cosy Moll
To help him to disburse ; while I belike
Would hide in haycock, or glide trembling
through
The grove of poplars to the spinney’s edge,
And beating with my fists against the pane
Would rouse the beadle and his ban-dog up,
With tale of robbery and murder vile.
Unless he caught me napping, trussed me
down,
And flung me with a rag-wad in my mouth
To wait my chance of rescue in the barn.
I warrant he would vanish by the dawn,
And take the smoky road to London town,
Lost with his Moll among the many roofs
And narrow streets and wild, rat-haunted
lanes.
Till ill-luck swings him high on Tyburn
tree,
Or draws a horrid reckoning with The Boot,
He would have had his hour. But I —not I,
Who do no more than turn the page of books
And hate their heavy clamps and old mil-
dews,
And spurn the slow insistence of the claim
Of gold that on a ringing surface fa 115....
There is a quaint bent weapon on the wall,
The handle arabesqued. I think at times
That I will take it down, and try to read
The deep inscription someone graved there-
on,
Oommemorating honorable war
Or quiet murder in a quiet house
(A place surrounded by faint, glimmering
fields,
A filled-in moat, where field mice squeak
and rim,
With connate cunning, from the feathered
owl.
That only leaves the ivy in the dark.
But has such monster eyes in golden rings
I’or finding small things quivering in the
hedge.)
A master’s murder by his servant done.
A pale apprentice, who sits night by night
In musty corners of old window seats.
And feels his heart strive like a bird, against
His shabby nankeen vest? To break away
And seek her small, round face, and be a
man?
And kiss her breasts to swelling, and her
mouth
To those denying, yet surrendering, words?
• - . .To promise her a silk chemise to wear.
A silver girdle, and a satin coat.
A pair of shoes with scarlet heels, the like
The Lady of the Manor used to flaunt
To chapel, when the Yuletide snows were
here
Before she disappeared in coach and four
With that tall, haughty lover out of Spain.
Leaving my lord a sword thrust in the back?
Why will my thought run ever on the way
A sharp point pricks an opening in the
flesh?
Young men bleed more than old. My lord
was young—
lias she forgotten, as the Spaniard’s trull,
How his blood made a puddle in the snow?
One spot of moonlight finds a tiny hole
In the dull russets of the faded blind.
It. is as bright and snaring as her eyes,
That small-faced, dimpled and demure
Nanette
Who wanders all alone, where primroses
Are like pale lanterns in the hushing grass.
Alone —but am / sure she is alone?
Only the pulsing of a rebel heart!
Only the clock and click of devil’s dice!
Dry as the rattle in a dying throat!
And think what this could bring me! Her,
'at least 1
Like women all, she dotes on pretty things.
A sterling coin, no matter how ’tis flung,
Will buy a man a cheap, loose mouth to
kiss.
But jewels (like the scales along the back
Of mountain dragon, issuing from his cave
And blowing sulphur fumes on hills sur-
prised )
And slipping chains, and dainty scented
shoon —
These things would buy Nanette. Her price
is high.
But there is gold enough to get it here.
He shuffles to the blind (did he misread
That spot,of moonshine for a robber’s eye?)
Tightens the cord, and stiffly bars the door;
Shuts out all help, should he by any chance
Squeal in his Death strife, like a strangling
rat.
Not once he turns to look at me. No fear
Of me, the cowed apprentice, comes to him.
So he creaks back into the wooden chair;
But in that moment, with no shield between,
With no worn velvet coat, no frayed cravat.
No twisted shape, no straw-like fringe of
hair,
1 saw the pools of light the gold heaps
made —
A ransom for a king, a white queen’s price—
And thus once more the song of imps begins.
The chorus of the damned, the chink of gold.
And through some crack in these grey-
lozenged panes
There drifts the passion-stimulus of flowers,
Of flowers that grow in grasses hushed and
long
To hide the fancies that might shame the
day.
If I should lift that weapon from the wall
Its resting place would be outlined in dust,
The dust of many lonely, hateful eves.
But once the thing was done, I could wipe
clean
The blade of stains that are more damp than
dust,
And place it there again so no one hnew.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The Double-Bed Dialogues.
SETTLING DOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I VERY woman is at heart a married
1 i woman. So is every man, though he
doesn’t know it till it is too late —
till he is married.
“This marriage business,” I murmured to
Babette, “is terribly worrying.”
We were in our engagement days, when
it was occasionally necessary to make a
remark between cuddles. Otherwise you
can’t tell exactly where one cuddle leaves
off and the other begins; and the statistics
get all muddled.
“Why?” Babette cooed dreamily, reluc-
tantly releasing her lips from my right eye.
“Look at all the married couples we know,
dear, and then recollect that we’re going to
get married ourselves. Doesn’t it make you
afraid?”
“Surely you don’t think that we’ll be like
them, do you ?”
“That’s what I’m afraid of. Look at the
Brown-Smiths. They’re perfectly happy;
the ideal marriage everybody calls it. They
adore each other. They’re wrapped up in
each other.”
“Well, so are we,” Babette reminded me.
“No people could be more wrapped up in
each other than we are now.”
There are several ways in which a nice
girl can sit on a man’s knee. Babette’s long
experience of sitting on male knees had
eliminated all the awkwardness of that posi-
tion. and her figure so adapted itself to the
pose that it required some ingenuity to
unwrap ourselves. On this occasion, I did
not try.
“But how obviously they love each other,”
I went on. “They’ve no time for anybody
but themselves. And how dull they are!”
“Frumps!” said Babette. “But we’ll never
be like them.”
“Still, look at the others. There’s the
Smith-Browns. They’ve been married only
three years, and you’d think they had been
born married. Their conversation consists
of married remarks—silly matrimonial jokes
that they understand, and nobody else does.
They haven’t an interest beyond their home.
And there are the Smith-Smiths and the
Brown-Browns —they’re all the same.”
“But you can’t compare them with us,”
Babette cried. “They’re just dull, conven-
tional people who’ve done the usual conven-
tional thing. To them marriage is just
marriage; but to us, dear, marriage is going
to be a continual adventure. They’ve all
settled down. That’s all. But we're never
going to settle down.”
I hopefully agreed with her; and we
settled down for a quarter of an hour.
“Fancy comparing us with that frumpy
lot!” Babette took advantage of the cramp
in my left leg to remark.
“And yet,” I said, “perhaps Mrs. Brown-
Smith made the same remark once.”
“Well, it all comes of settling down. And
I’m not going to settle down. I mean to be
just the same to you when I’m your wife as
I am now. And you must promise,” she
went on, “to be just the same to me when
you’re my husband.”
“All that, and more!” I said.
Babette kissed me. Sometimes Babette is
extraordinarily intelligent.
“We won’t be commonplace. Most wives
I know are just wives. They don’t take the

back and a dull pain through the loins and the kidneys,
and my secretions became unnatural and irregular. 1
consulted a doctor, who recommended Turkish baths;
but though I got a little ease, 1 could not work. I used
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, and obtained wonderful
relief. I am so much better I can now do heavy work.
1 am firmly convinced that Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills are a true kidney remedy, and I never fail to

Fifteen years later Mr. Carroll confirms the above: —“I
have been quite free of backache and urinary trouble ever

ago. My cure proves that Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills

secretions. I tried many different remedies, which were

I
slightest interest in their husband’s business.
And look how I love to hear all about your
work!”
My business was —and .still is—to dictate
a series of business letters concerning but-
tons. The firm in whose office I occupy a
responsible post manufactures buttons. It
makes other things, too, like hooks and
eyes; but my department is devoted
solely to buttons. I served my apprentice-
ship as a junior clerk in the button depart-
ment of the office; and gradually as I ex-
tended my knowledge I rose to my present
position. This is the age of specialisation ;
and, though I had chances to go in the hook-
and-eye selling department, I sternly re-
fused to consider them, A man who knows
buttons as I know them will always com-
mand a steady salary.
And in those engagement days I used to
fill in the necessary pauses between more
serious preoccupations with information
upon buttons. It was a subject that appealed
to Babette’s woman’s heart. She grew quite
romantic.
“It brings us so close together, doesn’t it?”
she used to exclaim, forgetting that no button
Could have brought us closer than we usually
were.
“And when we’re really married,” she
went enthusiastically on, “you must take me
to your office. I’d love to see the place
where you toil all day, so that I could pic-
ture you slaving away for little me when
I'm at home by myself. And I could go as
your wife, couldn’t I, even in office hours?
And you must show me your desk where
you dictate your clerver letters. And you
must tell me all about the clerks —is she fair
.or dark?”
“Who?”
“The woman you dictate your letters to.”
“Oh, her ! I’ve never noticed. Something
mousey or mud-color, I think. But if you
like, I’ll look carefully to-morrow, aud let
you know.”
“Oh, no; you needn’t,” Babette hastily
answered. “If she saw you looking at her,
she might think you were interested in her.”
“Interested in her, when I’ve got you !” I
bundled.
But, nevertheless, I was interested. She
had quite a nice mouth and chin, I found.
So, two inexperienced navigators, who
knew less of each other than they did of the
great ocean they were about to explore, we
planned and perfected our equipment. In
our frail new boat we—with our beautiful
innocence and our glorious confidence—were
going to avoid the shoals on which, all
around our horizon, other ships as badly
found and as incompetently captained, had
run hopelessly aground. But in our few in-
tervals of sanity—even engaged couples have
such fleeting intervals —when he is wonder-
ing, at the street corner why she is so late, or
when she is wearying because he has not
written—in these few intervals Fear bat-
tened on our dear hopes. So many millions
of ships had as bravely set sail, and though
we found few that had gone to the bottom,
broken in two on some cruel reef (like a
chorus-girl or a lodger), many were ashore
in the mud (breakfast 305 times a year),
many becalmed (afternoon-teas and bridge),
and some so covered by the barnacles of
dullness that they would assuredly never
reach port.
Yet, with all these sailings in the past,
surely we had ourselves some inherited in-
stinct that would guide us aright?
Bravely, through the mist ot confetti, we
started off! Those first cosy evenings, after
we had crossed the bar of the honeymoon,
when sitting in our drawing-room, Babette,
mv wife, told me so humorously of her diffi-
culties with her maids ! We kept only one
—one at a time : the plural justifies itself.
And she would sit, just as I had pictured her,
daintily darning my socks, while I smoked
the new pipe she had chosen for me—in the
privacy of our home was the only place
where I could smoke it without causing an
obstruction of the footpath—and told her the
most thrilling of the events of my day at the
office. Sometimes I brought home to her
copies of letters that I had dictated about
buttons. One of them Babette insisted on
keeping. She thought the phraseology so
beautiful.
In the intervals between maids, I used to
help Babette to wash up the dinner-things.
How we enjoyed that picnicking, as we called
it. in the scullery ! And what fun we had
over Babette’s cooking, and how clever she
was always to have a stock of tinned herrings
to fall back upon !
Then one night when I came home tired
out with a big order for buttons, and found
that my dinner wouldn’t be ready for an
hour, I admit I got rather irritable, and
sharply asked Babette. why she could not
manage to keep her maids more than a week
at a time? She remarked that I was a
brute. I mentioned that if I could manage
my department of the office so capably, why
couldn’t she manage one maid? And she
informed me that she didn’t ever want to
hear another word about nr, tin-pot office.
The washing-up that night wasn’t at all
like a picnic.
We made it up, of course, in the double-
bed. But after that, I found that Babette
wasn’t in the least interested in the button
trade, and that her enthusiasm for darning
my socks had been transferred to her own
hats.
There was, therefore, nothing for me to do
but to refuse to discuss my business affairs
with my wife, and to take up a book when
Babette began upon hats.
“I was thinking,” Babette remarked one
night, “we don’t entertain enough. Suppose
we ask the Brown-Smiths down to dine some
evening, and we could have a little game of
bridge afterwards? Our evenings, with just
you and me, are so dull, aren’t they?”
“The Brown-Smiths!” I echoed. “But
they’re so horribly married.”
“Well, so are we.”
“Are we?” I asked in dismay. Looking
back at that evening—which was just like
any other evening—I had to admit that
Babette was right. “But we were going to
be so different,”l reminded her.
“It’s no use,” Babette sighed. “We can’t.
Nobody can. If you’re married, you’re mar-
ried, and that’s the end of it —and of you.
I’ve tried to keep up -with my old girl
friends —we were such friends before you
married me” —Babette almost accused me of
marrying her —“but I can’t get really inti-
mate with an unmarried girl. They’re so
ignorant and so —so romantic. They look
on men as if they were something super-
human. Why, Elsie Janaway looks up to
you as if you were a god ! Fancy! I used
to think her so sensible. And there are so
many subjects that you can’t discuss with
unmarried girls, and so many stories that
you’ve got to explain the point of to them.
I feel like a teacher in a Sunday-school.”
This set me thinking. Looking over my
friends I found that I had rather lost inter-
est in the unmarried ones. They seemed,
these young fellows, so irresponsible, so
happy-hearted, so free! And they looked on
girls, too, with a curious admiration mixed
with an incomprehensible awe. Now, there
is nothing awesome or specially admirable in
women—even Babette, I had gradually dis-
covered, was in many ways, just a woman.
“Yes, marriage does open your eyes,”
Babette went on. “I used to think. Mrs.
Brown-Smith hopelessly stodgy, but now
I find 1 get on with her so well. There’s
so many things we have to talk about —com-
paring husbands. Every married woman
is interesting as soon as you start compar-
ing husbands. And you soon find out that,
no matter how different you thought your
husband from all the other husbands, all hus-
bands are the same husband. All we women
are married to the same man. I think it is
that —the fact that we’re all of the same
family—that brings us together. You like
Mr. Brown-Smith, don’t you?”
The curious thing was that I did like him.
I had found in him, once I got to know him,
that comprehension of wives that I found
nowhere in the ranks of my unmarried
friends. For one thing, he didn’t treat wives
as a joke. Not that Brown-Smith hadn’t
a sense of humor. Some of his jokes about
marriage were excruciating. The chief ad-
vantage of marriage is that a husband is
always sure of finding someone who hasn’t
heard the latest—or, if she has, it has been
femininely bowdlerised. And there is
nothing in unmarried life like the camara-
derie of knowing that your friend’s wife has
hear'i the latest you told her husband at
lunch-time.
I did like Brown-Smith. So we lay dis-
cussing the. question of asking the Brown-
Smiths to dine. And when he had definitely
decided to join the lost legion, we merely
turned our backs and settled down to go to
sleep.
Suddenly Babette sat up in bed. “Henry,”
she tragically exclaimed, “we’ve settled down
—like all the rest of them!”
I was nearly asleep. T half-turned my
face. In the diffused milky white of the
moonlight outside, filtering through our
blinds, I saw Babette faintly white, a vague
vision of lace and loose hair, leaning over
me. I felt the rich warmth of her bosom
on my shoulder. I felt her mournful eyes
on mine.
“Well.” I grunted, “there’s no need for
you to wake me up when I was just off to
sleep to tell me that! Good night, dear.”
And I turned over and settled down ; and
Babette, that miracle of loveliness and love,
turned over too and settled down; and after
I had gently shoved her over to her half of
the double bed —-she’s always sneaking more
than her share—we went tranquilly to sleep.
Henry James James.
About Nightmares.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 LOOKED over the palings, and saw
that they were nightmares.
There was a whole drove of them,
feeding on toadstools and dried spiders and
the herb mandragore. They were guarded
by a melancholy-looking basilisk, who wore
green spectacles.
“I do have the most fearful trouble with
them,” he said, when he heard me cough.
“But it’s little to what the night stallions
give me when I’m on that job. I once had
to look after Pegasus, but he was nothing
to them. I just kept his wings clipped once
a fortnight regular, and that held him inside
his fences. I got good pickings out of that
game too, retailing his pin feathers to the
female angels for bookmakers to use on
choir days. Though lie did roar a bit at
clipping time. But those there night stal-
lions, you can’t cut their wings, because
they’re leather-like, and it would hurt them,
so that the Overseer’d be on to me like a
gross of thunderbolts. There’s no holding
the creatures, and at branding time they
fair give me the scarlet horrors. The other
day one of the boundary riders lets a broken
paling go, and there they were, the whole
pack of them, out and in among the true
dreams, and next season there was the Old
Man to pay, and no mistake. Half of every
consignment of dreams sent down was mixed
like—crossed, you see —and Dfeere was so
many complaints sent in I near lost my job.
Three archbishops and a cardinal that had
been ordered pleasant hack-dreams for bed
exercise woke howling of hell fire, and I
was bit by one myself that fair turned my
hair grey and give me boils. • I’d ha’ got
over that, only a little peasant girl (Pippa
they called her) was so badly thrown by
one of these here night brumbies, as you
might call them, that she was translated.
She was on the right side of the ledger, of
course —just my luck —so they made her a
cherub, and no sooner was she through the
ivory gate and given her admittance ticket
than she goes and reports me to the Over-
seer. Then there was trouble.”
The poor old basilisk wiped his eyes with
a grimy, red-bordered pocket handkerchief,
and looked up at me, piteously.
“A shame,” I said, with sympathy.
The nightmares fed with a loud, champing
sound, snorting violet flame. They were of
all shapes, but the queer thing is that I
cannot remember one of them. Besides,
they changed each moment, sprouting fresh
teeth and claws and wings, as they ate and
moved.
There came a sudden stir amongst them.
“Thunder!” roared the basilisk. “If there
ain’t a paling down, and a seraphs’ tea-
party in the next paddock !”
The drove broke, and he after them, his
green beard waving, ns they poured in all
colored cascades across the boundary, and —
I dropped out of that land of dreams, and
woke.
HARRY SULLIVAN.
